ENHANCING THE ENVIRONMENTAL SUSTAINABILITY

Enhancing the environmental sustainability of start-ups and small businesses 
User-centred development of consultancy services
Danish title:

rådgivningsydelser

==PAGE:4==
iv
This master’s thesis documents the development of NIRAS sustain, which was 
conducted as a collaboration project between three MSc Design and Innovation 
students at DTU and the engineering consultancy NIRAS. The basis of the project 
comes from an opportunity identified by NIRAS. They experienced that many sustain-
ability-driven start-ups lack environmental assessments and eco-strategic consultancy, 
but NIRAS’ current offerings were not suitable for start-ups. The aim of the project 
was thus to develop a new sustainability consultancy service, targeted at sustainabili-
ty-driven start-ups and small businesses. 
To identify the characteristics and needs of sustainability-driven start-ups, and to 
investigate the identified gap between consultants and start-ups, a systematic litera-
ture review and a qualitative case study of potential users were conducted. From the 
consolidation of the literature review and an analysis of the case study, key factors 
could be determined as contributors to the identified gap, the most important being: 
The limited amount of resources within start-ups; complex and costly NIRAS offer-
ings; the high level of uncertainty in start-ups; and finally, that start-ups lack holistic 
sustainability perspective and knowledge. Consequently, the gap was caused by the fact 
that start-ups find it difficult see the benefits of sustainability consultancy services, 
and that consultancies cannot benefit economically from start-ups as customers.
Based on the obtained insights about start-ups and factors contributing to the gap 
between sustainability consultants and start-ups, design specifications for a potential 
solution were produced, ensuring a clear connection between user needs and the final 
concept. The concept development process continuously involved users and stake-
holders using co-creation, to ensure that the developed concept would be user-centred. 
The final concept, NIRAS sustain, consists of four predefined services shaped directly 
from the identified user needs, aimed at standardising and automating processes, so 
that NIRAS can offer desirable services to start-ups, which are still feasible and viable 
for NIRAS. To support the implementation, the services and new customer touch 
points had been thoroughly tested and detailed - the result is a deliverable, that NIRAS 
can take over and implement straight away. To further support the services, a toolbox 
was developed, utilising sustainability approaches like ecodesign, LCA and PSS, to ease 
the transfer of knowledge between consultants and start-ups employing method cards 
as boundary objects. 
With its user-centred foundation and educational value, NIRAS sustain provides the 
basis for enhancing the environmental sustainability of start-ups, while providing a 
viable business case for NIRAS.
Abstract | English
==PAGE:5==
v
Dette kandidatspeciale dokumenterer udviklingen af  konceptet NIRAS sustain, der 
blev til i et samarbejde mellem tre studerende på Design og Innovation, DTU, og 
den rådgivende ingeniørvirksomhed NIRAS. Grundlaget for projektet stammer fra 
en specifik mulighed, som NIRAS har observeret. De oplevede, at mange startups 
søgte rådgivning i miljømæssig bæredygtighed, men NIRAS’ nuværende ydelser stemte 
ikke overens med behovene hos de startups, der havde henvendt sig. Formålet med 
projektet var således at udvikle en ny service, der er rettet mod bæredygtigheds-orien-
terede startups og små virksomheder. 
For at kortlægge målgruppens karakteristika og behov, samt identificere de faktorer, 
der bidrog til uoverensstemmelsen mellem NIRAS’ konsulentydelser og behovene hos 
startups, blev der udført et systematisk, litterært studie og et empirisk, kvalitativt studie 
af seks case startups. 
De mest fremtrædende faktorer var startups’ begrænsede økonomiske ressourcer og 
generelt store usikkerhed; at NIRAS’ ydelser var for komplekse og dyre; samt at de 
undersøgte startups manglede et holistisk perspektiv på og viden om bæredygtighed. 
Konsekvensen deraf var, at startups har svært ved at se fordelene ved NIRAS’ konsu-
lentydelser. 
Med udgangspunkt i litteraturstudiet og det kvalitative studie, kunne der opstilles en 
række designspecifikationer til ”den gode løsning”, hvilket sikrede, at der var en klar 
sammenhæng mellem brugerbehov og det endelige koncept. Konceptudviklingen 
involverede løbende brugere og andre interessenter ved hjælp af co-creation, ligeledes 
for at sikre at det udviklede koncept ville være brugerorienteret.  
Det endelige koncept, NIRAS sustain, består af fire prædefinerede services udledt 
direkte fra brugerbehov. De fire services er tilpasset de forskellige forretningsudvik-
lingsfaser, en startup gennemgår. Formålet med konceptet var desuden at standardi-
sere og automatisere mange af de manuelle og mindre værdiskabende opgaver, der er 
forbundet med at levere en service, for således at kunne tilbyde en attraktiv service til 
startups, der samtidig var realistisk og økonomisk levedygtig for NIRAS. 
Resultatet var en leverance, som NIRAS kan overtage og implementere med det samme. 
For yderligere at understøtte servicen, blev der udviklet en værktøjskasse, baseret på 
bæredygtighedstilgange som ecodesign, LCA og PSS, for at lette vidensoverførslen 
mellem konsulent og startup. 
I kraft af den brugerorienterede tilgang til udviklingen, har NIRAS sustain poten-
tiale til at danne grundlag for en forbedring af startups’ miljømæssige bæredygtighed. 
Samtidig repræsenterer NIRAS sustain en interessant forretningsmulighed for NIRAS, 
både økonomisk men især i form af den positive værdi og erfaring, en startup-rettet 
rådgivningsydelse med bæredygtighed som omdrejningspunkt, vil give NIRAS.

This master’s thesis is the final delivery of Charlotte Amalie Schiøttz Hassing, Chris-
tian Peter Frigast Zinckernagel and Thomas Peter Sønderby on the Master of Science 
in Design and Innovation at the Technical University of Denmark. The project ran 
from January 2018 to June 2018. 
The authors would like to thank the many people who contributed to make this thesis 
a reality.
In particular, we want to thank all the case start-ups and the start-up experts from 
Skylab and Væksthusene,  who helped us by participating in interviews and workshops, 
and by contributing with their opinions, ideas and knowledge to help us understand 
the context of this thesis. 
We would like to thank Rune Jørgesen and Nynne Bech  from NIRAS for helping 
us with their insights and knowledge about NIRAS and their work practices, guiding 
our design decision. Furthermore, we  wish to express our gratitude to the seven 
consultants from NIRAS, who were willing to spend their time to help us understand 
NIRAS and issues and opportunities of sustainability. 
We would like to thank our supervisors Tim McAloone and Louise Laumann Kjær 
for their support, feedback and supervision, without which this thesis would not be a 
reality. 
Additionally, we would like to thank Daniella Pigosso and Niki Bey for their time to 
help evaluate our work. 
Finally, we would like to express our gratitude to our family and friends for their 
support and love throughout the project.

This master’s thesis is structured based on four work packages, each corresponding 
to a stage in the research and design process. Each work package is equipped with an 
introduction and a conclusion, to provide an overview for the reader.  The Chapters 1 
and 2 are preliminary to the work packages, which commence in Chapter 3. 
Sustainability specialists & consultants
We recommend that specialists and consultants within sustainability focus on the 
following chapters: 
 
+ Chapter 7: Identifying the gap
 
+ Chapter 11: Concept presentation
 
+ Chapter 13: Reflection
 
+ Chapter 14: Conclusion and further work
These chapters will provide insight into how sustainability specialists and consult-
ants can better accommodate the needs of start-ups and innovative, small businesses. 
Particularly Chapter 11, which presents a detailed concept proposal of how to address 
start-ups and innovative, small businesses.
Start-ups & innovative, small businesses
We recommend that start-ups and innovative, small businesses that are interested in 
sustainable practices focus on the following chapters:
 
+ Chapter 3’s section: The sustainability context
 
+ Chapter 11: Concept presentation 
By looking into these chapters, you will find an overview of methods within sustain-
able practices and inspiration on how to get started with them.  
Researchers & students
Researchers and students will benefit most from the following chapters:
 
+ Chapter 2: Methodology
 
+ Chapter 4: Research focus
 
+ Chapter 5: Literature review
 
+ Chapter 7: Identifying the gap
 
+ Chapter 10: Developing the toolbox
 
+ Chapter 13. Reflection
 
+ Chapter 14. Conclusion and further work
These chapters describe the methodological approach in the project, the research focus 
and conclusions, and present a collection of Ecodesign, LCA and PSS methods, which 
have been characterised according to their applicability. There are several opportuni-
ties for continuing this work, that other students or researchers may find interesting. 
Other stakeholders
Other stakeholders that find this thesis interesting, and wish to obtain an overview of 
the focus, outcome, limitations and implications of the project should consult:
 
+ Chapter 4: Research focus
 
+ Chapter 7: Identifying the gap
 
+ Chapter 11: Concept presentation
 
+ Chapter 12: Concept evaluation
 
+ Chapter 13. Reflection
 
+ Chapter 14. Conclusion and further work

Abbreviations
Term definition
Sustainability
“Sustainable development seeks to meet the needs and aspirations of 
the present without compromising the ability to meet those of the 
future” (Brundtland, 1987). Throughout this thesis, sustainability will 
refer to environmental sustainability.
Start-up
A start-up is a business in the early stages of product or service 
development, implementation and/or operation, often less than 5 
years since establishment. In this thesis, the term may also include 
initiatives that are not legally registered as businesses yet. 
Consultancy 
services
The collaboration partner of this master’s thesis is the engineering 
consultancy NIRAS. Whenever referring to consultancy, it will should 
be perceived as engineering consultancy.   
Ecodesign
Ecodesign is a product development approach ensuring considera-
tions of environmental impacts of the product/service during the 
entire product life cycle.
Innovation
Innovation is the realisation of a feasible, relevant offering such as a 
product, service, process or experience with a viable business model 
that is perceived as new.
Predefined service
Service package
These two terms have been used interchangeably to describe a set of 
service elements
Service element
Physical evidence of or offerings that constitute a service. In this 
thesis it will often refer to the specific offerings within a predefined 
service
Concept Solution 
Service
These three terms have been used interchangeably to refer to the 
outcome of the WP3 Concept Development
Toolbox
An element of the developed services: In this thesis it refers to a 
collection of ecodesign, PSS and LCA tools and methods

Introduction

==PAGE:18==
2
Intro  | Introduction
Chapter 1: Introduction
Sustainable development is an exponential topic in the global awareness. The Brundt-
land report introduced the term in 1987, and in 2015, United Nations published the 17 
Sustainable Development Goals shown in  Figure 1, which have placed the addressing 
of climate change and social issues on the agenda in  businesses, organisations and 
institutions. At the same time, and perhaps for that reason, the Danish industry is 
experiencing a growth in the number of start-ups with sustainability as a core driver 
in their value proposition. However, sustainable development is not always straight-
forward - it requires a holistic perspective on the entire ecosystem, which can be a 
challenging task for the inexperienced. The basis of this master’s thesis project thus 
comes from an opportunity identified by the engineering consultancy NIRAS. They 
have experienced that many of these sustainability-driven start-ups lack environmental 
assessments and eco-strategic consultancy, but NIRAS’ current offerings are not suit-
able for start-ups. The aim of this project is to explore, learn, design, test and evaluate 
a concept aligning NIRAS’ offerings to the start-ups’ needs, in order to increase the 
number of start-ups that receive sustainability consultancy in the decisive early phases 
of product development.
The definitions of a start-up are not always clear - it most often refers to very small, 
young businesses, trying to introduce a product or service that is new to the market. 
Even though large corporations are often perceived as the heavyweights, when it comes 
to climate change, small and medium sized enterprises account for a large portion of 
the environmental impact in Europe (Hillary, 2000), as seen in Figure 2. By designing 
sustainability consultancy services targeted at start-ups, it is possible to implement 
the right mindset for sustainability in the early stages of product development, where 
many of the decisions concerning the product’s environmental impact are made.  
1.1 
Design brief
The Danish consultancy NIRAS have experienced an increased demand from start-ups 
wanting to become more environmentally sustainable. However, their offerings do not 
currently align with the expectations of the start-ups. In light of this issue, NIRAS are 
Figure 1: UN’s 17 Sustainable Development Goals
==PAGE:19==
3
Intro  | Introduction
interested in developing offerings that can better fulfil the needs of the start-ups and 
innovative, small businesses. As a result, we - the thesis team - have two target users of 
the concept to be developed. The first target user is NIRAS, because if the solution 
is not well designed for NIRAS, it will never reach the second target users, which are 
start-ups and innovative, small businesses. To differ between the target users, NIRAS 
will sometimes be referred to as the ‘client’, and the start-ups and small businesses as 
the ‘customers’ or ‘users’.  
1.2 
Delimiting the scope
The following resources are available in this project: 
 
+ Three students working on the project corresponding to 30 ECTS points per 
student
 
+ Time: The project is limited to a duration of five months. 
This project is about sustainability, and therefore it is necessary to emphasise that 
sustainability per definition refers to three aspects: Economy, society and environment 
(Brundtland, 1987). During this project, sustainability will refer to environmental 
sustainability. The reason for this is that the client, NIRAS, currently focuses on envi-
ronmental footprints and life cycle assessments (LCA), and it is the authors’ ambition 
that a developed service is within the organisation’s scope, even though we would also 
like to challenge the current ways of working. In addition to the above delimitation 
of thesis’ scope, it is also necessary to stress that the included case companies and 
the start-ups, for which the results will be relevant, should be product-oriented to 
some degree, and not only provide a ‘pure service’ (Tukker, 2004). This is also due to 
NIRAS’ experience within the assessments of physical systems and products.

1.3 
Project contributors
The project has benefited from multiple contributors, described in Table 1, who have 
provided essential insights and data, which helped us to a deep understanding of

the first life cycle. The company has contributed to this project 
with insights and knowledge about the process of becoming a 
sustainable business, and what challenges they have faced. The 
insight was used as support when categorising the needs of the 
case start-ups.

Intro  | Methodology
This chapter presents the overall design research methodology, which has guided the 
structure of the research, and the approaches and methods that have been applied in 
the design process.
2.1 
Methodological framework
Before introducing the Design Research Methodology (DRM) proposed by Blessing 
& Chakrabarti (2009), it seems natural to first discuss the motivation for following 
that research approach. The thesis team has limited experience with the conduction 
of large design research projects, and thus guiding principles on how to proceed were 
needed. The thesis supervisors recommended using the design research methodology 
by Blessing & Chakrabarti, and therefore it was decided to proceed with that.
The DRM proposes that a research project should consist of four main stages: 
Research Clarification (RC), a Descriptive Study I (DS-I), a Prescriptive Study (PS) 
and a Descriptive Study II (DS-II). The aim of the Research Clarification stage is 
to find evidence or indications to support assumptions and formulate research ques-
tions. Furthermore, the ambition is to reach an understanding of the existing situation 
that is explored based on assumptions about the research project and to generate 
criteria to measure the outcome of the research. In the Descriptive Study I stage, 
the understanding of the existing situation is elaborated on, through literature reviews 
that should indicate factors that influence the situation. The stage is completed, when 
those influential are detailed to a degree, where the researchers can interpret them 
and generate the task clarification for the following design. The stage may include an 
empirical study, if the literature review is not sufficient. In the Prescriptive Study 
stage, the researchers generate a desired situation by using the insights from the DS-I 
stage. The idea is to indicate, which of the influential factors to address in the existing 
situation, in order to realise the desired situation by designing support for those 
factors. In the Descriptive Study II stage, the researcher analyses and investigates the 
potential impacts of the designed support. The stages should be executed in a linear 
process from RD → DS-I → PS → DS-II, and in a comprehensive manner. However, 
some research projects might not include all the stages or conduct each stage in the 
same level of detail. Within the Design Research Methodology, there are seven main 
types of research designs. For each of the stages described above, there are three 
levels of research. A review-based study only relies on a review of the literature. 
A comprehensive study relies on the literature review as well as an empirical study 
conducted by the research team. An initial study provides only the first steps in a 
stage, showing the pitfalls and results while enabling other researchers to continue the 
research.
The structure of a research type 5 will guide the research conducted in this project, 
i.e. “Development of  support based on a comprehensive study of  the existing situation” (Blessing 
& Chakrabarti, 2009, pp. 18):
RC → 
DS I → 
PS → 
DS II → 
Review-based
Comprehensive
Comprehensive
Initial
The reason for choosing this research type is that the purpose of the project is to 
develop a service targeted at start-ups in a user-centred manner, which requires a 
comprehensive understanding of the existing situation and the users. Therefore, this 
project should include both the establishment of understanding (a comprehensive 
DS-I) and, on that foundation, the development of support (a comprehensive PS). 
The final stage will be an initial DS-II - an initial evaluation of the research project, 
outlining the conclusions, and recommendations for future research. Figure 3 depicts 
the generic activities and outcome of each research stage in a type 5 research, and the 
reader will find that the sections of this report correspond to the research stages, and 
the chapters correspond to the research activities.

2.2 
Design paradigm: User-centred development
The purpose of this section is to answer the question: Which design paradigm are we 
working under? The reason for doing this is to explicitly state our views, assumptions 
and beliefs (Blessing & Chakrabarti, 2009), both to increase the quality of our own 
research, but also to give the reader an opportunity to relate to the choice of research 
focus and the steps undertaken - i.e. the research questions, the applied methods and 
the approaches. Formulated in our own words, it would be that we are always user-cen-
tred, considering the experiences, opinions and beliefs of the user to be of immense 
value, but at the same time, we also acknowledge that the user may not always be 
fully aware of his/her needs in a complex context. As such, the research should be 
grounded in empirical user studies conducted by the team and existing studies on the 
topic. The topic of interest is a rather specific and contemporary phenomena, which 
is why it was deemed necessary to conduct user studies ourselves, and not only rely on 
the research conducted by others. At the same time, it would not have been sufficient 
to only include our own user studies, because they are limited and conducted within a 
short period of time, and therefore it was also necessary to include the work of others. 
Combining the a literature review with an empirical study also helps to increase the 
quality of the research via data triangulation (Ravitch & Mittenfeller, 2015).
The DRM is recognised by researchers as beneficiary, when conducting in-depth 
research projects, but it provides limited support in the user-centred development of 
products and services. This led us to utilise a pragmatic combination of the product 
design and development approach by Ulrich & Eppinger (2015) and Stanford’s 
d.school design thinking (Plattner, 2010). The combination allowed the thesis team to 
use the d.school design thinking as an overall framework guiding the design process, 
while applying specific methods and tools from Ulrich & Eppinger in each process 
step. The design process is visualised in Figure 4, presenting each step, its applied 
methods and inputs to/outputs from it. The coloured icon of each step correlates to a 
stage in the DRM. Whenever a specific method or approach has been followed, it will 
be introduced and discussed in the beginning of the respective chapter.  
2.3 Structure of the report
The Design Research Methodology (DRM) was used to structure the research 
process, and the design thinking inspired the design process, especially in the phases 
concerning concept development, detailing and prototyping. Consequently, the report 
will document elements from both. To give the reader an overview of which chapters 
correspond to DRM activities, and where to read about the design process, Figure 5 
can be consulted.

2.4 Project activities
The project was planned according to DRM’s research type 5, which is illustrated 
in Figure 3. Appendix 1 shows an overview of the actual activities. The preliminary 
project plan and overview of meetings are in Appendix 2 and Appendix 3. 
The optimal project process would have had less overlap between the stages than 
this project did, to ensure that a stage is properly finalised before initiating the next 
stage. This is of course an idealised situation, and in practice, there will be unexpected 
areas to explore or challenges to overcome, which lead to deviations from the initial 
plan. However, the risk of not finalising a research stage properly, before initiating 
the next, is that decisions regarding the direction of the research will be made on 
a less enlightened foundation. In hindsight, it is assessed that the outcome has not 
been affected particularly, seeing that the conclusions from Work Package 2 Needs 
exploration were not surprising or contradictory.   
With the empirical study before the literature review, Work Package 2 Needs explora-
tion was conducted in reverse order, compared to the recommendations by Blessing 
& Chakrabarti. The reason is that the research team had decided early in the project 
that an empirical study should be conducted due to the contemporary nature of the 
investigated phenomena. Normally, it is not decided until after the literature review, 
where it can be determined if the foundation for the prescriptive study is adequate.      
2.5 
Conclusion
This chapter presented the overall design research methodology that has guided the 
structure of the research conducted for this thesis. It consists of four main stages, of 
which the first two revolves around obtaining a sound understanding of the context 
and nature of a problem. The third stage is about proposing a solution to the problem, 
and the fourth stage is about evaluating the proposed solution. For this thesis, a 
research approach with emphasis on the stages up to and including the third stage 
has been selected. The approaches and methods that have been applied in the design 
process have been presented, but will be elaborated in the respective chapters, in which 
they appear. Finally, the project process has been discussed.   
With the end of this chapter, the actual research and design process commences, 
which begins with the Work Package 1 (WP1): Research clarification.

WP1 Introduction
Work Package 1 Research Clarification contains an analysis of the context surrounding 
the problem definition. Sustainability as a concept is explored, and the approaches 
ecodesign, PSS and LCA are introduced as means to incorporating sustainability 
practices. The project stakeholders; case start-ups, NIRAS and other experts are 
introduced, and all experts as well as two of the case start-ups are interviewed. The 
methods applied in this work package are:
 
+ Semi-structured interviews
 
+ A basic market analysis
 
+ A mapping of the development arena
The outcome of the work package is a focus for the rest project, three research ques-
tions and formulation of research success factors. 
==PAGE:32==
16
 Work Package 1: Research Clarification | Context analysis
This chapter elaborates on the elements of the problem definition: Sustainability, 
start-ups and the consultancy NIRAS. The purpose was to obtain an understanding 
of the context surrounding the problem definition, and to identify some of the factors 
that influence it. Sustainability as a concept is explored, after which NIRAS’ structure 
and capabilities are investigated through qualitative interviewing. Finally, an initial 
understanding of start-ups and the start-up consultancy market is reached by meeting 
with case start-ups and experts and a basic market analysis.
Semi-structured interviews and basic market analysis
For the initial research, a qualitative, semi-structured interview guide approach 
was used (Patton, 2002) to interview the project’s stakeholders. A key focus was to 
prevent bias and to ensure comparability of answers between interviewees. To prevent 
recalling error in the analysis, the interviews were recorded and loosely transcribed, 
and the transcriptions were then used as the basis of an analysis. The sampled cases 
represent in great part the ‘client’, namely nine NIRAS employees and three specialists 
from Skylab, Really and think.dk. Because of the good connection to NIRAS Envi-
ronmental Footprint’s Rune Jørgensen and Nynne Bech, established by supervisor 
Louise Laumann Kjær, it was possible to arrange so many meetings with NIRAS 
specialists, and the thesis team saw it as an opportunity to absorb as much knowledge 
as possible, early in the project. The relatively high number of specialists supports a 
source triangulation (Ravitch et al., 2015), which help to ensure that the team obtained 
a wide range of viewpoints on environmental sustainability; start-ups and consultancy 
thereof; and NIRAS. This increases the quality and trustworthiness of the insights. 
The interview guide used for interviewing the NIRAS specialists can be found in the 
first part of Appendix 4, where the second part consists of transcriptions from the 
meeting and audio recording of the meeting with Henrik Kærgaard. 
To be able to create an overview of the context of the topic: Sustainability consul-
tancy of start-ups, a simplified version of a development arena was applied (Jørgensen 
& Sørensen, 2002). The arena modelling allowed us to create an overview of the 
development arena, its actors, objects, interactions and relations, hence giving rise to a 
definition of the system boundaries. 
3.1 
The sustainability context
Sustainability as a concept
What does sustainability mean? How is sustainability measured? Who decides what is 
sustainable? These are some of the questions which are inevitable to encounter, when 
discussing sustainability as a modern discipline. Sustainability does not only refer to 
recycling initiatives, but can be seen as more of a mindset. The definition of environ-
mental sustainability is the study of how biological systems can remain diverse and 
productive in a perfect balance (Kuhlman & Farrington 2010). In a way, the essence 
of sustainability is that it is as a worldwide initiative to protect the natural environment 
and the ecological and human health, while ensuring innovation and development 
of the species. In order to provide the basis of understanding the development of 
sustainability as a global phenomena, the main events of the sustainable development 
will be described and visualised. 
Evolution of corporate sustainable development 
During the last 30 years, world leaders, influencers, national organisations and 
governments have shaped sustainability as a core driver in their efforts of finding and 
developing a world enabling long-term growth. Figure 6 highlights the main corporate 
sustainability events in history, shaping the sustainability framework that most busi-
nesses work according to.
The timeline, inspired by the magazine Strategic Finance (Busco et al. 2017), under-
lines 6 major events from the Brundtland Report to UN’s Sustainable Development 
Goals, each with an important role in defining the sustainability concept. The following 
sections will shortly describe each event and their importance.
Chapter 3: Context analysis

 Work Package 1: Research Clarification | Context analysis
Brundtland commission, 1987
The Brundtland commission was established in 1983, formerly known as the World 
Commission on Environment and Development (WCED). It published the Brundt-
land Report in 1987, with the purpose of uniting countries to pursue a more sustain-
able development. The philosophy behind the report, from the chairwoman of the 
commission, Gro Harlem Brundtland, was as follows, “Development that meets the needs of  
the present without compromising the ability of  future generations to meet their own needs” (Brundt-
land commission, 1987). After releasing “Our Common Future” in 1987, also known 
as the Brundtland Report, the Brundtland Commission was officially dissolved, but 
the publication and the work of the commission laid the foundation for the 1992 
Earth Summit, hence the adoption of Agenda 21, the Rio declaration, as well as the 
establishment of the Commission on Sustainable Development.
The Global Reporting Initiative, 1997 
The Global Reporting Initiative (GRI) was founded in Boston, USA, by the US 
non-profit organisation, the Coalition for Environmentally Responsible Economies 
(CERES), the Tellus Institute and The United Nations Environment Programme 
(UNEP). The GRI project department was officially set up in 1997, as a result of a 
framework for environmental reporting introduced in the early 1990s by Dr. Robert 
Massie and Dr. Allan White (Brown et al., 2007). The aim of the project department 
was to create an accountable platform ensuring that companies affected by the CERES 
principles for responsible environmental conduct.
The World Summit in Johannesburg, 2002
Following the Earth Summit in Rio 1992, The World Summit on Sustainable Devel-
opment (WSSD) in Johannesburg 2002 had the purpose of defining tools to reduce 
poverty and to reach ecosystem security, while setting specific targets for the imple-
mentation of Agenda 21, from 1992 (Frantzius, 2004). This goal was not accomplished 
during the summit for multiple reasons, but mainly because of slow negotiations and 
communication issues related to the specification of targets. From a civil point of 
view, the WSSD was perceived as a missed opportunity to create new targets and 
timetables, but from a UN point of view, many new partnerships, both binding and 
non-binding, were created empowering sustainable development to become a broader 
framework.
Sustainable Accounting Standards Board, 2011
The Sustainability Accounting Standards Board (SASB), is a non-profit organisation 
established in 2011 to target, develop and implement sustainability accounting stand-
ards. With the rising importance of sustainability and environmental measures, the 
former FASB (Financial Accounting Standards Board) did no longer provide suffi-
cient standards including environmental aspects. The purpose of the standards are to 
describe both impacts and opportunities for improved innovation, and together “they 
characterise a company’s positioning with respect to sustainability issues and the potential for long 
term value creation” (Gilman et al. 2013). The SASB aimed at integrating their standards 
into the form 10-k, that public companies file in collaboration with the U.S. Securities 
and Exchange Commission. The SASB differs from GRI, because the standards are 
developed within the current financial regulations system (FASB). Today, many people 
link the SASB with Peter Drucker’s phrase, “What gets measured gets managed”, since 
their philosophy is to ensure transparent reporting and measurements.
The International Integrated Reporting Council, 2013
Integrated Reporting (IR) was developed to promote a cohesive and more efficient 
practice for corporate reporting and the structure encourage companies to improve 
the quality of data and information visible to investors of financial capital. The goal 
is to enable a more transparent and effective allocation of capital (IIRC, 2013). The 
==PAGE:35==
19
 Work Package 1: Research Clarification | Context analysis
International Integrated Reporting Council’s long term aim is to provide the foun-
dation for integrated thinking in mainstream business practices, both in private and 
public sectors. The IR platform should provide the basis for a corporate measure. 
These kinds of initiatives force companies to become more transparent and stream-
lined, which in terms of sustainable development has a huge potential. 
The Sustainable Development Goals, 2015
The Sustainable Development Goals was commissioned by the United Nations at the 
General Assembly on 25th of September 2015, as support to end poverty, protect 
the planet and ensure prosperity for all. There are 17 goals indicated by a colour and 
a SDG logo, all represented by the colour wheel, to graphically mark the awareness 
of the Sustainable Development Goals (United Nations, 2017). Each goal targets 
different aspects of achieving the above, and should be addressed and achieved by 
2030. The 17 SDGs and underlying 169 targets seek to further build on the “Mille-
nium Development Goals” and complete what these goals did not help to achieve. All 
the goals aim in collaboration at establishing a balance between the three dimensions 
of sustainable development: Economy, society and environment.
Environmental sustainability focus
From the delimited scope of this project and initial information from the client, 
NIRAS, it is defined that sustainability in this project refers to environmental sustaina-
bility. Therefore, the following sections will introduce Eco Labelling and International 
Standards, all focusing on improving and standardizing environmental performances 
and impacts.
EU further pushing for sustainability
The European Commission has become aware that the planet we live on has limits 
in terms of the needs the world currently has. The challenge of becoming more 
sustainable means more than just recycling materials and prolonging the durability of 
products. The European Commission have emphasised the importance of achieving 
peace among the nations, secure economic success across the world, achieve peace 
with nature and among people, all to ensure a path towards a more sustainable future 
(European Political Strategy Centres, 2016). Furthermore, the 2035 sustainability goals 
from the EU Commission state:
“Europe needs a narrative that shows the possibilities for reducing global 
emissions, fighting climate change locally, securing energy supplies, promoting 
wider socio-economic interests and increasing competitiveness – all at the same 
time.”
(Bontoux & Bengtsson, 2015).
Figure 7: Official Danish eco labels 
==PAGE:36==
20
 Work Package 1: Research Clarification | Context analysis
This push from EU for sustainability has - besides triggering new business to become 
more sustainable - also contributed to the fact that the ecolabels and standards have 
had a greater impact on the market. The following sections shortly introduces eco 
labelling and standards and their impact in Denmark.
Ecolabelling in Denmark
There are two official ecolabels in Denmark, The EU Ecolabel (also known as the 
flower) and the Nordic Ecolabel (also known as the swan). The Nordic Ecolabel apply 
to all the Nordic countries and was invented in 1989 by the Nordic Ministers. The EU 
Ecolabel is the official European environmental eco label and was invented in 1992 by 
the EU Commission (Miljømærkning Danmark, 2017). The purpose of the ecolabels, 
from a Danish perspective, is to minimise the total environmental impact of Danish 
consumers. The ecolabels assess the entire process and include all the environmental 
issues that occur during the life cycle, with the purpose of maintaining the natural 
resources while benefiting the people and nature on the planet. The following Figure 
7 shows the two ecolabels. The Nordic Ecolabel emphasises sustainable solutions 
based on life cycle assessments, while promoting circular economy by ensuring green 
procurement and resource efficiency. The Ecolabel consists of tough requirements 
towards environmental impacts in all aspects of the product/service life cycle, as 
well as constantly tightening the obligations to ensure development of The Nordic 
Ecolabel (Nordic Ecolabelling, 2017).
The EU Ecolabel is, much like The Nordic Ecolabel, a certificate that allows compa-
nies and suppliers to signal that both environmental performance and quality is high, 
and it can also be used to market the product/service amongst competing options. 
The difference between the two labels is that The Nordic Ecolabel is required to 
provide a green company profile while the EU Ecolabel is received to stand out from 
competitors, hence more quality based (Lange, 2014).
Receiving the two ecolabels provide a valuable effect, and over 15.000 products and 
services in Denmark have been granted one of the labels. The granted businesses 
provide anything from soap, detergent, paper, toys, construction products, electrical 
goods and furniture to services like cleaning companies, printing companies and hotels. 
From the perspective of private organisations and companies, the eco labels can be 
used for positioning and branding of products and services, while creating credibility 
and trustworthiness of the organisation and its vision and goals. The following Figure 
8 and Figure 9 indicate key numbers regarding the two ecolabels.
ISO environmental standards
ISO, International Organization for Standardization, is an independent, non-govern-
mental organization with a membership of 161 sub-standard partners. As an organi-
sation, ISO manages experts from around the world with the purpose of combining 
knowledge to set standards and specifications on how products, services and systems 
ensure quality, efficiency and safety. ISO has published more than 22000 international 
standards clearly indicating their importance on industries worldwide (We’re ISO: we 
develop and publish International Standards, 2017). Out of all the ISO standards, 
there are multiple standards within sustainability and one of the most acknowledged 
frameworks, aims at environmental management, known as the ISO 14001 standard. 
The standard has been implemented by more than 160 national members of the ISO 
standard society and its application is widely spreading amongst European countries. 
Around 154.572 certificates has been granted in 148 countries.
The ISO 14001 standard sets requirements for environmental management systems. 
The purpose of the standard is to enable companies to improve their environmental 
performance, by streamlining their use of resources, and reduce their amount of waste, 
hence gaining a competitive advantages as well as trustworthiness of surrounding 
actors. The main goal of this ISO standard is to manage, monitor and control environ-
mental aspect with a holistic approach (ISO 14001, 2015). Like all other ISO manage-
ment standards, this standard is subject to continuous improvements on the different 
aspect of environmental issue, like pollution degree, water and sewage, waste and soil

contamination and so forth. The ISO 14001 standard was updated in 2015, hence 
the new name ISO 14001:2015, and is now used by companies and organisations 
within all industries as a management tool, emphasising the role of environmental 
communication even more (ISO 14001:2015, 2015). The application of the standard 
also enables the companies to effectively manage their environmental efforts even 
more, thus achieving more visible results on the bottom line.
Sustainability practices and start-ups
With rising international legislations, goals, labels and standards on how the world can 
become more sustainable, companies and entrepreneurs have realised the importance 
of integrating sustainability as a core element in their business strategy. This trend 
is not only backed up by market potential but is supported by multiple new funding 
options available for start-ups and SMEs with a sustainable business. These new 
funding initiatives derive from the pressure coming from the European Commission, 
pushing for a sustainable future, that is allocate resources to new businesses wanting 
to sustain the world. These are initiatives like LIFE, CIP-EIP, ERDF, ESF, Horizon 
2020 and more (EU Commission, 2016).
There are several ways of incorporating sustainability practices into businesses. 
Within product-oriented start-ups, three approaches are essential: Ecodesign, PSS and 
LCA. These are briefly introduced in the following, before being elaborated on in 
Chapter 10.  
Ecodesign
Ecodesign is defined as: “The integration of environmental aspects into product 
development and design, with focus on reducing environmental impacts throughout 
the entire product’s life cycle” (Navajas et al., 2017). Ecodesign can be seen as a 
proactive mindset with the goal of minimising environmental impacts, but without 
compromising essential aspects of the product, such as performance, product func-
tionalities, and quality. Ecodesign must be implemented into strategic and operational 
management, in order to be fully integrated and become transparent in the product 
development process. Ecodesign requires knowledge and skills from many aspects of 
the development process, as knowledge about processes, logistics, materials, energy, 
durability and recyclability is likely to be the foundation for potential reduction of 
environmental impacts in the product design (McAloone & Pigosso, 2017).
Life cycle assessment (LCA)
LCA is an approach to assessing the environmental impacts and potential impacts 
connected of a product/service or process. LCA assesses all inputs and outputs from 
the entire product life cycle, like raw materials, manufacturing processes, packaging, 
transport, product use and disposal after use. A“cradle to grave” assessment includes 
everything from mining materials to product or material disposal. LCA quantifies all 
relevant emissions and resources related to environment, human health and resource 
depletion, in the effort to reduce environmental impacts and considering the impacts 
in a holistic manner. LCA can be used individually or in collaboration with develop-
ment processes, trying to make production and consumption more sustainable (ILCD 
Handbook, 2010).
Product/Service-Systems (PSS)
PSS as a development approach is an innovative strategy proposing businesses to 
pursue servitisation as a strategy and move away from product sales being the only 
indicator of success. Compared to Ecodesign and LCA, PSS is a more holistic and 
radical approach, focussing on establishing the optimal combination of product/
services needed to fully accommodate the customer needs, while incorporating service 
thinking into the product development process (Mougaard et al., 2013). PSS seeks 
to reduce the environmental impacts by designing a more efficient product/service 
within the ecosystem, reducing the consumption of other similar products, as well as 
lowering the amount of stakeholders included in the product/service offerings.

3.2 Stakeholders
3.2.1 
NIRAS
NIRAS Group A/S is an international engineering consultancy with 2.200 employees 
in 42 offices distributed over 21 countries. NIRAS currently have 108 worldwide 
projects working with everything from urban planning and third world aid, to energy 
supply,  environmental assessments, construction, infrastructure and much more 
(Kloch, K., 2016). 
Since NIRAS is the client of this project, it is essential to understand the organisa-
tion, their knowledge and competencies, in order for the service to be developed in 
this project to be implementable and effective in their organisation. To better under-
stand NIRAS, their organisational structure is mapped and multiple relevant in-house 
experts was interviewed, building a solid image of NIRAS’ structure and capabilities 
within sustainability consultancy, the results are shown in the section: Organisation. 
Finally, key insights from the interviews and a general analysis of the company are 
presented.
NIRAS expert interviews
To gain useful insight about internal competencies, tools, approaches and methods, 
nine consultants within NIRAS were interviewed, as shown in Table 2. The interviews 
were transcribed and can be seen in Appendix 5. The interviews were conducted as 
according to the semi-structured interview approach, allowing the experts to speak as 
freely as possible, but still enabling the authors to navigate the interview if needed. 
The duration of the interviews were about 1 hour each, and they were all recorded. 
The insights obtained from the interviews are discussed at the end of the chapter.

Organisation
Using the organizational structure presented by Eppinger and Ulrich (2015,  pp. 
27-29), NIRAS’ organisational structure was analysed. Generally, NIRAS is organised 
as a Lightweight Project Matrix Organisation as shown in Figure 10, with a functional 
manager having the greater responsibility. However, certain teams will to a larger 
degree work according to the Heavyweight Project Matrix Organisation as shown in 
Figure 10, with the project managers being the main responsible.
Through the interviews, it became clear that NIRAS has developed an environ-
ment where cross-disciplinary projects are easy to set up so that internal competition 
between departments are kept to a minimum. Niels Bahnsen stated it this way:
“We celebrate the victories together, and cry over the losses together”
In essence, NIRAS is skilled in using the employees competencies across the depart-
ments, when it is necessary. This is also emphasised by the internal innovation fund in 
NIRAS. If a group of employees across departments see a new business opportunity, 
they can apply for this grant to pursue the business opportunity. 
The key finding for this project is that the service that will be developed in this project 
may require competencies from several departments. Since NIRAS has a so-called 
“flat” business structure with a large degree of knowledge transfer between the busi-
ness units, it can be assumed that a cross-disciplinary solution is feasible. The flat 
business structure is visualised in Figure 8.

introduces the case companies, and how they have contributed to the process. The 
case start-ups were chosen, because they all have sustainability as a core driver in their 
business model and are product-oriented. Each of the start-ups have different ways 
of trying to achieve a sustainable business and target different markets, but this will be 
elaborated in Chapter 6. 
In this research clarification stage, two of the case start-ups - Amino Wool and Hacktus 
- participated in shorts interview so we could obtain an initial understanding of their 
work with environmental sustainability and their needs in this regard. The results are 
presented at the end of this chapter. 
Amino Wool works with recycling, and uses waste from rapeseed oil to 
create a substitute for wool fibers, essentially trying to create a competing 
business to wool production, hence a B2B. The nature of their contribu-
tions is insights about challenges and opportunities, e.g. data that later were 
translated into needs, constraints and requirements. They also participated in 
interviews, user testing and validation of the final solution.
FiveMoreMinutes has developed bedding produced by flax instead of cotton, 
which has a lower environmental impact. B2C. Besides that, the texture of 
the linen are softer. The nature of their contributions is knowledge about 
the challenges of sustainability practices, which later was used to generate 
user needs. They also participated in interviews, user testing and validation 
of the final solution.
Dropbucket is a company producing cardboard bins that can be disposed 
with the trash. The main market is currently large events and festivals, B2C 
and B2B. DropBucket’s sustainability focus is on the material used: Reused 
cardboard, and the fact that the cardboard allows for folding the bin to 
ensure more efficient transportation.
AA-water is a technology supplier with competencies within research and 
development of water supply and drinking water, hence B2B. AA-water has 
developed a method to soften the drinking water by using air instead of 
chemicals. The nature of their contributions is knowledge about the chal-
lenges, they have faced when trying to build a sustainable business model, 
which later was used to generate needs and requirements. They also partici-
pated in interviews, user testing and validation of the final solution.
CRR Denmark is a Danish start-up that delivers high performance and cost 
effective exhaust gas treatment filter technology that will aid in the reduction 
of exhaust gases from heavy fuel burning engines from ships as well as city 
machinery, i.e. B2B. By reducing the extraction of gases into the sphere, 
CRR Denmark attempts enhance the air environment on the oceans and 
in cities. CRR Denmark has participated in the empirical study, helping to 
generate needs used for the development of the service.
Hacktus is a Danish start-up that enables sustainability through develop-
ment of new products and services. The company originally developed 
IRIS, which is a redesign of to-go coffee lids design with recycled materials, 
hence B2C. The idea was later abandoned, and now the company works on 
another concept. The company has contributed to this master thesis as a 
case start-up and provided knowledge on challenges and needs of start-ups.

3.3 Market analysis 
The initial interviews, meetings and observations were all part of understanding 
the development arena that the service to-be-developed will affect. In order to get 
an understanding of the market, NIRAS will enter, when developing a service for 
start-ups, it was necessary to conduct a basic market analysis. Specifically, a SWOT 
and Porter’s 5 Forces (Porter, 1979) were chosen as methods. SWOT is a useful tool 
to analyse the internal factors in NIRAS and the relations to the market. Porter’s 5 
Forces, on the other hand, is good for analysing external pressure in the market and 
mapping the competitive situation. 
3.3.1 SWOT analysis of NIRAS 
Based on the acquired preliminary knowledge from the interviews, observations and 
research, a SWOT analysis  was conducted  with the goal to understand the market 
situation by indicating the potential strengths, weaknesses, opportunities and threats 
NIRAS could have if the chose to develop  a service aimed at start-ups . The SWOT 
analysis was used to generate a concrete picture of where and how competitors can 
challenge NIRAS. Figure 13s hows the results of the SWOT analysis followed by a 
discussion of the results. 
Within Strengths it is shown that NIRAS has a vast knowledge and expertise that spans 
a wide degree of fields - through the interviews it also became apparent that NIRAS 
is skilled in developing and conduct cross-disciplinary projects. Since NIRAS is an 
established company with a history another strength is that they have an established 
brand which is trusted by their customers. They are somewhat agile to changes in the 
sense that cooperation across departments is easy and supported. Another strength, 
in relation to sustainability, is that NIRAS is leading in doing corporate environmental 
footprint assessments.

Figure 13:  SWOT analysis of NIRAS
==PAGE:47==

 Work Package 1: Research Clarification | Context analysis
The weaknesses for NIRAS in the given setting, is that most start-ups are not 
currently able to pay for their services. Equally NIRAS has a lower limit for what can 
be considered minimum price before a case can be considered to pay off. Another 
weakness is that NIRAS has little experience in work start-ups and thus are not expe-
rienced in what the needs are for them.A final weakness is that NIRAS is to some 
degree risk averse, since they are an established company. The risk is that NIRAS will 
be become too comfortable and risk averse and miss out on potential growing markets. 
The opportunities for NIRAS is to expand their market with the sustainability sector 
and gain more start-ups and SMEs as customers. In general the interest and focus 
on sustainability is a growing “trend” which also means that especially start-ups have 
figured out that businesses can be built around it. At the same time governmental and 
european regulations are evolving and becoming stricter. These factors mean that the 
market for sustainability counseling is likely going to increase. 
In the interviews with NIRAS several of the interviewees mentioned that it would 
be difficult to find make a business model for NIRAS with start-ups as customers. 
Generally seen the problem is that the start-ups in most cases have limited resources 
and that the rate for consulting at NIRAS is rather high. Further work will need to 
investigate this issue. 
In the interviews another potential threat was broad up. Some competitors will 
market themselves as providing sustainable consultancy. The critique was that some 
competitors are offering “Greenwashing” consultancy, because they do not take into 
account the product system and life cycle. If the start-up does not already have a 
thorough knowledge of sustainability this will be very difficult to see through. 
A final threat is linked to the general understanding start-ups and people have of 
sustainability. An example could be that some have the impression that if a product is 
made of recycled materials it is by definition sustainable. However, this doesn’t take 
into account the energy consumption required to reuse, what the material is replacing 
etc. Myth or misunderstandings about sustainability like this can potentially be a barrier 
for NIRAS to acquire customers. The question is if a start-ups has this kind of myth 
based understanding of sustainability and are content about it why would they go to 
NIRAS for help?
3.3.2 Porter’s Five Forces 
In order to further explore market further and gain insight into potential competitors 
it was chosen to conduct a Porter’s Five Forces analysis (Porter, 1979). Porter’s 5 
forces model was chosen because it offers a systematic approach to map and identify 
potential competitors or “threats” in the market. 
Since no actual service aimed at start-ups directly is currently available the analysis was 
done on the assumption that such a service did exist. 
Since the offering is a service the supplier power was estimated not relevant and thus 
left out. The results of Porter’s Five Forces can be seen in Figure 14.
Threat of substitution: Low
Incubator hubs
In general there are many possibilities of substituting a sustainability guidance offering. 
The first examples are incubator hubs like: Væksthus and Startvækst. Some of these 
hubs are able to give simplified guidance that may potentially substitute NIRAS’ 
offering. 
Online sustainability tools and research tools
Other alternatives are online tools like Cirkvirk, EREK, these tools are free tools to 
help companies improve their environmental performance. Likewise research or tools 
from universities about sustainability could be potential substitutes. With these tools 
from universities or online the companies could in theory “do it themselves” to a 
degree that could be considered a substitute.

Doing nothing
Other alternative substitutes could simply be that start-ups decide to do nothing in 
terms of getting help with sustainability. In reality this is likely the most common path 
for most start-ups. The reason are difficult to pinpoint exactly but some of the likely 
causes could be lack of knowledge, lack of resources or a gap between sustainability 
counselors and start-ups. 
Greenwashing
A final substitute threat could be that a start-up chose a “greenwashing” solution. 
Greenwashing is in this cased defined as a situation where the product/service is 
marketed to appear environmentally sustainable without having concrete data that this 
is indeed the case. This could be both a intentional or unintentional decisions. If the 
decision is unintentional the cause can be assumed to be similar to doing nothing, that 
there is a lack of knowledge about sustainability. If the decision is on the other side 
intentional it is likely to assume that the motivation for working with sustainability is 
mainly to gain profit. 
Threat assessment
The threat of substitution from incubator hubs is estimated to not be very high, since 
the main focus is not specialized on environmentally sustainability minded start-ups. 
The threat from online sustainability tools and research is also estimated to be fairly 
low. Even though many of these tools are free and developed specifically to help 
companies, we argue that the start-ups often don’t use them. In the interviews with 
the case companies some of the reasons was identified: Lack of resources and lack 
of knowledge. As a start-up the resources are often limited both in terms of time and 
money, thus the start-up will often be very focused on their core business. This means 
that spending time on researching relevant sustainability tools or learning how to use 
may not be a high priority. Likewise they may not know where to look. If they do use 
a tools there is also a problem whether they can trust the results they produce with 
the tools. 
The threat from doing nothing and greenwashing is on the other hand estimated to 
be rather high since many start-ups will likely choose to “do nothing” and not contact 
an external sustainability consultancy company like NIRAS. Many start-ups may also 
chose to “greenwash”.
Threat from new entrants: Low
The threat from new entrants in the market is in general estimated to be fairly low. The 
reason being that for a new entrant to enter the market a very broad and in depth set 
of skills and knowledge is required. A new entrant could potentially 
To compete with NIRAS it would require a large staff with different specializations 
within sustainability. Something that would likely be very difficult for a new entrant 
to achieve. Also a new entrant will not have built a reputation in a way that NIRAS 
has. The only likely threat from new entrant would be if an entrant would compete on 
parts of the offerings NIRAS is offerings. For example only offer consultancy aimed 
at start-up without offering actual assessments. It is more likely that a new entrant 
could offer a solution in this range potentially threatening NIRAS. 
Competitive rivalry threat: Medium
A series of direct rivals to NIRAS offering within sustainability exist on the Danish 
market. The identified competitors are shortly described in Table 5.
The main competitors similar to NIRAS is identified to be Rambøll, COWI and 
Force Technology. Their offerings are fairly similar with a broad range of services 
offered. A specific offering aimed towards precisely towards start-ups or similar was 
not found on any of the competitors websites and it thus assumed that like NIRAS 
the main focus in these companies is more towards larger customers. The threat from 
rivalry from this group of competitors is thus estimated to be fairly low. 
Other smaller competitors but with a more focused around sustainability are DGE, 
DMR and Viegand&Maagøe. With a more narrow focus on sustainability in its different 
aspects makes them potentially larger threats. It is however estimated that the focus of 
==PAGE:50==
34
 Work Package 1: Research Clarification | Context analysis
DMR and DGE is mostly on traditional sustainability fields related to regulations like 
buildings, pollution of ground, risk assessment etc. Areas of sustainability that in most 
cases will not be very relevant for start-ups. The closest competitor that might have 
an offering that could be relevant for start-ups is Viegand&Maagøe who for example 
has offerings like development of circular businesses, ecodesign and communication 
of sustainability - thus resulting in a higher threat score. Overall the threat from rivals 
is estimated to be medium since there is many companies with similar competencies 
to NIRAS however many of them does not have offerings directly customized to 
start-ups.

 Work Package 1: Research Clarification | Context analysis
Threat of buyer power: High
The threat from the buyer power is split into two parts. The existing customers in the 
market NIRAS is currently at the moment most involved in and the new potential 
customers of the solution in development. The reason for looking at the existing 
customers and their power is estimate the potential and risk of developing this new 
offerings compared to the already established customer segment.  Looking at the 
existing customers they are characterised by often being large companies or govern-
mental agencies like municipalities. The task they need help with are often large 
projects with large budgets. Often they are not very price sensitive. The projects they 
need solved are often of a size and complexity that only a few competitors would be 
able to solve them besides NIRAS, and the substitution power is thus not very high for 
the customers. Since the offering can be considered a service, the cost of changing for 
the customer is on the opposite side not very high. Overall this means that the buyer 
power for the existing customers is low to medium. 
Looking at the buyer power of the new target customers, the start-ups the situa-
tion is very different. Since start-ups are small businesses most of them will be very 
price sensitive - and will in general only consider searching for help with sustainability 
counseling if they see that the benefit will lead directly to an improvement in their 
core business - for example that it will increase the likelihood of getting funding or 
increased sales. As with the existing customers the cost of changing is very low to 
non-existing. As mentioned in the threat from substitutes section many substitute 
offerings exist that could potentially compete with NIRAS offering - but that are most 
likely not a very large threat. However, in general the threat from the buyer power for 
start-ups is estimated to be high due to the above mentioned factors. 
Conclusion
From the SWOT analysis it became clear that one of NIRAS’ main strengths is that 
they have a vast amount of expertise with environmental sustainability and that with 
the growing focus on the subject there could be an opportunity to gain new customers 
by focusing on start-ups. One of NIRAS main weaknesses is that their offerings are 
currently very considered to be too expensive in the start-up community and that 
they don’t have a lot of experience in delivering services to this customer group. This 
makes it challenging for NIRAS to actually profit from this customer group. At the 
same time it was shown that there is a problem in the field with companies “green-
washing” their products.
From the Porter’s Five Forces model it was shown that there is a relatively low threat 
of substitute services since the alternatives like start-ups hubs are either competent 
within sustainability or that the alternative does not offer expert guidance, like in the 
case with online tools. A potential substitute that does have some degree of threat is 
the case of greenwashing of products or that start-ups do not address environmental 
issues at all. 
It was found that it was unlikely that new entrants would be a large threat as it 
would require establishing a trusted brand. The rivalry from competitors was esti-
mated to be medium since several competitors exist. Most of these competitors focus 
on larger customers at the moment, but they could potentially develop services aimed 
at start-ups. Finally it was shown that the buyers has a high degree of power in this 
market - mainly because focusing on environmental sustainability is not yet (in all 
cases) a must have for start-ups. At the moment start-ups can still chose to some 
degree to  ignore this matter. However, the subject of environmental sustainability 
has been shown to only be an increasingly “popular” topic, and it may be the case that 
more and more start-ups will adapt environmental sustainability as a key focus in their 
business. 
In general the Market analysis showed that their is a potential for NIRAS to develop 
a service directed towards start-ups although several challenges must be addressed to 
ensure a successful implementation.

Universities
Contributes to the development arena by enabling a research platform, 
publishing research and methods. Also providing the link to the thesis team. 
Universities help to create the framework for legislation and regulation 
through their research and knowledge sharing in the development arena.
Existing solutions within sustainability counsel for start-ups
These are other tools and approaches, developed with the purpose of guiding 
companies to become more sustainable. Many of these tools are developed by EU 
funded organisations, trying to promote the Sustainable Development Goals. Most 
of the tools and approaches are developed for the companies to use themselves 
and initial research indicated that there is a breakdown between these services and 
the start-ups because the tools require time and knowledge and does not provide 
the credibility that the start-ups seek.
Think tanks, incubators and NGO’s
These organisations are available for Danish businesses and contribute 
to setting the agenda for given problem definitions and areas of research 
and development. Incubators guide start-ups and SMEs in developing solid 
business models that seek to solve relevant problems.
Suppliers and partners
Suppliers and sub-suppliers that contribute to the different business types 
by supplying parts, knowledge and se forth. They are included in the devel-
opment arena, because they are a part of the life cycle of a product or service, 
hence they should be included in a potential assessment of the companies and 
provide either data or knowledge. 
Miljøstyrelsen
The Environmental Protection Agency maintain legislation on environmental 
aspect and provide information and tools to guide private and public 
organisations and companies to make the right decisions and develop green 
businesses.
NIRAS competition
Other consultancy companies or organisations offering services within 
sustainable development and LCA. They contribute to the development arena 
because they also target the Sustainable Development Goals
UN
The organisation United Nations have generated the 17 Sustainable Develop-
ment Goals, that combined are trying to ensure a sustainable future for the 
world. The Goals have a huge influence on sustainability has become a major 
part of how companies build their business model and have created a new 
foundation for sustainable development.
Established large business
Companies with more than 200 employees, which already are client with 
NIRAS. They seek consulting in environmental aspect, especially when 
European legislations and regulations force them to react. NIRAS perform 
large scale LCA project with them.
EU-commission
The European Union commission have an important role in this development 
arena, since they have the responsibility to implement the UN Sustainable 
Development Goals in each of the European countries. They also push for new 
legislations and regulations on how to achieve the goals.
Start-ups
The case-start-ups included in this project. There is a breakdown between the 
start-ups and NIRAS as well as a breakdown between start-ups and existing 
solutions trying to bridge the gap, since NIRAS currently does not have any 
services build for start-ups and that the existing solutions does not solve the 
essential needs from the start-ups.
Certificates and standards
In order to maintain the development towards a more sustainable future, 
certificates and standards are important because they, step by step, target 
specific issues to solve. These are Ecolabels and ISO standards, which all are 
backed by EU.
Small and Medium-sized Enterprises
Companies with less than 200 employees. Most of NIRAS’ clients in this 
category are municipalities and local companies working with symbiosis
Funding
From the research it was acknowledged that start-ups find it very difficult to 
apply for funding because it require time and experience that the start-ups 
does not have. 
NIRAS
The collaboration company and therefore the client in this master thesis 
project. The solution should be implementable with there organisation and 
should enable the foundation for new start-up clients.

Initial identification of the “gap” between NIRAS

3.6 Conclusion
In Chapter 3, the historic context  and  development of environmental sustainability 
was  introduced. The purpose was to provide an overview of how  environmental 
sustainability has evolved as a concept and research field, enabling us to better under-
stand its impact today. Over the course of 30 years, sustainability has evolved from 
being a little known term to being a central and crucial topic shaping today’s societies. 
A market analysis was performed to determine the competitive situation, NIRAS will 
be in by offering sustainability consultancy to start-ups covered. The analysis showed a 
gap in the market, and that NIRAS has the competencies required to deliver offerings 
to start-ups, but that it would require development of new services. 
Finally, Chapter 3 covered a series of initial stakeholder interviews conducted as a 
part of the research clarification stage. From these interviews, it was possible to iden-
tify some preliminary factors that keep start-ups from seeking help from sustainability 
consultancies like NIRAS. The results are shown in Figure 17.
==PAGE:55==
Lack of resources
The start-ups in general lack resources to fund the initial assess-
ment from NIRAS. Besides this, the start-ups think that experts 
like NIRAS are too expensive to even consider during early product 
development.
Not aligned
NIRAS’ offerings are not aligned with the start-ups needs, and 
NIRAS do not now how to become relevant for the start-ups or how 
to get in contact with them. The start-ups do not think that NIRAS 
can offer any services relevant in early product development.
DATA
Lack of knowledge and data
The start-ups lack internal knowledge and data about production, 
environmental impacts, value chain data, origin of materials and 
much more. This means that NIRAS has to provide generic data, 
which is time consuming and inaccurate. 
Are there any visible benefits?
The start-ups can not see benefit from NIRAS’ offerings, compared 
to the cost. They need value-for-money, and they often focus more 
on short-term aspects like increasing the amount of customers and 
raising capital.
Start-ups as customers
Start-ups are difficult customers to get a hold of for NIRAS. One of 
the main barriers is the limited resources they have. The question 
is consequently, if there is viable business case in offering consul-
tancy to start-ups trying to achieve a sustainable business.
Internal competencies and services 
The typical customer within sustainability consultancy is primarily 
larger corporations and governmental institutions. This type of 
customer has very different needs than start-ups. This means that 
NIRAS is not experienced in delivering offerings that fit the needs of 
start-ups. It is also likely that NIRAS will need to develop or achieve 
new skills in order to successfully satisfy the needs of start-ups.

4.1 Determining areas of relevance and contribution
The topic of interest (Blessing & Chakrabarti, 2009) is to enhance the environmental 
sustainability of product-oriented start-ups and innovative, small businesses through 
user-centred development of consultancy services on behalf of NIRAS. Investigating 
the topic of interest requires that areas and disciplines that may be relevant to include 
in the project, are considered. It is also important to look into, what has been done 
before, to ensure novelty. This approach is common, when developing products and 
services, since successful innovations often originate from different technologies and 
practices that in combination create new value. To create an overview, the areas rele-
vant to this thesis are mapped in Figure 18, while assessing their degree of relevance. 
In addition, the areas, where the thesis will hopefully be able to contribute, are empha-
sised with a black outline. The latter will be elaborated in Chapter 14.
The diagram shows that to answer the problem definition, it has been necessary to 
obtain a comprehensive understanding of the targeted users (start-ups and entre-
preneurs) and their drivers and barriers towards sustainability practices. The aim 
was to design consultancy services employing approaches like ecodesign, PSS and 
LCA by categorising these according to start-ups’ business characteristics. The 
emphasis of the service design has been on the specification processes and the 
use of boundary objects to enhance the transfer of  knowledge from specialists to 
start-ups.

4.2 Research questions
To position the thesis in an academic context and to perform a review of existing 
literature, research questions were formulated, seen in Figure 19. The scope of the 
research is wide, and therefore it was necessary to decompose the research questions 
into three separate questions (Biolchini et al., 2005).
The main research question, RQ3, will be investigated throughout the entire process 
and answered at the completion of the project. RQ1 and RQ2 are sub-questions that 
will be answered during the second stage of the project: Needs exploration. Linking 
the research questions to the design process, one might say that answering RQ1 will 
determine the content of the service to-be-developed (the what); answering RQ2 
will contribute to the shape or format of the service to-be-developed (the how); 
and answering RQ3 will contribute to actually delivering the content of the service 
to-be-developed. This is also illustrated with colours in Figure 19.
4.2.1 The nature of the research questions
The research questions build on underlying hypotheses that have not been explicitly 
stated. The underlying hypothesis of the entire thesis is that an engineering consultancy 
can support start-ups and small businesses in attaining an environmentally sustainable 
business model, and the aim of the thesis is to verify (or falsify) that hypothesis by 
proposing a viable solution (or by finding that no viable solution exists), based on 
existing and own conducted research. 
Besides the main hypothesis, there are several other underlying hypotheses: 1) That 
a gap exists between sustainability-driven start-ups and sustainability specialists and 2) 
that approaches within PSS, ecodesign and LCA can help bridge that gap. The first 
hypothesis has been sufficiently verified in this first stage, Research Clarification, but 
the second cannot be verified until a concept has been developed to a degree, where 
it can be tested.

 Work Package 1: Research Clarification | Research focus
RQ1 is of a descriptive nature, whereas RQ2 and RQ3 are of a more exploratory 
and relational nature, investigating the relationship between variables (Blessing & 
Chakrabarti, 2009). RQ1 could be answered through a quantitative study, e.g. a survey, 
distributed in start-up communities to obtain characteristics about and needs from 
start-ups in a systematic way. RQ2 could also be answered through a quantitative study 
based on a set of identified factors or factors presumably contributing to the gap, 
and a following factor analysis to determine, which factors are the most influential. 
However, RQ2 could also be answered in a more open manner, through a qualita-
tive study, where new factors or views might arise. Finally, RQ3 would require an 
exploratory approach, and a qualitative study would hence be appropriate. The chosen 
approaches will be elaborated in Chapter 6, when the empirical study is presented.      
In RQ2, the independent variable is ‘the factors’ and the dependent variable is 
‘the gap between sustainability-driven start-ups & small businesses and sustainability 
consultants’. Seeing that the independent variable is not specific, control variables 
are redundant. In RQ3, the independent variable is ‘approaches within ecodesign, 
PSS and LCA’ and the dependent variable is ‘bridging the gap between sustainabili-
ty-driven start-ups & small businesses and sustainability consultants’. Here, there are 
several control variables to consider, e.g. external factors such as industry trends or 
the context of the involved stakeholders, and this should be taken into consideration 
when answering RQ3.  
4.3 Research success criteria

start-ups and small businesses, as shown in Table 6. However, this is a goal, which is 
unlikely to be reached within the scope of the project, and there will be several other 
factors than the outcome of this project that could affect their environmental sustain-
ability, especially over a period of time. Instead, some more concrete research success 
criteria were generated after the completion of the second stage of the project: Needs 
exploration. Meeting all the criteria should ultimately lead to the goal of enhancing the 
environmental sustainability of start-ups and small businesses.
