 Work Package 1: Research Clarification | Research focus
WP1 Conclusion
Work Package 1: Research Clarification contained an analysis of the context 
surrounding the problem definition: that NIRAS’ Environmental Footprint have 
experienced a misalignment between their current services and the expectations 
of sustainability-driven start-ups. They wish to align their services so that they can 
better accommodate the start-ups. Sustainability as a concept was explored, and the 
approaches ecodesign, PSS and LCA were introduced as means to incorporating 
sustainability practices. The project stakeholders; case start-ups, NIRAS and other 
experts are introduced, and all experts as well as two of the case start-ups were inter-
viewed. The insights obtained from the interviews in combination with a basic market 
analysis led to the establishment of a development arena, and an identification of 
preliminary factors that seem to contribute to the gap between NIRAS and start-ups, 
namely: A lack of resources, knowledge and (product) data within start-ups; that the 
content of NIRAS’ services is not aligned with the needs of start-ups; that start-ups 
find it hard to see the benefits of paid sustainability consultancy; that start-ups are not 
profitable customers for NIRAS; and that NIRAS’ internal competencies and services 
are adapted for larger customers. Finally, this led to the focus of the research and the 
formulation of three research questions:
 
+ What are the characteristics and needs of product-oriented start-ups and small 
businesses wanting to become more sustainable?
 
+ What are the factors contributing to the gap between sustainability-driven 
start-ups & small businesses and sustainability consultants?
 
+ How can approaches within ecodesign, PSS and LCA contribute to bridging the 
gap between sustainability-driven start-ups & small businesses and sustainability 
consultants?
The research questions are used to guide the research in the Work Package 2: Needs 
exploration.

 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Research focus
WP2 Introduction
In WP1, the team clarified the focus of the project by performing an initial market 
analysis, investigating the development arena and its actors and objects, and by formu-
lating three research questions. The aim of the thesis has emerged from an opportu-
nity identified by NIRAS: To enhance the environmental sustainability of start-ups 
and innovative, small businesses through a user-centred development of consultancy 
services. In Work Package 2 Needs Exploration, an essential part of a user-centred 
development process is to understand the targeted users: Start-ups and small busi-
nesses. This process is twofold:
 
+ A systematic literature review based on the research questions 1 and 2
 
+ An empirical study with a direct involvement of selected case start-ups, that are 
potential users of the service to be developed, applying methods from co-crea-
tion and grounded theory
Thus, Chapter 5 presents and discusses two literature reviews based on the research 
questions, to build a solid understanding of the situation and the targeted users, and to 
provide a direction of the empirical study of the case start-ups.   
In Chapter 6, the conduction of the case study involving the case companies is 
presented. Furthermore, the development and conduction of co-creation sessions are 
elaborated on, as well as the analysis process, where principles from grounded theory 
method were applied to produce a list of needs and general insights about the targeted 
users. 
Finally, in Chapter 7, the insights obtained from both the literature reviews and the 
case study are consolidated and used to further specify the aim of the research project.
==PAGE:62==
46
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Literature review
The aim of this chapter is to provide a preliminary answer to the first research ques-
tions: 
 
+ What are the characteristics and needs of product-oriented start-ups and small 
businesses wanting to become more sustainable?
 
+ What are the factors contributing to the gap between sustainability-driven 
start-ups & small businesses and sustainability consultants?
Systematic literature review
The purpose of the literature reviews is to provide a solid foundation of data, context 
and history about the topic of interest, in combination with the case study conducted 
in the research project. A systematic review template as proposed by Biolchini et 
al. (2005) has been applied to ensure that as many relevant studies as possible were 
included in the review, however the structure of the systematic review process and the 
systematic review article, i.e. the literature review article, will not follow Biolchini et al.’s 
approach. The approach is presented and discussed in the following section. 
The aim of this literature review is to obtain knowledge about RQ1 and RQ2, seeing 
that RQ3 will be investigated later. It is assumed that many studies on the needs and 
behaviours of start-ups and small businesses have already been conducted, and those 
will be used to generate a list of needs and insights used to support or oppose the 
needs and insights acquired through the research team’s own case studies. 
The approach to conducting the literature search is inspired by the systematic review, 
which follows a “...well defined and strict sequence of  methodological steps, according to an aprio-
ristically developed protocol” (Biolchini et al.). The purpose of a systematic review is to be 
able to create generalisations about the topic of interest based on existing studies, to 
reduce bias, and to allow others to conduct the same review to compare the outcome. 
However, it cannot be claimed that this review follows the systematic review approach 
completely; this would require an unnecessary amount of meticulousness, considering 
that the research questions are about a contemporary, qualitative phenomena, and 
not eg. statistics or results from medical studies. That would most likely be the scope 
of an entire research project! The point of these literature reviews was to obtain the 
most common insights about start-ups’ and small businesses’ characteristics and needs 
regarding sustainability practices, and further to explore the gap that seems to exist 
between sustainability consultancies on the one side and start-ups and small businesses 
on the other.
The literature search is only conducted in English, based on the assumption that little 
academic literature exists on the topic in Danish. The entire process is documented 
in appendices: Appendix 6, Appendix 7, Appendix 8, Appendix 9, Appendix 10, 
Appendix 11, Appendix 12, Appendix 13, Appendix 14 and Appendix 15. Since the 
appendices: Appendix 11,  Appendix 12, Appendix 14 and Appendix 15 are very long, 
it was decided to show a preview, but the full length documents can be accessed via the 
Google Drive folder. The results are presented in the following sections, one section 
for each of the two research questions. A joint discussion of the literature reviews 
follows.
Blessing & Chakrabarti (2009) suggest updating the initial gap factors and research 
focus, questions and criteria from Work Package 1 straight after the literature review, 
in order to determine whether the obtained understanding of situation is satisfac-
tory, or if empirical studies are needed. However, seeing that the empirical study of 
case companies will be undertaken, the updating will take place after the conduction 
thereof.   
Before initiating the literature reviews, the terms start-up, small and medium sized 
enterprise (SME) and ecopreneur are defined to provide clarity and a common under-
standing, before reading the literature reviews.  
Chapter 5: Literature review
==PAGE:63==
47
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Literature review
Definitions
SMEs: The definition of SMEs varies across the world ranging from fewer than 100 
employees to 500 employees. Since this literature review is based on mainly research 
conducted in Europe, it seems relevant to use the European Union (EU) definition 
of SMEs. From the European Union Commission Recommendation Report (EU 
Commission, 2003), SMEs are defined as enterprises with less than 250 employees, 
consisting of three sub-types; Micro businesses are defined as less than 10 employees, 
small businesses as less than 50 employees and medium businesses as less than 
250 employees. In Europe, as of 2017, micro, small and medium sized enterprises 
accounted for a very large share of the total economic activities. 99.8% of all compa-
nies within the European Union are SMEs and they reach nearly 24 million companies 
in total while employing around 66% of all employees in Europe, which approximately 
is 94 million people. Out of 24 million European enterprises 93% of the companies 
are classified as micro businesses  with less than 10 employees (Muller et al., 2017). In 
average micro businesses actually have around 2 employees (Buttol et al. 2012). This 
indicates that when SMEs are characterised in the literature, the characteristics can 
often be interpreted as micro business characteristics.
Start-ups: A start-up is, according to Merriam Webster defined as “the act or an instance of  
setting in operation or motion” or “a fledgling business enterprise”. According to the Cambridge 
Dictionary a start-up is defined as “ a small business that has just been started”, and it most 
often refers to very small, young businesses, trying to introduce a product or service 
that is new to the market. In this project, the scope has been limited to sustainabili-
ty-driven, product-oriented start-ups. These start-ups are perceived to be run by a few 
people including the owner(s). These owners can be categorised as ecopreneurs.
Ecopreneurs: The definition of ecopreneurs (environmental entrepreneurs and green 
entrepreneurs) is not yet fully adopted in the literature and there are multiple defi-
nitions of an ecopreneur. One of the first and most basic definitions of the term 
stated that the word ecopreneur is a combination of ‘entrepreneurship’ and ‘ecolog-
ical’ (Schaltegger, 2002). Others have defined the term as “Entrepreneurs who found new 
businesses based on the principles of  sustainability” (Kirkwood & Walton, 2014, p. 38) and as 
“those entrepreneurs who combine environmental awareness with their business activities in a drive 
to shift the basis of  economic development towards a more environmentally friendly basis” (Gibbs, 
2009, p. 65 ). For the thesis, we have chosen to adopt the definition of an ecopreneurs 
as: Environmental sustainability-driven entrepreneurs.
5.1 
Characterisation and needs of start-ups and SMEs
The purpose of this literature review is to determine characteristics of start-ups and 
innovative, small businesses, and their barriers to and drivers of engaging in sustain-
ability practices. It is based on 33 studies, obtained from a systematic search. The 
investigated research question is:
RQ1: “What are the characteristics and needs of product-oriented start-ups and small 
businesses wanting to become more sustainable?”
Drivers of sustainability practices
Since the Brundtland Report was published in 1987, sustainability has become impor-
tant to successful product development. Many design practitioners have successfully 
developed and marketed products based on their sustainability principles, which have 
caused researchers to develop methods and tools to help businesses overcome various 
challenges associated with environmental sustainability in practice. It is clear that there 
is a growing commitment and demand for sustainability principles (Gliedt & Parker, 
2007). As a result, today’s consumers and designers attempt to overcome the chal-
lenges of sustainability and make world a better place to live in (Gast et al., 2017). This 
indicates the importance for green product development and designers have learned 
that consumers begin to choose green product over conventional products. But; 
==PAGE:64==
48
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Literature review
besides the market opportunities, what is it exactly that drives the ecopreneurs behind 
sustainability-driven businesses, to embrace the environmental sustainability principles 
and implement them as a core driver in their product development? Rodgers (2010) 
and Linnanen (2005) determine that the desires behind ecopreneurs are twofold and 
can be classified as follows:
 
+ The desire to change the world by improving the environment
 
+ The desire to earn money and grow a successful business venture
Linnanen (2005, p. 38) explains the desire to change the world as a “raison d’etre” 
trait, meaning that a strong opinion exceeds the drive for profits - usually to change 
the world and make it a better place. Kirkwood & Walton (2010) and Raudeliuniene et 
al. (2014) define some key success factors of sustainability driving start-ups and SMEs. 
These can be categorised and explained by personal and societal drivers as follows:
Personal drivers
 
+ Their “green” values
 
+ Making a living
 
+ Passion to drive a successful businesses
 
+ Being their own boss
Societal drivers
 
+ Identifying a gap in the market 
 
+ Making the world a better place
 
+ To comply with legislation and regulations
Raudeliuniene et al. (2014), Klöppel & Stummer (2015) and Kane (2012) further look 
into how implementing the above drivers can benefit small businesses and provide the 
foundation for a more competitive business - in times where environmental sustain-
ability has grown to be a considerable success factor for successful products. They 
highlight the following key benefits of being on the forefront with environmental 
sustainability principles:
 
+ Reducing operation costs by optimizing the production to get a higher margin or 
a more competitive structure
 
+ Becoming more robust and ready to adapt to future legislations and regulations, 
like green taxes and customer demand - thus reaching a strong customer orienta-
tion and win over niche markets
 
+ Reducing the risk of being targeted by NGO and regulators - avoiding green-
washing and to some extent resist market pressures
 
+ Establishing PR and marketing opportunities
 
+ Experiencing more motivated employees and attracting new recruits with shared 
values
Barriers to sustainability practice
In 2008, the Commision Of The European Communities, developed an action based 
plan aimed at helping start-ups and SMEs to comply with environmental legislations 
and regulations. During the development they discovered some crucial barriers rele-
vant for proactive SMEs trying to participate in eco-innovation activities. The barriers 
are as follows (Europeia C., 2008), supported by the findings of Kirkwood & Walton 
(2014).
 
+ Little awareness and knowledge of environmental problems, impacts and risks
 
+ Lack of holistic perspective on sustainability practices 
 
+ Insufficient access to adequate environmental information, tools and training
 
+ Limited financial and human resources/expertise for dealing with compliance
 
+ Short-term planning of business activities
 
+ Lack of infrastructure
 
+ Hard to prove sustainability practices, hence market the business on it
==PAGE:65==
49
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Literature review
The above mentioned barriers are further classified by Buttol et al. (2012, p. 214) in 
two categories. The first three barriers can be described as “market barriers” and the 
last four barriers can be defined as “technical, economic and social barriers”. This 
emphasises the need for specialised solutions, that gradually can help start-ups and 
SMEs to overcome the barriers of complying with legislation, as well as to implement 
environmental sustainability as a core driver (Simpson et al., 2004). In continuation of 
this Grünhagen (2008), Condon (2004), Kirkwood & Walton (2014) and Klewitz et 
al. (2012) find that SMEs have the following barriers to implementing environmental 
changes in their businesses: 
 
+ Environmental issues not seen as significant - cannot see the benefits
 
+ Size of the business - few personnel (low human capital)
 
+ Financial constraints - limited time
 
+ Diversity of SMEs - there is no general guideline
 
+ No management systems - IT based documentation and management of sustain-
ability
Most of the existing tools, developed to support environmental sustainability, were 
initially developed for larger companies (Falle et al., 2016). Therefore, start-ups find it 
difficult to engage with the tools, because the acquired expertise is difficult to obtain 
(Rezai et al., 2016). Furthermore Loucks et al. (2010), Woolman & Veshagh (2007) and 
Long et al. (2018) emphasise that since this expertise is not an in-house competency, 
the start-ups and SMEs are forced to ‘invest’ in the expertise, which problematically 
cost resources they do not have. Linnanen (2005) and Woolman & Veshagh (2007) 
further indicate that the start-ups and SMEs also find it difficult to see the potential 
environmental investment providing any short-term return. This can be seen as an 
indirect barrier, caused by the fact that the start-ups and SMEs cannot recognise the 
customer demand on the market (Sáez-Martínez et al., 2016).
Characteristics of start-ups and SMEs
The above described barriers and drivers of environmental sustainability clearly 
indicate that there are some major challenges associated with the implementation of 
sustainability principles, but also many benefits to be gained. Some of the personal 
traits that drive the key persons behind these businesses to aim for environmentally 
sustainable products have been identified, helping us to better understand the targeted 
users. In order to fully understand the causal links between barriers, opportunities and 
drivers, the following sections investigate and conclude on the characteristics of these 
businesses, underlining their main traits. Kirkwood & Walton (2014), Klewitz et al. 
(2012), Condon (2004), Schick et al. (2002) and Koch et al. (2012) all find that start-ups 
and SMEs: 
 
+ Have limited resources
 
+ Are limited by company size
 
+ Have a low degree of complexity
 
+ Have high owner influence
 
+ Focus particularly on the day to day activities - lacking strategic planning 
 
+ Lack holistic perspective on sustainability benefits
==PAGE:66==
50
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Literature review
Sáez-Martínez et al. (2016) emphasise that start-ups and SMEs have a short term 
focus during development of new products, which means that they find it difficult to 
see opportunities of improving the environmental performance and instead associate 
the work as cost without short term return. In continuation of this Jerónimo et al. 
(2016) conclude that there are three company types within sustainability; Conventional 
(incipient), Responsable (progressive) and Essential (inclusive). The three company 
types and their relation to sustainability can be simplistically described as follows:
 
+ Conventional: Profit over sustainability performance
 
+ Responsable: Profit equal with sustainability performance
 
+ Essential: Sustainability performance over profit
Furthermore, Hansen et al. (2012) support Rodgers (2010) and Linnanen (2005) and 
emphasise that ecopreneurs with a desire to change the world are more willing to take 
risks to achieve a successful product with a better environmental performance.
Potential role of intermediaries
Hofman & De Bruijn (2010), Klewitz et al. (2012), Memon et al. (2014) and Georgeson 
et al. (2014) determine that start-ups and SMEs need help from intermediaries - or in 
the context of this project interpreted as consultants - to overcome the challenges of 
incorporating sustainability practices. They find that intermediaries should take the 
role of being mentors and characterise, what this role implies:
 
+ Transfer knowledge - combination of practices
 
+ Transfer competencies
 
+ Forecast and diagnose sustainability challenges ahead - legislations and standards
 
+ Empower the start-ups and SMEs with the right attitudes
 
+ Provide network - connect the start-ups and SMEs
 
+ Gatekeeper - support in understanding contracts and agreements
 
+ Test and validate products
 
+ Evaluate and improve product performance - sustainability impacts and poten-
tials
In continuation, Shields & Shelleman (2015) and Szekely & Strebel (2013) find that 
intermediaries should provide the basis for strong internal leadership within the 
start-ups and SMEs, emphasising the importance of pushing these leaders in the right 
direction. With this, they mean that the start-up leaders should be empowered with 
adequate knowledge and competencies to take one step at a time towards improving 
the environmental performance of their products. Schick et al. (2002) further support 
that argument underlining that entrepreneurs need support in the implementation of 
sustainability practices in the early product development phases. They emphasise the 
opportunity for sustainability consultants within the start-up market: “ …new business 
ventures are generally more open to new ideas, because they lack an already existing organisational 
culture; therefore, it should be easier to introduce sustainable thinking into new ventures than into 
established enterprises.” (p. 61).
Furthermore, Cambra-Fierro et al. (2011) find that the adoption of sustainability 
practices, currently is delivered as stand-alone services, and argues that there is a need 
for more coherent educational programs, that over time can provide a deeper under-
standing of sustainability, specifically aimed at start-ups and SMEs. Finally, Jamous et 
al. (2013) find that the SMEs currently are dependent on experts to manage complex 
sustainability issues, which increase the operational cost of the business, hence indi-
cating that start-ups and SMEs need intermediary services to include guided aid, 
allowing them to continue working with the issue on their own.

5.2 
Gap between specialists and start-ups
Based on the search string, it was possible to obtain 25 studies to include in this 
literature review.  The focus of this review is on the gap that was identified in the 
research clarification stage between sustainability consultancies and start-ups and 
innovative, small businesses that are motivated towards sustainability practices. The 
insights obtained from the studies are interpreted continuously, and summarised in a 
conclusion. The investigated research question is:
RQ2: “What are the factors contributing to the gap between sustainability-driven 
start-ups & small businesses and sustainability consultants?”
Gap between small businesses and sustainability practices 
In general, smaller businesses are limited by scarce economic and time-wise resources 
(Labonne, 2006; Friedman et al., 2000; Depken & Zeman, 2017; Shahedul et al., 2016; 
Natarajan & Wyrick; 2011), making it hard to focus on anything other than the core 
business and generating a cash flow, which is not necessarily linked to sustainability. 
Bos-Brouwers (2010) supports that finding based on a case study of 26 small busi-
nesses, concluding that a lack of economic, knowledge and skill resources hinders 
the “sustainable innovation capacity” (Bos-Brouwers, 2010, p. 421) of small businesses. 
Through a large-scale survey with 240 respondents, Becherer & Helms (2014) find that 
environmental considerations are more common for businesses with 21 employees or 
more. They point to the reason being that those businesses are more structured, which 
provides a foundation for the formal implementation of environmental practices, and 
state: “Once a start-up evolves beyond the chaotic early months or years, environmental issues can 
become a consideration in strategy” (Becherer & Helms, 2014, p. 14). As such, this points to a 
challenge for the service to-be-developed in this thesis, that must be taken into consid-
eration. Contrary to Becherer & Helms, Bos-Brouwers (2010) and Lee (2009) find that 
the less structured nature of small businesses compared to larger businesses leads to a 
more adaptable business culture, where the personal motivations of the employees set 
the direction of the business to a higher degree, which in turn enables an easier imple-
mentation of new practices. A way of overcoming the economic resource scarcity can 
be through funding, but as Stayton & Mangematin (2018) observe; “Early funding 
has high costs: the financing process takes time and energy. A tension is generated 
between conducting activities to advance a new product launch and collecting more 
resources to fully develop the new product while launching an organization” (p. 4). 
The quote indicates that start-ups - despite their need for economic resources - can 
find it hard and outside of the core activities to apply for funding. 
The limited knowledge of small businesses is also stressed by Ferenhof et al. (2014), 
“ … small and medium enterprises (SMEs), in particular, are often unaware of  their impact on 
the environment and lack the knowledge and expertise needed to implement and manage EMS.” 
(p. 1). The same observation is made by Ghazilla et al. (2015), that furthermore 
observe that not all small businesses are convinced of the benefits of sustainability 
practices. Combined with the limited economic resources, it can be concluded that it 
is difficult for consultancies to engage small businesses, because small businesses are 
not necessarily aware of their environmental impact or are convinced of the benefits 
of sustainability practices, hence they will not see the importance or environmental 
services, particularly paid services. On the other hand, Oxborrow & Brindley (2011) 
observe from their case study that most small businesses are aware of and concerned 
about sustainability issues, which reveals a potential for the service to-be-developed.  
The relatively small size of start-ups and small businesses compared to larger busi-
nesses can also be a barrier for environmental supply chain management due to small 
quantities of materials ordered from a supplier (Oxborrow & Brindley, 2011) and 
limited knowledge about alternative suppliers, leaving the small businesses with little 
bargaining power (Porter, 1979). 
==PAGE:69==
53
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Literature review
Motivations for sustainability practices in small businesses
It has been established that economic resources are scarce for small businesses and 
start-ups and, as a result, engaging in sustainability practices should be easy and clearly 
value-creating (Friedman et al., 2000). This could be coined with the financial term 
‘return on investment’ - namely, that the returns from economically and time-wise 
investing in sustainability practices must be good in order for small business and 
start-ups to consider engagement. 
Another reason, that Friedman et al. (2000) find, for small businesses to engage in 
sustainability practices is the internal motivation of the business towards sustainability 
rather than external motivations due to e.g. legislative pressure. On a different note, 
Heidrich & Tiwary (2013) conclude from a review article that legislation is indeed 
pressuring small businesses to act towards sustainability. For the service to-be-devel-
oped, it should be clear whether the targeted businesses are engaging in sustainability 
practices due to internal or external motivation. Grimm & Amatucci (2013) develop 
a series of questions to determine just that, and they argue it can be used for the 
implementation of sustainable initiatives as well. It helps to assess a business’ commit-
ment to sustainability practices, and the business’ sustainability knowledge, to identify 
potential strategic partners and to determine the amount of funds, the business is 
willing to invest in sustainability practices. In the context of this thesis, it may be 
useful to employ a set of questions to determine the targeted customers’ motivation/
commitment to sustainability practices, their level of knowledge and their economic 
willingness. Another way of further categorising small businesses and their strategic 
sustainability motivation is to differ between small businesses that are ‘Sustainabili-
ty-rooted’, ‘Innovation-based’, ‘Anticipatory’, ‘Reactive’ or ‘Resistant’ (Klewitz & Hansen, 
2014, figure 4), which may also be useful in the categorisation of the targeted users of 
the concept.
Based on a case study of 15 small businesses, Oxborrow & Brindley (2011) propose 
a framework for understanding what drives sustainability in the three, overall phases 
of business development: ‘Initiation’, ‘Development’ and ‘Learning’ (Oxborrow & 
Brindley, 2011, figure 1). In the ‘initiation’ phase, it is particularly the social or environ-
mental impact, the small business may potentially have, that drives sustainability. In the 
later phase ‘development’, the authors find that the driver for sustainability will be the 
potential for improving the product and production method, or the potential for new 
market opportunities. In the last phase ‘learning’, the driver for sustainability is cost 
reduction. The framework is consistent with NIRAS’ view of how sustainability can 
be implemented; it can either be by changing something entirely (“omstille”) or making 
it more efficient (“effektivisere”). Changes are most likely to take place in early stages 
of a product development phase, whereas making something more efficient could 
likely take place at later stages. In conclusion, the service to-be-developed should take 
into consideration that start-ups in different development phases will have different 
drivers, hence needs, for sustainability. Early phase start-ups will need change-oriented 
sustainability initiatives, whereas late phase start-ups will need efficiency-oriented 
sustainability initiatives.
Bridging the gap
Burke & Gaughran (2007) investigate the implementation of environmental manage-
ment systems to fulfill the ISO standard 14001 in small businesses, and they suggest a 
model consisting of incremental steps towards implementing environmental manage-
ment systems. Even though that may not be the goal for the users of the service 
to-be-developed, the steps can serve as an inspiration for the development of the 
concept. The steps suggested by Burke & Gaughran are grounded in an in-depth, 
qualitative study of eight SMEs. The first three steps ‘Environmental Awareness 
Program’, ‘Initial Environmental Review’ and ‘Strategy Development’ (Burke & 
Gaughran, 2007, figure 2) could in particular be interesting as these are more easily 
transferred to start-ups, whereas steps 4-8 are more relevant to larger SMEs than 
newly established businesses. The model can be interpreted to obtain the insight that 
==PAGE:70==
54
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Literature review
before conducting environmental analyses or developing environmental strategies, it 
must be considered that the small business have adequate knowledge about and moti-
vation towards sustainability practices.
Collinson & Gregson (2003) point to the high level of uncertainty within start-ups 
and entrepreneurial businesses, which stresses the need for knowledge networks 
to enable start-ups to obtain new knowledge and test their concepts. Knowledge 
networks refer to institutions like start-up communities or hubs, and hence Collinson 
& Gregson’s insights may not be directly transferable to the focus of this thesis which 
is on consultants or specialists, but it can be concluded that start-ups experience high 
levels of uncertainty, and knowledge transfer and validation of concepts are suitable 
ways to address that uncertainty.
Many of the above-mentioned studies have identified that SMEs have a lack of 
knowledge of and experience with sustainability practices, and this is a barrier for 
engaging in the practices. The service to-be-developed in this project will involve 
consultancy services, and to support the feasibility of the concept, it can be mentioned 
that transferring knowledge to action within business practices is most effective 
through human interaction (Jones & de Zubielqui, 2017), as well as influencing atti-
tude to the environment can be achieved via educational activities (Quinn et al., 2009). 
The direction of small businesses with regards to sustainability and general strategy 
is highly influenced by the personal values and identity of the business owners and 
managers. Kaesehage et al. (2014) state: “Business leaders understand and situate climate 
change within personal values and belief  systems to produce their own personal lay knowledge of  
climate change, which in turn influence their decision-making” (p. 94). Based on a case study of 
30 SMEs, the authors suggest that to attract SMEs to engage in sustainability practices, 
the personal values of the business leader must be targeted. Furthermore, it has been 
observed that business owners or managers of micro enterprises are five times more 
likely to engage in sustainability practices, if they have received training (Cassells & 
Lewis, 2017). The above studies all point to the fact that the service to-be-developed 
should target the start-ups at a personal level by being educational and involving 
human interaction.

5.2.2 Discussion of literature reviews
Limitations and future research
Given the limited experience with conducting literature reviews, the process of devel-
oping, testing and using the search string can be seen as a limitation to the study. After 
conducting the search, we found relevant additional words that had not been included 
in the search string, e.g. ‘barriers’. On the other hand, we did perform additional 
searches when finding highly relevant terms such as ‘ecopreneur’.  Future work could 
investigate if there are any other prominent words missing from the search string, that 
might lead to obtaining even more relevant studies. In continuation, the quality of the 
obtained studies have not been thoroughly evaluated and discussed, since the thesis 
team did not feel qualified nor able to - within the scope of the project - to assess the 
quality of the obtained literature. This reduces the validity of the obtained insights, 
but, seeing that there were no ground-breaking, contradictory conclusions from the 
studies in the literature reviews, it is assessed that the validity is not unsatisfactory low. 
Relevance of research topic
From the literature reviews, it is clear that many studies have been conducted on SME 
implementation of environmental management systems and eco-oriented manage-
ment strategies. However, very few focus on the implementation within start-ups or 
micro businesses. Furthermore, no studies were found that investigated the interaction 
between sustainability consultants and start-ups, which indicates that this thesis will be 
able to shed light on a so far unexplored area.

 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Literature review
Empirical study focus
The empirical case study should focus on deepening the understanding of the target 
users, and should thus sample case companies that can be characterised as prod-
uct-oriented start-ups or innovative micro businesses, driven by internal sustainability 
motivations. 
Start-ups and innovative micro businesses. It is well-known that changes for a positive envi-
ronmental impact are more effectual when implemented in early product development 
stages (McAloone & Tan, 2005). It also became clear through the literature reviews 
that ‘SME’ and ‘micro business’ are wide terms covering 98 % and 93 % of European 
businesses, respectively. As a result, it was decided to limit the focus of the empirical 
study to involve start-ups and innovative micro businesses.  
Product-oriented. From the scope limitation, it was established to focus only on prod-
uct-oriented businesses, due to NIRAS’ competencies.
Internal sustainability motivation. From the literature review, it became clear that there 
are different motivations for engaging in sustainability practices. External motivations 
refer to the pressure from legislation or buyers, whereas internal motivations refer 
to personal values that steer the business in a sustainable direction. It is assumed 
that start-ups fuelled by internal motivations are more likely to pay for sustainability 
consultancy services, hence making them a more viable business case.  
In the rest of the report, the term ‘start-up’ will cover both start-ups and/or inno-
vative micro businesses for the sake of simplicity. 
5.3 
Literature review conclusion
Through the systematic literature review the researchers of the project was able to 
identify several answers to the first two  research questions of the project.
To answer the first research question, a list of barriers to sustainable practices was 
identified. For example that start-up/SMEs experience a general lack off knowledge 
and awareness of environmental issues. Furthermore a list of drivers of sustainability 
practices  for SMEs and startups was found in the literature. For example new busi-
ness opportunities and competitive advantages. Additionally a list of characteristics 
of start-ups and SMEs was found in the literature. A few examples are, that start-ups 
and SMEs in general have limited resources and the focus on sustainability is often 
dependant on the owner of the business. Finally the literature showed how interme-
diaries can play a role in overcoming challenges with achieving sustainable business 
practices, for example by transferring knowledge and competencies and by helping a 
start-up or SME to develop sustainable strategies.
To answer the second research question the literature was studied to identify some 
of the factors that lead to a gap between start-ups and sustainable practices. An 
example is that the small size and limited resources of start-ups and SMEs, means that 
sustainable practices are difficult to implement for them. Furthermore the literature 
was able to define the gap that exist between consultancies and start-ups, for example 
that a lack of knowledges means that the benefits of employing sustainable business 
practices are not always obvious for start-ups /SMEs. Finally the literature showed the 
opportunities  for consultancies to deliver services to start-ups and SMEs, for example 
that start-ups/SMEs have a very high adaptability and are more risk willing.
From the systematic literature review it can thus be concluded that research question 
one and two was successfully covered and answered to an extent that the knowledge 
gathered could be used as the basis for further work in the thesis project.
==PAGE:73==
57
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
In this chapter, the conduction of the empirical study is described as well as the applied 
methods. The qualitative study involved six case start-ups, in a combination of co-cre-
ation sessions and interviews. Based on a thorough analysis of the data, a list of needs 
is generated, alongside a number of more general insights about the case start-ups 
and their experiences with sustainability practices. Start-up development phases are 
explored to enable a better understanding and differentiation of the target customers. 
Finally, the six case start-ups are categorised based on the start-ups characteristics 
obtained from the literature review.
6.1 Qualitative case study
When establishing the research focus, it was considered how to answer each research 
question. RQ1 would be most appropriately answered through a quantitative study, 
RQ2 could be answered through a quantitative and/or a qualitative approach, and 
RQ3 should be answered through a qualitative study. A qualitative approach was 
chosen for three reasons:
1. The main research question should have the deciding vote, and the main RQ3 
would be most appropriately answered through a qualitative study. Even though 
the aim is not to be able to answer RQ3 after the conduction of this Work 
Package, having performed a qualitative study involving case start-ups, will make 
it easier to involve the cases later in the process.
2. The aim of the project is to develop a sustainability consultancy service based 
on the needs of the target users; start-ups. However, the nature of a consul-
tancy service is not final; the establishment cost of offering a new service is 
relatively low (if based on existing competencies), compared to establishing a 
whole new production process for a product. It was assessed that even though 
the user needs are crucial to the success of the service, they can also change 
quickly influenced by societal factors or consumer trends. Therefore, conducting 
a comprehensive, quantitative study to obtain an exhaustive list of user needs 
would be overly thorough, taking time from other parts of the project. To make 
up for the lack of a quantitative, systematic approach to collecting user needs, the 
literature review had been systematic, and axiomatic design would be applied to 
the needs obtained from the user involvement in the empirical study, to ensure 
measurability.  
3. Finally, RQ2 could be answered through a qualitative as well as a quantitative 
study.       
  
Exploratory and inductive
The aim of the empirical study is to obtain a greater understanding of the targeted 
users and the surrounding context. Therefore, it is appropriate to conduct an explor-
atory, inductive study, in order to better understand the contemporary phenomena 
– the reason being that the desired outcome of the study will be context-dependent, 
concrete results to be applied by the involved stakeholders.
Case study
The qualitative study investigates product-oriented start-ups or innovative micro busi-
nesses, driven by internal sustainability motivation. To serve that purpose, a flexible 
research design is chosen (Maxwell, 2012), and more specifically a multiple case study 
research (Gray, 2014). The reason for choosing a case study research design is that 
the research questions consists of a “how?”-question, which requires in-depth and 
extensive understanding to be answered. The project is characterized by limited time 
and resources – and even though qualitative studies are time consuming and maybe 
not the most appropriate approach to select in a time-limited research design – the 
focus of the research required a research design which allowed for in-depth responses 
and flexibility.
Chapter 6:  Empirical study
==PAGE:74==
58
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
The collected data will be depend very much on the situation that the studied cases are 
in - e.g. where in the design phase, which industry and level of experience. Therefore, 
it is necessary to have a flexible and adaptable method, like semi-structured interviews, 
also known as the interview guide approach (Patton, 2002). However, when studying 
the case start-ups, the aim is to obtain insights about their needs, and therefore the 
research team will also apply methods from co-creation to facilitate a dialogue, in great 
part because it is less controlled by the research team’s presumptions. 
It is important to be aware of the following influencing factors shown in Table 7 when

6.1.1 Case selection
The purpose of the case study is to deepen the understanding of the target users, and 
it should thus sample case companies that can be characterised as product-oriented 
start-ups or innovative micro businesses, driven by internal sustainability motivations. 
The aim of the case selection was to purposefully sample the cases in a representative 
manner, in order to increase the validity of the research. The case start-ups had been 
sampled, so that several development phases and a variety of industries and level of 
sustainability experience were represented. The aim was that all the case start-ups 
should be product-oriented start-ups or innovative micro businesses, driven by 
internal sustainability motivations, and not external motivations like legislative pres-
sure on their business, but in reality two case were less product-oriented and more 
technology-driven. The six case start-ups are presented in Chapter 3, and they will be 
characterised further after the conduction of the empirical study.  
The six case start-up that were selected for user involvement sessions participated 
in two co-creation sessions - similar to focus groups - and four interviews. It can be 
assumed that 80 % of the user needs will have been identified, according to Figure 
22 adopted from Ulrich & Eppinger (2015, pp. 77). This seems like a reasonable 
percentage, seeing that a comprehensive literature study has also been performed.  
6.1.2 Co-creation method
Co-creation is a design method with a user-centred approach. However, a clear and 
distinct definition of co-creation does not exist at the moment. A reason is that the 
term is used across different fields, where conductors are assigning individual and 
different attributes to the term. In this project, the term is used with reference to the 
definition developed in design research. Sanders & Stappers (2008) define co-creation 
as: 
Table 7: Influence factors
==PAGE:75==
59
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
“The authors take co-creation to refer to any act of  collective creativity, i.e., 
creativity that is shared by two or more people” 
(Sanders et al., 2008)
In relation to co-creation the term co-cesigning is to some degree used interchangeably. 
However, Sanders and Stappers define co-design as a specific instance of co-creation, 
in the following three examples:
“By co-design we indicate collective creativity as it is applied across the whole 
span of  a design process…”
“...co-design is a specific instance of  co-creation…”
“(Co-design) refer to the creativity of  designers and people not trained in 
design working together in the design development process.”
(Sanders et al., 2008)
Mattelmäki & Sleeswijk (2011) also define co-Creation and co-Design: 
“Co-Creation is
a creative, mood/mindset and methods within co-design process.
is about exchange of  ideas, experiences, expertise.
is temporary. It has specific parts within the design process.
Co-design is
utilised in design context in which designers are involved and the topic of  the 
activity is related to design exploration, envisioning and solution development.
an empowering mindset and it gives voice and tools to those who were not 
traditionally part of  design process.
about engagement of  potential users but also about stakeholder collaboration.
a process and tools of  collaborative engagement, e.g. events for learning and 
exploration.”
(Mattelmäki et al., 2011)
1
2
3
4
5
6
Figure 22: With six user sessions, 80 % of customer needs should be identified
==PAGE:76==
60
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
Mattelmäki & Sleeswijk contradict Sanders & Stappers by claiming that co-creation is 
an area within co-design, adding to the confusing of the terms. However, Mattelmäki 
& Sleeswijk provide a more narrow definition of what elements co-design and co-cre-
ation consist of. Compared to conventional product design, the role of the user is 
shifted with this approach, by focusing on how non-designers can be involved in the 
design process. Sanders & Stappers also look at how co-creation shifts the role of the 
researchers and users, as illustrated in Figure 23. In conventional design processes, the 
user is merely “studied” by the designer. In co-creation, the user becomes an “expert” 
who is actively involved in the creative design process. This means that the role of 
the designer also changes, from being responsible for translating the user needs, to 
being responsible of facilitating and guiding the users in the creative processes of 
co-creation.
The main benefits of co-creation is that involving users in the design process can lead 
to more valid understanding of the users, because the user involvement has been less 
affected by the presumptions of the design researcher (Lee, 2008). The disadvantage 
on the other hand is that co-creation, compared to interviews or surveys, may easier 
derail from the actual aim of the user involvement, thus more time consuming to 
conduct. But, this in turn can lead to insights and opportunities that may not have 
been discovered otherwise.  
In the DTU course 42081 Staging co-creation, co-creation was taught as a practical and 
with a “learning-by-doing” approach. One of the outputs from the course was that 
the students developed a guidebook with good practical examples, concrete advice and 
guidelines on how to successfully plan and facilitate a co-creation workshop. A guide-
book was developed by Thomas Sønderby, which was used as inspiration to develop 
the co-creation workshop during this project. A sample from the guidebook can be 
seen in Appendix 16.
Figure 23: Roles of the user and researcher in a conventional design process vs. co-de-
sign/co-creation. (Sanders et al., 2008)
==PAGE:77==
61
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
Applying co-creation
The general approach from co-creation is to divide a workshop into five steps: 
Opening, Create Comfort, Stage Discussion (Generate ideas), Develop Together 
(Generate solutions) and Closing. The main goal of the workshops to be conducted in 
this project was to to identify customer needs related to and experiences with sustain-
ability practices. Thus, developing solutions at this stage was deemed undesirable. The 
workshop was instead designed to develop solutions in the sense that the needs of 
the participants later will become the foundation for the developed solution. It must 
be noted that the workshop for this case study cannot be regarded as a “genuine” or 
consistent use of co-creation. Instead, co-creation was used as a tool to gather insights 
and user needs and as a way of including the user in the development process to some 
degree.
From the literature review, it had been established that this empirical study should 
focus on deepening the understanding of the target users, particularly providing the 
insights to answer the first research questions 1 and 2.  A playbook to guide the co-cre-
ation was developed found in Appendix 17. Through a brainstorming session within 
the thesis, a series of relevant areas of interest were identified as seen in Appendix 
18. These areas were used to create the overall questions to be explored in the co-cre-
ation sessions. The identified areas were: Competencies in the start-ups, Challenges 
for start-ups, Sustainability and Funding. The questions related to competencies, 
challenges and funding were not directly aimed at collecting needs, rather the purpose 
of these questions was to gain insights into the general challenges that start-ups face, 
as well as being able to characterise the start-ups based on the answers. Some of 
the main theoretical considerations in developing the co-creation session template are 
elaborated in the following.
Creating comfort can be difficult with small groups. It is easier to push people out of their 
comfort zone in larger groups, as a person can easier “hide” in the crowd. It is also 
important to consider how much the boundaries can be pushed, when preparing for 
the workshop. In this case it was estimated that it would be most appropriate to keep 
the workshop fairly generic without any “silly” exercises. Instead, the researchers tried 
to create comfort by creating a cosy atmosphere by incorporating simple warm up 
exercises, for example making the participant come up with a new slogan for a new 
NIRAS start-up department.
“Diverging activities should initially be done individually.” (Valgeirsdottir, 2016). An example 
of this is during brainstorming. Working individually ensures that all the participants 
are heard, and not just the “loudest” talker. This was implemented by trying to ensure 
that the idea generation was done individually by writing ideas and thoughts on post-its.
“Converging activities should be done in teams” (Valgeirsdottir, 2016) - while still ensuring 
that everyone is heard, which can be achieved for example by voting or ranking. This 
ensures that the average opinion is brought forward. This was implemented by using 
ideas and thoughts as a discussion objects and by ranking them. Since the teams of 
participants were rather small it was estimated that everyone could be heard. 
Ensuring that the participants are not primed to give the answer the facilitator is looking for. This 
is always a difficult challenge. With interviews it is easy to prime the participant(s), 
simply with the way you interact with them. For example nodding if a participant 
say something you find interesting. Something this simple can actually be a risk of 
influencing or priming be participants in the direction you wish, ultimately giving false 
results (Valgeirsdottir, 2016). Thus, it was a very important when designing  and facil-
itating the workshop to be aware of these risks and try to avoid them. An example of 
avoiding this was to make sure that the participants answered some of the question by 
writing their ideas/thoughts down on post-its. This ensures that the answers were less 
primed by the facilitator and other participants.
==PAGE:78==
62
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
6.1.3 Grounded Theory Method 
Due to the qualitative nature of the case study, the overall inductive approach and 
the team’s experience, it was decided to use the Grounded Theory Method (Bryant 
& Charmaz, 2007) as the theoretical foundation for the analysis of the collected data. 
Grounded Theory Method is focussed on the empirical data, and that the researchers 
must stay grounded in the data when deriving theories and drawing conclusions. 
Therefore, it will be necessary to transcribe all co-creation and interview sessions, and 
then go through the process of coding (Saldaña, 2009) to extract valuable insights and 
needs from the data. During the coding process it is essential to analyse the data from 
a neutral standpoint, in order to avoid any presupposed opinions about the research 
topic, caused by conducting and interpreting the research before coding. 
6.1.4 Execution
An overview of the interviews and co-creation sessions completed in the case study 
is presented in Table 2. As shown in the table, it was not possible to go through with 
co-creation sessions with all case companies. Some did unfortunately not have the time 
it required, and it was necessary to find alternative solutions. Instead the sessions were 
carried out as interviews, still following the developed template, to ensure comparable 
results. A discussion of the conduction of the co-creation can be found in Appendix 
19.
6.1.5 Data analysis
After conducting and transcribing five out of six user involvement sessions, an analysis 
of the raw “data” was performed according to the Grounded Theory Method (GTM). 
A method within  GTM is the so-called ‘coding’, where qualitative data is analysed bit 
by bit. According to Saldaña, a code is: 
 “ … a short phrase that symbolically assigns a summative, salient, essence-cap-
turing, and/or evocative attribute for a portion of  language-based or visual data.” 
(Saldaña, 2009, pp. 3).

The analysed data can be anything from observation notes, interview transcripts and 
artifacts. The recordings of the user involvement sessions were transcribed, and as 
artifacts there were post-it notes from the co-creation sessions documenting compe-

The coding procedure was done line by line to break the data into separate bits, over 
the course of four steps, see Figure 25. The first two steps and the interview tran-
scriptions are documented in Appendix 20, and an example of them are depicted in 
Figure 26.
For the FiveMoreMinutes interview transcript, the team applied process coding 
(Saldaña, 2009, pp. 77) in addition to highlighting inspired by the “in-vivo” coding, 
because it allows the researcher to interpret more, than by just highlighting. See an 
example in Figure 27.  However the team found that it was too time consuming, and 
did not apply this type of coding to the other transcripts.

After the second step, called focussed coding, 64 needs and 10 constraints had 
appeared, identified by a prefix ‘N’ or ‘C’, respectively. When translating the codes into 
needs, it was important to translate as directly as possible from statement to need in 
order to ensure an explicit connection link between needs and requirements for the 
solution. 
The third step of coding was to cluster similar needs, which is documented in 
Appendix 21. The purpose was to reduce the amount of needs and identify, which 
needs appeared most frequently. As a result, 22 needs clusters appeared, and in the 
final step of coding these clusters were applied to the CRR Denmark transcript. The 
code/need clusters had a high degree of fit with content of interview, and only three 
new needs emerged, which increases the reliability of the codes derived from the first 
five interviews. 
6.1.6 Coding results
The result from the four steps of coding was a list of user needs and three client 
constraints, as shown in Table 9. Sentences in italic are our clarifications.
In addition to identifying needs, the coding process also led to the identification of 
design constraints limiting the solution space. Table 10 shows the identified design 
constraints.
Clearly, the most frequent user needs revolved around getting a sustainability veri-
fication, e.g. needs 1, 3, 4, 5, 6, 7. There are two underlying reasons behind these 
needs, which can be obtained from the context of the codes. The first reason is that 
the start-ups need a validation of their concept or product - i.e. are they headed in 
the “right” direction, if they want to run a sustainable business? This is similar to the 
findings of Collinson & Gregson (2003), who conclude that start-ups - having a high 
level of uncertainty - benefit from concept validation. The second reason is that - in 
line with the findings of several of the studies from the literature review - having 
an external expert evaluation would increase the validity of a concept’s or product’s

6.1.7 Insights from case study
From the empirical study, a number of valuable insights were obtained, which are 
presented below and in Figure 28. The origin of the insights, i.e. which case start-up(s) 
they stem from, is presented in square brackets below. The interview transcriptions 
and codes are found in Appendix 20.   
 
+ Throughout the co-creation and interview sessions, it was pointed out that the 
case start-ups suffer from very limited resources, especially economic resources 
[Amino Wool, Hacktus]. The start-ups expressed that economic issues have been 
a reason for not reaching out to external consultants. 
 
+ Another insight that was obtained is that start-ups have little of  the data 
required to perform comprehensive environmental assessments [Amino Wool, 
Hacktus]. An example came from Amino Wool, who have actually reached out to 
NIRAS in the past, but amongst other realised that they lacked data and knowl-
edge to get the services they needed. 
 
+ A third insight was that case start-ups experienced that they lack knowledge of  
environmental sustainability [Amino Wool, FiveMoreMinutes, DropBucket]. 
It was expressed that they had limited knowledge of environmental sustaina-
bility and found it complex and hard to define, what is actually sustainable. 
Particularly the latter can help explain why one of the case start-up were not 
convinced that sustainability consultancy is worth paying for [AA Water].   
 
+ The fourth obtained insight was that start-ups need external help to validate 
the environmental sustainability of their concepts or products [Amino Wool, 
FiveMoreMinutes, Hacktus, DropBucket, CRR Denmark]. This relates to the lack

6.2 Start-up development phases
From the literature review, it was found that the needs of start-ups differ, depending 
on which phase, they are in. Product development phase refers to the typical process 
steps, a product development team will go through when developing a product. A 
typical example and description of this process is shown in Figure 29 (Ulrich & 
Eppinger, 2012). The business development phase of start-ups is a way of describing, 
how far a business is in its development. A example from the online start-up resource 
website start-upcommons.com is shown in Figure 30. The model describes the steps 
necessary to go from a business idea to establishing a product or service on the market.
Start-ups often have a limited range of products or services, if not only one. This 
could potentially mean that the development phases of the start-up or micro business 
involved in early product development are very similar to the product development 
phases as suggested by e.g. Ulrich & Eppinger. It is attempted to link the business 
development phases (BDP) of start-ups and product development phases (PDP) in 
Table 11 and to emphasise the similarities between them.

The first two phases from the models are very similar, but of course there can be 
different starting points - i.e. a start-up will start with an idea, whereas product devel-
opment teams often start from an identified need or gap in the market. Again, the two 
models align quite well, however the phases from the PDP model 3. System-level-de-
sign and 4. Detail Design are clustered together as they correspond to the 3. Commit-
ting phase from BDP model. The 4. Validating phase from BDP corresponds well to 
the 5. Testing and Refinements phase from the PDP model. Finally, the 6. Production 
Ramp-Up from PDP corresponds well to the 5. Scaling from BDP. The final phase of 
the BDP model 6. Establishing does not have a counterpart in the PDP model. Based 
on this comparison, a hypothesis is put forward:
Hypothesis: “Product development phases and the business development 
phases of a start-up that is product-oriented cohere with one another”. 
If the hypothesis is robust, it suggests that the target users of the service to-be-devel-
oped could both be start-ups and innovative micro businesses involved in new product 
development.
Start-up adaptability and funding
Figure 30 showed the start-up development phase from the online start-up advisory 
company start-upcommons.org (2018). The model described the phases a start-up will 
typically go through from the initial idea to maintaining a growing business. 
Product development and design changes
Figure 31 describes the relation between the phases of a product, the potential number 
of design alternatives and the cost of design changes (Ehrlich et al., 1994). The model 
is not specific to start-ups, but can be used to describe how the product/service design 
specification decisions made in the early phases will be costly to change later in the 
development.

 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
Commercialization of  products
Figure 32 describes a generalised process of commercialising a product and the profit/
loss-correlation in this process (Osawa & Miyazaki, 2006). The model was originally 
developed in the context of large-scale R&D projects in Japanese companies, but 
is estimated to be general enough to be used as a model to describe the profit/loss 
of start-ups as well. The models shows the challenge of investing in new product 
development, particularly the ‘valley of death’, where all of the investments has been 
made, and no profit has yet been generated. 
6.2.1 Introducing a new model: Start-up development phases, 
funding and adaptability
From Figure 30, Figure 31 and Figure 32 a combination model was created in an 
attempt to describe the correlation between the factors in the previous mentioned 
models. The model is shown in Figure 33.
The constant variable between the models is the x-axis, which describes the develop-
ment phase. The six business development phases from the BDP model (start-upcom-
mons.com, 2018) are used for that axis, assuming that the hypothesis about linking 
business development phases with product development phases is robust. Above 
the x-axis there are the two lines; cost of change and number of design alternatives, 
adapted from Ehrlich et al. (1994). Finally, the profit/loss-correlation model is imple-
mented by showing the assumed cumulative profit/loss graph of start-ups. It must be 
noted that the exact time-frame of the different models will not always match each 
other in reality. Instead, this model attempt to describe a general correlation. At the 
bottom of Figure 33, the characteristics of each development phase are elaborated in 
a table. The table is divided in the six business development phases, with an overall 
categorisation of early-, in-between- and late-phase business development. The row in 
the table are a score for each business development phase on the parameters: ‘Amount 
of funds’ and ‘adaptability’. The parameters are described in depth in the following 
sections.
Amount of funds
This parameter relates to the cumulative profit/loss-correlation. In the model, it 
is shown how start-ups will go through a long period without generating revenue, 
while the product/service is being developed. In this period, start-ups will be very 
dependant on funding, investments or loans in order to be able to advance to the 
next development phase. Technological start-ups will probably not be able to reach a 
product launch without some sort of economical backing. However, the amount of 
funding necessary to keep the start-up running will be small initially and grow as the 
start-up moves towards product launch. The high number of design alternatives does 
however mean that the funding that can be applied for is smaller due to the high levels 
of uncertainty for investors, banks or funds. Start-ups in the later phases are more 
likely to receive larger sums of funding, loans or investments since their product/
service is more well defined. For the service to-be-developed, this means that start-ups 
in the initial development phases are likely to have less funds available compared to 
the later stages, hence they are less able to spend money on sustainability consultancy 
offerings. 
Adaptability
In the bottom row of Figure 33, the score for adaptability is shown for each of the 
development phase categories. Start-ups in the early development phases will have 
a high adaptability. The reason being that they have not yet defined the product/
service attributes and they have not yet invested in these decisions, making it easy to 
adapt to new inputs. On the other hand, start-ups in later phases will already have 
locked in on the product/service attributes and invested in these decisions The result 
is a low adaptability. For NIRAS this means that start-ups will likely be open to and 
seek input and advice from experts on how to implement design changes that can 
move the start-up in a more environmentally sustainable direction. On the other hand, 
start-ups in the late phases will likely be more closed to advice or inputs that require 
fundamental design change.

Categorising the case start-ups
After having introduced the theoretical foundation of start-up development phases, it 
is possible to characterise the start-ups that were involved in the case study. The case 
start-ups are characterised in Table 12.
Several of the start-ups have the same professional background as the research team, 
so to ensure variety, case start-ups like FiveMoreMinutes and CRR Denmark had been 
included. The case start-ups vary across the entire range in ‘Industry’, ‘Business devel-
opment phase’, ‘Source of income’, and the size of the case companies with regards 
to ‘Number of people’. A particularly interesting column is the one that ranges the 
case companies based on their ‘Sustainability motivation’. Two of the case companies 
are classified as sustainability-rooted, whereas AA Water, CRR Denmark and Hacktus, 
are classified as innovation-based. This means that they have been able to use envi-
ronmental issues as a source of innovation, and they are classified this way, because 
they are all driven by their technology, which has a sustainable potential. Finally, Drop-
bucket’s sustainability attitude was characterised as anticipatory, because their aim is 
to grow a product which is already on the market, and working with environmental 
issues is a competitive advantage for them (Klewitz & Hansen, 2014). The two last 
columns show the involvement of the case companies, the testing refers to the user 
involvement in the next Work Package 3 Concept development. Finally in Figure 34, 
the case start-ups can be plotted in the model combining ‘Start-up phases, funding and 
adaptability’ to compare, how they should perform according to the model, with the 
actual findings presented in Table 12.
Testing the developed model
The developed model indicates that Hacktus - being in the earliest business develop-
ment phase, because they currently have not decided on an idea to pursue - have a high 
number of design alternatives, and have incurred almost no costs. This is coherent 
with the information obtained about Hacktus, presented in Table 1. 
Amino Wool is in in the process of developing a fiber technology to replace wool, 
but they have not yet committed to any specifics about the technology’s introduction 
to the market; thus, they are in the ‘Concepting’ phase. They have received some 
funding which has been used primarily for technical equipment, so they have incurred 
some costs. If their fiber technology is perceived as their ‘product’, they do not have 
a large number of design alternatives, hence the theoretical model is incoherent with 
practice. 
AA Water is similar to Amino Wool in the way that their ‘product’ is also a tech-
nology. The technology can soften water without using chemicals. They are further 
in their business development phases, because they have actually developed the tech-
nology funded by large amounts compared to e.g. Amino Wool. They are currently 
finalising the design of the container, in which the technology will work, hence 
‘Committing’ to the conceptual design, and soon moving on to the ‘Validating’ phase. 
The theoretical model suggests that AA Water will incur more costs before generating 
revenue, which is not unlikely, seeing that the testing of the concept will most likely
