Figure 34: Case start-ups in developed model: ‘Start-up phases, funds and adaptability
==PAGE:90==
74
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
be costly. Regarding the number of design alternatives, the theoretical model is more 
coherent with practice, because there is still room for design changes in the design of 
the container without incurring much loss. 
CRR Denmark have produced a filter for the maritime industry, which is being testing 
in docked ships at the moment. Consequently, they are in the ‘Validating’ phases. They 
have, similar to AA Water received large amounts of funding, and have also incurred 
costs for the development of the filter. They have reached a point, where changes 
to the design are not made without losses, because that would diminish the testing 
performed so far. The theoretical model seems to be coherent with CRR Denmark’s 
current situation. 
FiveMoreMinutes are at the verge of launching their product, which is bedding 
made from linen/flax rather than cotton. They buy the linen from a supplier, and 
supervise the production themselves, after which they will be selling B2B and B2C. 
They have incurred some costs for testing tactile quality of the materials and estab-
lishing a website. It it likely that they incur more costs, before generating revenue as 
they will need to stock and acquire customers. They have few design alternatives, and 
the cost of change will be high, once they have invested in a stock of their products. 
The developed model is coherent with FiveMoreMinutes’ situation.  
Finally, DropBucket is in the latest phase, ‘Establishing’, because they have been on 
the market for a couple of years, and are now expanding to an international market. 
They are generating revenue, as the model also suggests, and they have few or no 
design alternatives.
The developed model is based on the hypothesis that “product development phases 
and the business development phases of a start-up that is product-oriented cohere 
with one another”. It was conceptually tested using the information about the case 
start-ups. For four case start-ups: Hacktus, CRR Denmark, FiveMoreMinutes and 
DropBucket, the developed model seemed to be coherent with the actual situation of 
the case start-ups. However, the developed model was not consistent with the current 
situations of Amino Wool and AA Water, which had fewer design alternatives than the 
model suggested. This challenges the underlying hypothesis, but when investigating 
closer, it seems that the mismatch between theory and practice occurs, because Amino 
Wool and AA Water cannot be categorised as product-oriented start-ups, but rather 
technology-based. The other four case start-ups are product-oriented, and the model 
were consistent with their current situations. The implications for the developed model 
is, that it is not well suited for start-ups that are not product-oriented. Service-oriented 
start-ups, for instance, would also not fit into the developed model. 
The underlying hypothesis has not been properly validated by testing the developed 
model, which builds on the hypothesis, but for the remainder of the thesis, the hypoth-
esis is assumed to be valid, in order to simplify the later application of ecodesign, PSS 
and LCA approaches, which have primarily been developed with product developers 
in mind.
6.3 Discussion of the case study
Reliability
To ensure scientific reproducibility the following steps were taken:
1. The case study followed a systematic approach by applying the methods presented 
in the research method section
2. Data was collected while trying to avoid researcher bias or priming of inter-
viewees by using the tools from the co-creation methodology, and by considering 
the wording of the semi-structured interview guide
3. Data was collected systematically by having documenting co-creation playbooks 
and semi-structured interview guides
4. The collected data was analysed in a systematic manner using the Grounded 
Theory Method and later translated to requirements applying the Axiomatic 
Design Method
==PAGE:91==
75
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
5. That case companies was selected in a manner that would represent reality 
by categorising the case companies on relevant parameters to ensure a broad 
representation 
Validity
A critical point in ensuring data validity is the sampling method. The empirical study 
was conducted as a qualitative case study with 6 companies. The relatively low number 
of case companies lowers the validity of the research. An alternative approach that 
could have ensured a higher validity could have been a quantitative, systematic study. 
However, in the scope of the project this was estimated not to be possible. On the other 
hand, the qualitative case study allowed the researchers to use co-creation methods to 
develop and later test the solutions with the case companies, which enabled a deep 
understanding of the users and the situation, in turn leading to more valid results. 
The insights obtained from the case study about start-ups, small businesses and 
their needs regarding sustainability practices were coherent with the conclusions of 
the literature review.   
Case sampling
One of the challenges in the project was selection or locating of case companies. The 
biggest constraint in the project was a very limited time-frame of the project of only 
five month. This naturally limited the amount of how many case companies could be 
involved in the project. This also meant that the case companies needed to be involved 
earliest possible in the project to ensure that it was possible to plan their involvement 
in the project. 
Another major challenge was finding case companies that was representative. Early 
in the project several of the case companies was located through the researcher 
contact. However, it was found that it was necessary to ensure a broader representation 
and thus a few extra case companies was found by through MUDP a environmental 
sustainability fund. 
Co-creation as a method
Co-Creation was chosen as method since the methodology offers an approach that 
enables to activate the users in the development and utilise their “expert” knowledge. 
The methods especially enables the user participate with tacit knowledge, which is 
often a challenge in more traditional interview/development methods. 
In general the use of Co-Creation as a method was found to be beneficial to the 
project. However, in some cases the use of Co-Creation was found to be difficult 
or less applicable. One example was the amount of participants in the sessions. In 
literature many examples and methods of Co-Creation are made for  larger group of 
participant (5 or more). In this project many the sessions was with 1-3 participants. In 
the sessions with only one participant it became clear that some aspects didn’t work as 
well as with the larger groups. Although no specific rule apply to a minimum number 
of participants in a Co-Creation session many Co-Creation methods also rely on using 
the interaction between the participants as a key element to achieve results. This was 
also the reasoning for doing some of the interviews in a more traditional manor. 
Despite the challenges it was estimated that it did not affect the results. 
Coding the data
The use of coding as the method for interpreting results from interview was chosen 
since it is a useful method to translate qualitative results in a systematic and inductive 
manor. This also means that the needs expressed by the case companies could be 
translated with minimal researcher influence on the results. 
The fact that the team skipped a step, and translated “in vivo” codes directly to 
==PAGE:92==
76
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
needs may have affected the width of the analysis, because the coding became very 
focussed on identifying need and constraints rather than obtaining a broad range of 
codes and then consciously discarding the less relevant codes. 
As mentioned in the result section this meant that the result became very literal to 
the actual statements and sometimes did not take into account the underlying meaning. 
Going through an additional step - like in the analysis of the FiveMoreMinutes inter-
view - would have provided a more sound picture of the complex situation, and might 
have opened up the solution space. On the other hand, it would have been a very time 
consuming process that could have led to a list of codes that could be hard to translate 
into needs. Given the well-defined problem definition and the limited scope of this 
thesis, this consideration is assessed to have little impact on the validity on the concept 
to-be-developed.   
==PAGE:93==
77
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration |  Empirical study
6.4 Conclusion
In this chapter, the approach to and the conduction of the empirical study involving 
six case start-ups was described and interpreted. The result is a list of user needs, 
that will be used to ensure that the developed services are grounded in user needs, 
and general insights about the case start-ups. The general insights were similar to the 
conclusions of the literature reviews; i.e. that start-ups lack knowledge, resources and 
data. Two additional insights were that the several case start-ups expressed the need 
for concept validation in terms of sustainability, and holistic advice on sustainability 
practices.
Combining models of product development with models of start-up development, 
a hypothesis was established, namely that; “Product development phases and the 
business development phases of a start-up that is product-oriented cohere with one 
another”. This led to the conceptual development of a model which combines start-up 
development phases with funding and adaptability. The model was tested by mapping 
the six case start-ups in the model according to their business development phase, and 
comparing the model’s output of funding and adaptability with the situation of the 
case start-ups in practice. The test showed that the model works well product-oriented 
start-ups, but for e.g. technology-driven or service-oriented start-ups, it would not be 
consistent with reality. For the sake of simplicity, the above hypothesis is assumed to be 
valid in the rest of the thesis, to ease the later application of sustainability approaches 
like ecodesign, PSS and LCA in a start-up context.     
==PAGE:94==
78
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Identifying the gap
In this chapter, the insights obtained from both the literature reviews and the case 
study are consolidated and used to specify the aim of the research project by using 
the reference model and the initial impact model, both from Blessing & Chakrabarti 
(2009). The reference model is used to define 12 factors, and based on these factors, 
the focus of the impact model is six factors of the gap between NIRAS and start-ups, 
that the service to-be-developed should particularly address.
System modelling
The reference model is a model that graphically represents factors that affect the gap 
between sustainability specialist and product-oriented start-ups motivated to sustain-
ability practices. In the model, a factor consists of an attribute and an element; e.g. 
quality (attribute) of product (element). This means that the factors must be formu-
lated in a neutral manner, and then the value of the attribute (positive, negative or 
neutral) describes the situation. The factors may be connected with causal links, if the 
attribute of one element influences the attribute of another element, or non-causal 
links, if two factors are related, but do not influence one another. The most important 
factors are the key factors and the success factors. The success factors are the factors 
at the end of the cause-effect chain (constituted by the causal links), and they are the 
factors that improve as a result of introducing a successful solution. The key factors 
are the factors that influence the other factors, hence are at the beginning of the 
cause-effect chain, and these are the factors that a solution should address directly. The 
model can be further explored by consulting section 2.4.1 in the DRM-methodology 
(Blessing & Chakrabarti, 2009). 
Initial gap factors
The foundation of the reference model stems from the Research Clarification stage, 
where six initial factors were identified from initial research and interviews with 
NIRAS specialists, shown in Table 13.
The approach to developing the reference model was to place the above six factors 
on a table, in order to distribute the 25 needs and 10 constraints obtained from the 
case study, on the factors. This structure can be seen in Appendix 22. As expected, it 
was necessary to add factors as the initial ones did not suffice. The work in progress 
model is shown in Appendix 23. In addition to the needs and design constraints, the 
conclusions from the literature reviews presented in Figure 20 and Figure 21 and the 
conclusions from the case study presented in Figure 28 were added in the reference 
model as factors and linked. The process is discussed in the following section, and the 
reference model is depicted in Figure 35.
7.1

found by consulting the letters next to each link and the legend in the top right corner. 
An example is the causal link between ‘Amount of resources’, which is ‘low’ indicated 
with a minus sign. This then affects the ‘Degree of holistic overview’ to also be low. 
This link was established based on the case study, indicated  with a ‘C’. Some links are 
also assumed (‘A’), particularly between the factors that are at the end of the cause-ef-

==PAGE:96==
80
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Identifying the gap
fect chain. Two factors can also affect a third factor in combination, i.e. the effect on 
the third factors occurs only, when both factors and the current value of their attribute 
apply. This is illustrated by connecting two links close to the arrowheads, as can be 
seen around the factor ‘Suitability of offering’.
Amount of resources
From the literature review and the empirical study, it is clear that start-ups have very 
scarce resources, both with regards to economy, people and time. Before launching a 
product or a service, they are dependant on funding, investors or personal capital, and 
some even have other jobs or are studying besides their start-up-work. This means 
that there is almost never time for activities that are not directly related to generating 
a positive cash flow.
Degree of sustainability knowledge and experience
The limited amount of resources affects the time spent on acquiring new knowledge 
and skills. Several case start-ups mentioned that they feel unknowledgeable within 
environmental sustainability, and the literature review supported that observation. 
The research clarification stage also indicated that environmental sustainability is very 
widely used term, and that the meaning it covers is ill-defined and fluffy for non-spe-
cialists within the field. 
Degree of holistic perspective
The literature review pointed to the fact that due to few employees, the people behind 
a start-up will have a wide range of responsibilities. At the same time, some case 
start-ups expressed that they are often submerged into one activity at a time, which 
makes it hard to retain a holistic perspective on the activities and direction of the 
business. 
Visibility of benefits (of sustainability consultancy)
The literature review led to the conclusion, that as a result of SMEs’ limited knowl-
edge about sustainability and the ‘fluffiness’ of the term, the SMEs find it hard to see 
the benefits of implementing sustainability practices - in this case it could interpreted 
as finding it hard to see the benefits of a paid sustainability consultancy service. The 
same conclusion was supported by the case start-ups that found it difficult to see 
the benefits of NIRAS’ current offerings at the current price. The identified needs 
are among others that start-ups want to utilise sustainability in terms of positioning 
of product on market, but this need is not currently met by NIRAS’ offerings. It is 
assumed that the lack of a holistic perspective on the direction of the business also 
makes the benefits of sustainability consultancy less visible, because it may not pay off 
immediately in terms of e.g. increased revenue.  
Satisfaction of targeted customers
When the visibility of benefits of NIRAS’ current offerings is low, that will also affect 
the satisfaction of the start-ups that have actually received a service from NIRAS. 
This is partly assumed based on that a start-up - having spent scarce money on costly 
consultancy services - will be disappointed, if there are no clear benefits. In the case 
of Really, who has received a service from NIRAS, they found that the services were 
more complex than necessary, and the results were hard to utilise. 
Number of new target customers
The aim for NIRAS is to enter a new market of sustainability consultancy for start-ups. 
It is assumed that since the benefits of current NIRAS offerings are not visible to 
start-ups, they will acquire few customers in this new market. In addition to that, the 
satisfaction of prior customers within the new market (Really) is low, and that will 
contribute to even fewer new target customers.
==PAGE:97==
81
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Identifying the gap
Data availability
The literature review clearly showed that SMEs lack data or data collection methods to 
perform comprehensive environmental assessments. Many business also lack knowl-
edge about their own product and production, especially in the early development 
phases, presumably due to a high level of uncertainty.
Complexity of offerings
The current offerings of NIRAS’ Environmental footprint are comprehensive envi-
ronmental assessments, i.e. offerings of high complexity. This is because the current 
customers are primarily larger corporations and municipalities. In combination with 
the low data availability of start-ups, the suitability of NIRAS’ current offerings for 
start-ups is low.
Level of uncertainty
Collinson & Gregson (2003) emphasise that start-ups experience high levels of uncer-
tainty, especially in the early business development phases. That does not go well with 
highly complex offerings, where much effort is put into the comprehensive assessment 
of a single product’s life cycle, if the start-up changes direction soon after or decides 
on a different concept. As a result, the suitability of NIRAS’ current offerings for 
start-ups is low.    
Suitability of (NIRAS) offerings for start-ups
The low suitability of NIRAS’ current offerings for start-ups is also due to the from 
literature and case study obtained insight that start-ups have scarce economic resources; 
costly offerings do not accommodate that scarcity. It is assumed that this also affects 
how beneficial, NIRAS’ offerings are perceived, i.e. as relatively high investments with 
low returns. This results in a low visibility of benefits.   
Profitability of start-ups as customers
NIRAS’ current offerings are more complex - hence costly - than what is necessary for 
start-ups, and scarce economic resources of start-ups mean that they will not buy large 
services, where the profit margin for NIRAS is likely higher. Combined, these factors 
lead to that start-ups are currently not profitable customers for NIRAS. This issue was 
emphasised by several of the NIRAS specialists that were interviewed. 
Viability of business case
The business case is that NIRAS should provide services to start-ups within environ-
mental consultancy. The current potential of that business case is assumed to be low, 
seeing that the profitability of that type of customer would be low, and there would 
be few of them.

 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Identifying the gap
7.2 
Success and key factors
Figure 35 helps to create an overview of the factors contributing to the gap that are 
identified between start-ups and sustainability specialists, and the correlation between 
those factors. As mentioned, the most important factors are the success factors and 
the key factors. There are both success factors and measurable success factors, and 
these are at the end of the cause-effect chain.
The measurable success factors are:
 
+ Profitability of start-ups as customers
 
+ Number of new target customers
 
+ Satisfaction of new target customers
The success factors are:
 
+ To reduce the complexity of NIRAS’ offerings for start-ups
 
+ To propose a solution which is clearly linked to the identified needs of start-ups
 
+ In order to improve these, the key factors that are at the beginning of the 
cause-effect chain should be addressed directly. 
The key factors are:
 
+ Amount of start-up resources
 
+ Complexity of NIRAS offerings 
 
+ Level of uncertainty within start-ups
 
7.3 
Potential solutions to the gap
In the initial impact model, shown in Figure 36, potential solutions to the gap and their 
influence on the gap factors are explored. The inspiration for the potential solutions 
stems from the list of identified needs in Table 9, the literature review conclusions, 
Figure 20 and Figure 21, and from the interviews with NIRAS specialists. 
In the model shown in Figure 36, the potential solutions are illustrated with hexagons. 
Many of the links are now assumed (‘A’), as these are assumptions about how the 
change of on factor will affect another factors.  
Amount of resources: Assist with funding
The contact person Nynne from NIRAS Environmental Footprint, and NIRAS’ 
Morten Pedersen, who works with international funding consortia, suggested that a 
way to overcome the challenge of start-ups’ scarce economic resources could be for 
NIRAS to assist with the funding process on the condition of being hired to deliver a 
consultancy service to the start-up.  By doing so, the economic resources of start-ups 
will no longer affect any other factors than the ‘Profitability of start-ups as customers’, 
which as a results will be high. 
Complexity of offerings: Simple offerings
It is assumed that by lowering the complexity of NIRAS’ current offerings - and 
hence the cost of delivering them - will lead to a higher ‘Profitability of start-ups as 
customers’. Simpler offerings is also assumed to increase the ‘Suitability of (NIRAS) 
offerings for start-ups’, since these are customer with a high level of uncertainty 
and little data about their product and production, requiring far less comprehensive 
services than large corporations or municipalities.     
==PAGE:101==
85
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Identifying the gap
Level of uncertainty: Concept validation
Collinson & Gregson (2003) found that validation of concepts is a suitable way to 
address the high levels of uncertainty in start-ups. 
However, it is assessed by the thesis team that providing concept validation will not 
solve the issue of high uncertainty as that is the nature of the start-ups, but concept 
validation is a suitable offering in environments with high levels of uncertainty. 
In addition to addressing the key factors, the identified list of  needs suggested that the factors ‘Degree 
of  holistic overview’ and ‘Degree of  sustainability knowledge and experience’ should also be addressed. 
Furthermore, from the literature review, inspiration was found on how to increase the ‘Suitability of  
offerings for start-ups’. 
Suitability of offerings for start-ups: Service adapted to start-up 
development phase 
Oxborrow & Brindley (2011) propose a framework, emphasising that start-ups have 
different drivers, hence needs, for engaging in sustainability practices depending on 
which phase, they are in. By taking this into consideration in a solution concept, 
NIRAS will be able to increase the ‘Suitability of (their) offerings for start-ups’.
Degree of holistic perspective: Holistic sustainability advice
Multiple case start-ups expressed the need of some general advice with regards to 
sustainability practices. By offering holistic sustainability advice, it is assumed that 
start-ups will increase their holistic perspective on their business, despite their ubiq-
uitous people- and time-wise scarcity. This will in turn increase the visibility of the 
benefits, seeing that ‘Suitability of offerings for start-ups’ has also been increased.
Degree of sustainability knowledge and experience: Educational 
offering
Finally, a way of addressing the low degree of sustainability knowledge and experi-
ence could be to provide educational offerings. From several of the studies obtained 
on start-up characteristics, it is clear that the start-up manager or owner has a great 
influence on the direction of the business. In combination with an increased degree 
of sustainability knowledge and experience, this would increase the ‘Visibility of the 
benefits’ (of NIRAS offerings). 
7.3.1 
Impact model summary
By increasing the ‘Visibility of benefits’, through improvement of other factors, it 
is assumed that the result will be a higher customer acquisition, thus increasing the 
‘Number of new target customers’. At the same time, the ‘Satisfaction of target 
customers’ would also increase, again leading to an increased ‘Number of new target 
customers’. This chain of improved factors is conditioned by an improved ‘Suita-
bility of offerings for start-ups’, i.e. offerings that are more aligned with the needs of 
start-ups. Additionally, the ‘Profitability of start-ups as customers’ will be increased 
due to less complex (costly) offerings and funding assistance that can potentially help 
increase economic resources of the start-ups. 
By increasing both the ‘Number of new target customers’ and the ‘Profitability of 
start-ups as customers’ the combined effect will be an increase in the ‘Viability of 
business case’.
This purpose of the initial impact model was to illustrate the desired effect of poten-
tial solutions that are not yet detailed. The potential solutions presented in the model 
are very specific ways of addressing the gap - hence narrowing the solution space. In 
the next stage, Concept development, the process will be diverging, opening up the 
solution space.
==PAGE:102==
86
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Identifying the gap
7.4 Conclusion
In Chapter 7, the insights gathered during the literature review and empirical case 
study, were combined and consolidated in order to specify the aim of the research 
project. As such, the reference model was used to determine 12 factors contributing 
to the gap - based on these factors an impact model was used to define which factors 
to focus on - hence which factors that would lead to a positive chain of reactions in 
the system. The processes led to the identification key factors, that the final solution 
should address, in order to bridge the gap between start-ups and sustainability consult-
ants. The factors are as follows:
 
+ Amount of resources
 
+ Complexity of offerings
 
+ Level of uncertainty 
 
+ Suitability of offerings for start-ups
 
+ Degree of holistic perspective
 
+ Degree of sustainability knowledge and experience
These six factors will serve as the foundation of the Concept development.
==PAGE:103==
87
 Work Package 2: Needs Exploration | Identifying the gap
The purpose of Work Package 2 was to explore the needs, characteristics, barriers of 
and drivers to sustainability, by establishing an in-depth understanding of the target 
users - in this case start-ups and small businesses. The research was conducted with the 
purpose of answering the two first research questions:
RQ1: What are the characteristics and needs of product-oriented start-ups and 
small businesses wanting to become more sustainable?
RQ2: What are the factors contributing to the gap between sustainability-driven 
start-ups & small businesses and sustainability consultants?
In Chapter 5 existing literature was reviewed in order to collect other researchers 
insights, on the two research questions. Each systematic literature search - that is for 
RQ1 and RQ2 - resulted in a list of insights, emphasising the characteristics, needs, 
barrier and drivers applicable to start-ups seeking an environmentally sustainable busi-
ness. Furthermore, the literature indicated that start-ups and SMEs find it difficult 
to work with environmental sustainability and that there exist a gap between expert 
knowledge and start-up application of sustainability principles. 
Chapter 6 introduced and documented the empirical and qualitative study, conducted 
by the team in collaboration with 6, specifically chosen, case start-ups. The insights 
gathered during the empirical study indicated many similarities with the insights gath-
ered from the literature review - namely the following: start-ups have limited resources, 
start-ups lack sustainability knowledge and start-ups lack necessary data to assess 
sustainability.
Furthermore, the combined insights from the case study and the literature review, 
indicated that start-ups need external expert guidance and advice, in order to establish 
the holistic view necessary to implement and improve sustainability in their businesses.
Chapter 7 sought to combine and consolidate the insights gathered in Chapter 5 and 
Chapter 6, to establish the foundation for the upcoming concept development process. 
The reference model was used to visually represent the insights and understandings, 
gathered in the empirical study and the literature review. The impact model was used to 
identify the critical factors - which is solved - will positively affect the success factors. 
This resulted in the identification of six key factors, that the final solution should 
solve, in order to bridge the gap between sustainability consultants and start-ups. The 
key factors are:
 
+ Complexity of offerings
 
+ Suitability of offerings for start-ups
 
+ Level of uncertainty
 
+ Amount of resources
 
+ Degree of holistic perspective
 
+ Degree of sustainability knowledge and experience

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Task clarification
In this chapter, the aim is to further define, how to target potential solutions to the 
identified gap. This is achieved by applying axiomatic design principles to translate 
the identified user needs into solution requirements. The impact potential is clarified 
by distinguishing between internal and external gap factors, after which the internal 
(NIRAS) factors are explored. Finally, a number of key performance indicators for the 
concept to-be-developed are established in collaboration with NIRAS.
8.1 Axiomatic design method and requirement setting
To ensure a structured and well-backed development phase, the resulting needs from 
the case study must be translated into requirements for the final concept. This process 
of translating the user needs into precise and measurable requirements helps to ensure 
a direct line between the needs and the final concept. With the purpose of ensuring 
this process two existing methods are combined and used as inspiration and reference. 
These methods were introduced during the course: 41020 Product Analysis and 
Redesign, at DTU 2013. From this course Mary Kathryn Thompson introduced a 
new MIT inspired interpretation of the well-known Axiomatic Design method by 
N. P. Suh (Suh, 2001), that combines the traditional product specification (Ulrich & 
Eppinger, 2012) with the Axiomatic Design theory. The original design theory by Suh 
(2001) emphasizes two aspects of product development: What is needed and how it is 
achieved. In light of this, the product designer tries to achieve the perfect solution by 
interacting with both aspects. This sequence is classified in the Figure 37.
The user needs are limited to the customer domain before translated into functional 
and measurable requirements in the functional domain. The functional requirements 
should if possible be formulated in engineering words corresponding to what is tried 
solved. In this project, the method is used to generate requirements for the solution 
space and not to define the design parameters or design process. Therefore, the two 
first steps in Figure 38, of the process is highlighted in blue , indicating that the other 
two steps of the process is neglected.
For this exact process Mary Kathryn Thompson suggests a modified and more struc-

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Task clarification
tured theory on how to tackle the translation of needs into requirements (Thompson, 
2013).  This process consist of four structured steps towards a final requirements 
specification. The following Figure 38, shows the requirement specification steps.
The key stakeholders and case companies are introduced and defined in Chapter 3 
followed by a background and stakeholder research. The case companies’ needs are 
defined in this work package, in Chapter 7. All three steps of the requirement spec-
ification bolong in the Customer Domain in Axiomatic Design and are considered 
adequate, hence why only the fourth step is highlighted in blue. The translation from 
needs to requirements is not conducted until the next section, in Chapter 8.
The following section outlines the process of translating the customers needs into 
functional requirements, ensuring the essence of the customers needs by applying a 
systematic approach. The requirement specification is generated via a combination 
of the Axiomatic Design method (Suh, 2001) and the traditional Product Specifica-
tion method (Ulrich & Eppinger, 2012), customised by Mary Kathryn Thompson 
(Thompson, 2013), MIT as described earlier in the methodology section.
Based on the needs defined in Chapter 6, a requirement specification is generated 
by translating the needs into requirements for the solution space, in which the final 
solution lies. The final list of needs is a result of multiple processes outlined by Mary 
Kathryn Thompson in the requirement specification steps. Besides identifying stake-
holders and conducting background and stakeholder research, the needs have been 
derived from transcribing co-creation - and interview sessions, followed by a coding 
process ensuring that the customer needs represent the customer insights as much as 
possible. By doing so, the final needs can be translated directly into requirements and 
still represent the same customer insights.
Since the method presented by Thomason (2003) only is used to translate the needs 
into a requirements specification, this means moving from the concept phase (step 
1-3) into the product phase (step 4). The fourth step in the requirement specification 
(Thompson, 2013) is the process of defining the requirements for the product spec-
ification - translation of needs. The needs are translated into more measurable and 
functional requirements for the final solution. This systematic approach enables the 
requirements to be detached from the individual stakeholders and users. The purpose 
of systematically translating the needs into a requirement specification, is to build a 
solid foundation for the evaluation process, ensuring that the solution is compared 
with actual user requirements and not with criterias developed by the designers (Ulrich 
& Eppinger, 2012). Figure 39 illustrates an example of how customer needs are trans-
lated into requirements.
As the figure above illustrates, the requirement specification is generated from trans-
lating all the needs into detached requirements that individually represent a function 
that the final solution needs to comprehend, in order to fully solve the needs from the 
users. All 25 needs were translated with the same process. Importantly to mention, some 
needs generate multiple requirements as well as some requirements represent multiple 
needs. The following Figure 40 documents the process of translating all the needs.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Task clarification
8.2 Clarifying the impact potential 
In the Needs exploration stage, it was possible to identify six key factors and theirs 
values (in parentheses) to address in order to increase the overall viability of the busi-
ness case of NIRAS targeting start-ups, namely:
1. Amount of start-up resources (low)
2. Level of uncertainty within start-ups (high)
3. Degree of sustainability knowledge and experience of start-ups (low)
4. Degree of holistic perspective of start-ups (linked with ‘Visibility of benefits’ 
and ‘Degree of sustainability knowledge and experience’) (low)
5. Complexity of NIRAS offerings (high) 
6. Suitability of NIRAS offerings for start-ups (low) 
Recalling the reference model or initial impact model in Figure 35 and Figure 36, the 
first four factors concern the start-ups, and the last two factors are internal NIRAS 
factors. The value of the four start-up factors are caused by external conditions, over 
which NIRAS has little control. However, NIRAS may still influence the start-ups 
factors with the content of their offerings. Consequently, the format of the service 
to-be-developed in this thesis should primarily address the internal NIRAS factors, 
whereas the content of the service to-be-developed should address the start-up 
factors. The internal NIRAS factors are further explored in the following.
8.2.1 Complexity of NIRAS offerings
The current offerings of NIRAS’ Environmental footprint are comprehensive envi-
ronmental assessments, i.e. offerings of high complexity. This is because the current 
customers are primarily larger corporations and municipalities. In combination with 
the low data availability of start-ups, the suitability of NIRAS’ current offerings for 
start-ups is low. In order to reduce the level of complexity, the activities and processes 
must be mapped (Hvam et al., 2008), and in a service design, an appropriate tool for 
mapping service activities is the service blueprint (Shostack, 1984). 
Service blueprint analysis
A service includes different customer touchpoints and deliverables, which require 
both ‘front-stage’, ‘back-stage’ and supporting activities performed by NIRAS. The 
‘front-stage’ activities describe all the activities performed by NIRAS Environmental 
footprint that are visible to the customers, whereas the ‘back-stage’ activities are the 
activities performed by NIRAS Environmental footprint behind the line of visibility. 
The supporting processes can be people, systems and things that support NIRAS Envi-
ronmental footprint in their activities. Finally, there will also be ‘physical evidence’ for 
all customer touchpoints and deliverables. These are all parts of the service blueprint, 
which is illustrated in Figure 41, and in Appendix 25. The underlying analysis is based 
on interviews with NIRAS specialists and quotes from earlier projects performed by 
NIRAS Environmental footprint. The blueprint has been approved by NIRAS after 
completion to verify that it shows a correct image of the activities. 
In the service blueprint, there are several activities which require communication back 
and forth between NIRAS and the client. This is logical for customised consultancy 
services in a large project with high stakes, but for the less complex start-ups, the 
process could be simplified to reduce the costs. An example is the number of activities 
that are required before reaching an agreement with a customer. Figure 42 emphasises 
the range of activities NIRAS Environmental Footprint performs, even before the 
customers has committed to a service.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Task clarification
Figure 43 shows how NIRAS Environmental Footprint communicates the time distri-
bution on the different activities to their customers, and price corresponds with time 
consumption in a one to one ratio.
However, there are other expenses and activities associated with projects that the 
customers pay for, such as project establishment costs, customer acquisition activities, 
legal/insurance fees, expenses for hardware and software etc. The actual correlation 
between cost and time will thus be closer to the depiction in Figure 44. Consequently, 
NIRAS’ services need to have a certain duration in order to pay off, and considering 
the scarce resources of start-ups, this contributes to the gap between NIRAS and 
start-ups.
For the next step - ideation - overall categories from the service blueprint were used 
as starting point for a new concept generation, to ensure that essential elements in a 
consultancy service is not omitted, but also to challenge that some activities may be 
redundant and non-value adding in services targeted at start-ups.   
8.2.2 Suitability of NIRAS offerings for start-ups
The second internal factor to address is the currently low suitability of the NIRAS 
offerings for start-ups. The suitability is low for three identified reasons:
 
+ Complex NIRAS offerings are not well suited considering the low data availa-
bility of start-ups
 
+ Complex NIRAS offerings are unnecessary in the uncertain environment of 
start-ups 
 
+ Complex, hence costly, NIRAS offerings do not accommodate the economic 
scarcity of start-ups

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Task clarification
Oxborrow & Brindley (2011) suggest to differentiate start-ups based on which devel-
opment phase, they are in, to better accommodate their needs with regards to envi-
ronmental sustainability. The model shown in Figure 46 created from a combination 
of models is thus revisited, to identify a ‘sweet spot’ in the development phases, where 
sustainability consultancy could potentially have the most impact. The majority of the 
environmental impacts of a product is determined in the early product development 
phases, before product launch (McAloone & Tan, 2005). By targeting start-ups in 
these phases, NIRAS could create maximum positive impact by guiding the start-ups 
in the right direction and enabling them to consider the environmental impacts of their 
product in a holistic manner. Consequently, the primary target group will be start-ups 
in the concepting (PDP: Concept development), committing (PDP: System-level-de-
sign and detail design), validating (PDP: Testing and refinements) or scaling (PDP: 
Production ramp-up) phases. 
Furthermore, it could be concluded from the literature review and the case study that 
start-ups fail to see the benefits of sustainability consultancy, especially at high prices. 
In the following concept generation, there are three ways of accommodating that 
imbalance between visibility of benefits and price, presented in Figure 45. 
The first option is to increase the visibility of benefits, i.e. making the offerings more 
relevant and attractive to start-ups. The second option is to reduce the price, and thus 
meet the start-ups at their level of expectation. The third option is the in-between 
strategy; to reduce the cost and increase the visibility of benefits, but still keeping 
them in balance. Of course, seen from a customer point of view, there is also the 
fourth option of reducing the price and increasing the visibility of benefits, so the 
latter weighs the most. However, from a business perspective, the two should be in 
balance - else NIRAS would miss out on revenue. These two first strategies are used as 
the starting point for the concept generation in the following chapter.

8.3 Existing solutions
A quick scan for existing solutions to the identified gap factors was performed to 
ensure the relevance of developing a proposed solution, as shown in Table 14. The 
purpose was also to delimit the focus of the concept generation, if adequate solutions 
to a gap already exists or to be inspired by them.  
The factors ‘Suitable (sustainability consultancy) offerings for start-ups’, ‘Holistic 
perspective’ and ‘Reducing complexity of consultancy services’ seem unsolved. There 
are several means to obtaining ‘Sustainability knowledge’, which could serve as inspi-
ration in the concept development. 
‘Amount of resources’ and ‘High levels of uncertainty within start-ups’ are both being 
addressed by existing solutions, but they do not seem to be adequately addressed, 
seeing that the case study indicated otherwise.
8.4 Co-creation with client: Establishing KPIs
Before the ideation process, a co-creation session with Rune and Nynne from NIRAS 
was conducted with focus on enabling NIRAS to bring ideas and thoughts to the 
process. Since the project initially was generated by NIRAS, is was necessary to include 
KPI’s from NIRAS, ensuring that the solution can actually be implemented and used 
by the organisation. There were two main purposes behind the co-creation session; one 
to ensure that NIRAS was able to contribute to the ideation process, two to test with 
NIRAS whether or not the aim of the project was correct, hence ensuring that NIRAS 
gained a feeling of being co-creators of the final solution and thereby ensuring that 
they would want to use the solution. Furthermore the co-creation session enabled the 
authors to gain direct and concrete insights on KPIs from NIRAS for the successful 
solution.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Task clarification
The general theme of the co-creation session was focused on how NIRAS could 
become more visible to start-ups, both in terms of how to visualise the benefits of 
their offerings as well as how to brand NIRAS towards start-ups and become more 
known in appropriate communities. The session was steered in assistance with the 
co-creation playbook, generated before the session, providing an overview of the 
activities. The playbook can be seen in Appendix 26, indicating the different steps of 
the session as well as the needed material, like templates and post its.
Conducting the co-creation session
The co-creation session was scheduled to be 1.5 hours, with multiple different creative 
and theoretical exercises. Before beginning the co-creation session, it was necessary 
to set the mood and get the participants to feel comfortable. Therefore, the first step 
of the session was a simple and not relevant question, to get the participants to talk 
and relax in the environment. To get the participants to think creatively during the 
session, it was crucial to get them in the right mood and the following exercise forced 
the participants to draw funny logos and come up with slogans. This kind of exercise 
gets the participant to laugh and smile, hence creating a comfortable space to think 
creatively in, were no ideas can be rejected or not valid.
During the reminding time of the session the participants ideated on how to get in 
contact with start-ups, as well as how to visualise the benefits of the knowledge and 
processes that NIRAS currently have. Furthermore the participants had to generate 
offers and explain in detail how the different parts of the offerings could be changes/
removed, in order to generate new prices. During the workshop Rune and Nynne could 
follow the development of activities via the co-creation slideshow, also indicating the 
descriptions for each activity. The co-creation slideshow can be seen in Appendix 27. 
Getting insights from Rune and Nynne on the visibility of offerings and their benefits, 
was chosen as main focus of the co-creation session for two reasons. Firstly because 
the co-creation session was before the ideation process, hence the team wanted to get 
insights to bring forward to ideation and secondly because the team had experienced 
from the case start-ups that “becoming visible to start-ups” and” visualising the bene-
fits of offerings” was two major reasons why start-ups did not already know NIRAS.
Results from the co-creation session
The co-creation session clearly indicated that Rune and Nynne had a good under-
standing of what was necessary to do in order to become more visible towards 
start-ups and in start-up communities. They both believed that NIRAS had to become 
more visible at universities and at incubators. Knowing this underlined to the team 
that the reason why NIRAS is not already more visible, was not because they did 
not know what to do, they simply lack the right approach and material. Furthermore 
the session indicated to the team that the current NIRAS offerings might be too 
complex compared to the needs of the start-ups, hence the price of the offerings are 
not aligned with the start-ups. In order to simplify the offerings the sessions revealed 
that NIRAS lack templates and standard processes. The reason behind this was made 
clear. NIRAS’ current clients are large companies and municipalities, meaning that 
the aim and extend of their offerings almost always are tailored to the complex issues 
that these clients have. essentially this means that NIRAS does not know how to 
solve the simple request that start-ups have, and therefore they do not know how to 
generate the right templates and standardised processes. The results of the co-creation 
workshop can be seen in Appendix 28,  showing both the templates with post its and 
the generated insights from the co-creation session.
==PAGE:118==
102
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Task clarification
Key performance indicators (KPIs) for the good solution
As a last part of the co-creation session, after the participants had been creative and 
gained insight on some of the challenges of this project, Rune and Nynne were asked 
to generate KPIs for the good solution, hence indicating how the final solution could 
be implementable at NIRAS as well as successful. This exercise helped to create 8 
important indicators, as seen below. Each KPI is rated from 1-3 given their importance 
to NIRAS. The KPIs will later be used to screen and evaluate concepts as well as 
to evaluate the final solution in collaboration with the requirements from the case 
start-ups.
1. Little time required to implement - 3  
If the solution require too much time from NIRAS it is not realistic that it will 
be facilitated/implemented within the NIRAS organisation. 
2. Low economic investments necessary to implement - 2 
If the solution require large investments from NIRAS, it will not be imple-
mented. 
3. Utilise existing KSAs - 1 
If the solution is to be implementable, the required knowledge, skills and 
attitudes are not to extend what is already available in the NIRAS organisation. 
Reduce the time spent per customer - 3 
If the solution is to be successful, the price of the offerings have to be lowered. 
Since a part of the current price level is based on physical meetings, the amount 
of meetings have to be lowered as well. 
4. Assist with funding - 2 
If the solution is to be successful it has to include funding services to start-ups, 
since they have very limited resources.  
5. Provide results in a visual manner - 1 
If the solution is to be relevant for start-ups, the results of the offerings have 
to be presented in a visual manner, hence become more usable for the start-ups, 
when communicating and applying for funds.
6. Consider different start-up needs - 2 
If the solution is to be successful, the offerings need to include a broad range 
of start-ups needs, in order to become relevant for as many start-ups as 
possible. 
7. Increase awareness in start-up communities - 2 
If the solution is to be successful it has to increase the awareness of NIRAS 
in start-up communities, as well as clearly demonstrate the benefits of the 
offerings.
==PAGE:119==
103
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Task clarification
8.5 Conclusion
In Chapter 8, the problem and requirements were further specified, to ensure a solid 
foundation for the concept development. This included setting the requirements 
employing axiomatic design methods, clarifying the impact potential and co-devel-
oping KPIs with NIRAS. The requirement setting led to 46 requirements, which will 
be used to evaluate the final concept after the conceptualisation. Clarification of the 
impact potential led to the conclusion that the concept to developed should focus 
on reducing the complexity of and increasing the suitability of NIRAS’ offerings for 
start-ups in terms of price and visible benefits.   
A service blueprint analysis highlighted that several current NIRAS activities have 
potential to be optimised, removed or standardised, to reduce the complexity of 
NIRAS’ offerings. The chapter further concluded that an environmental sustainability 
consultancy for start-ups, should focus on start-ups in early development phases.
Finally, the co-development of KPIs with NIRAS resulted in 8 KPIs for the successful 
solution. The KPIs were used to screen the concepts during conceptualisation and will 
also be used to make a relative comparison of the final concept and NIRAS’ current 
offerings. The KPIs are as follows:
 
+ Little time required to implement
 
+ Low economic investments necessary to implement
 
+ Utilise existing KSAs
 
+ Reduce the time spent per customer
 
+ Assist with funding
 
+ Provide results in a visual manner
 
+ Consider different start-up needs
 
+ Increase awareness in start-up communities
==PAGE:120==
104
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Conceptualisation
This chapter presents the concept development process - from ideation to final 
concept - with the purpose of establishing a transparent overview of the development 
process.  The concept development process was conducted by the team following 
traditional methods like morphological chart and concept screening. The detailing 
and testing process was conducted in four iterative rounds, ensuring a comprehensive 
co-creation development of the final concept. Each testing outcome was analysed, 
interpreted and detailed, before used in the next test round.
Research methods 
During the concept generation process, a morphological chart (Cross, 2008, p. 137) 
was used to structure the overall categories of the ideation process. The categories 
in the morphological chart were formulated as “how might we” questions, which 
stemmed directly from the gap factors and the steps in the service blueprint.  The 
morphological chart was chosen because of the method’s ability to force developers 
to break down the functions of the desired solution and distribute the objectives into 
a matrix. Breaking down the desired solution enables developers to see and discover 
new combinations and configurations, which before might not have been visible. 
Structuring the concept generation this way ensured the inclusion of stakeholders, 
case start-ups and NIRAS, since each objective was based on insights from the analysis 
of the gap, user interactions and the NIRAS service blueprint. In overall this process 
helped to broaden up the solution space, while still ensuring a realistic and need-ori-
ented concept development process. The downside of the method is that the tight 
structure of the ideation phase, given the matrix setup, can mean that some of the 
“crazy” ideas which usually occur during a more loosen and chaotic setup, might not 
have been realised. 
To diverge up the solution space and create ideas in the above described ideation 
framework, each “how might we” question was solved with the brainstorm method 
(Cross, 2008, p 48). The brainstorm method was chosen to enable as much creativity 
as possible during the ideation phase. Brainstorming with “post its” was a good tool to 
bring both realistic and funny ideas to each category (“how might we”). 
The concept screening was based on “Concept Screening” by Ulrich & Eppinger 
(2015), adopted from the “Pugh concept selection” (Pugh, 1990). The objective of the 
screening stage is to narrow the number of concepts relatively quickly and to improve 
the concepts. Normally when using the screening method, each concept is scored in 
relation to a reference product (typically during a re-design process). But since there is 
no reference product in this thesis, each concepts were rated as having a negative (-), 
neutral (0) or positive (+) impact.
When conducting the testing and detailing rounds, co-creation principles was used to 
involve the participants. Since the first testing round focussed on co-developing the 
content of the service packages, co-creation techniques was used to promote physical 
artifacts, ensuring direct involvement from the participants. When conducting the test 
session it was thus important to allow the participants to express their opinions freely 
without controlling the environment more than necessary.
9.1 Ideation
The starting point of the ideation was the identified gap factors from the impact 
model in Chapter 7, with emphasis on the internal gap factor ‘complexity of NIRAS 
offerings’, by including the elements of the verified service blueprint. Thus, it was 
ensured that essential activities to deliver consultancy services were not omitted, and 
at the same being able to challenge those activities, with the purpose of reducing them. 
In the following section, the ideation categories are described. 
Chapter 9: Conceptualisation
==PAGE:121==
105
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Conceptualisation
Defining the categories
The morphological chart was used to generate ideas based on “how might we” func-
tions (IDEO, 2015). The functions were based on the two following aspects.
1. The gap factors (based on the literature review and the empirical study) 
a.  Each gap factor (being very high level) was divided into several functions 
that the concept should meet. See Table 15.
2. Reduce complexity (main focus area from the service blueprint)
b. High level categories from the service blueprint and a cost/time distribution 
were included as functions that the concept should meet. See Table 16.
Together, the elaborated gap factors and the service blueprint activities, led to 32 
functions, that the successful solution should meet. The 32 functions were formulated 
as “how might we”-questions allowing the team members to generate creative ideas, 
i.e. sub-solutions to each function.  The 32 functions can be seen below, indicating 
their origin to either the gap factors or the service blueprint. 
With the creation of functions it was possible to establish a structured and compre-
hensible overview of what easily could have been a very chaotic brainstorm process. 
Getting creative
Before beginning the brainstorming process, some rules and guidelines were estab-
lished with the purpose of controlling the creative process (Onarheim, 2013).
Guidelines:
 
+ No criticism, all ideas are seen as good ideas.
 
+ Every function has a time limit of 4 minutes.
 
+ Every idea must belong to one of the functions.
 
+ Generate as many ideas as possible by shifting post-its between participants.

During the brainstorm phase, the team members had focus on being informative and

participants had 30 seconds to explain the idea/thought behind each post-it. The idea-

with functions on the y-axes and sub-solution on the x-axes.
9.2 Concept generation
After generating ideas for the morphological chart, it was clear that some of the func-
tions overlapped a little, and it was necessary to look further at the structure of the 
morphological chart. 
The authors found that the overlap was not substantial enough to make any changes, 
but discovered instead that the order of the functions could be optimised in order to 
represent the actual activities in the service. For example, that ‘the solution should help 
NIRAS get in contact with clients’, was placed before ‘helping NIRAS with delivering 
results’, and so forth. The order was reorganised before the generation of concepts.
Specifying the concept themes
As the foundation for concept generation, relevant themes were formulated based 
on the empirical study and client KPIs. ‘Decrease cost/time’ was generated based on 
core needs from the case start-ups and ‘Easy to implement’ was generated based on 
of the KPI’s developed in collaboration with NIRAS. From the task clarification it 
was assessed that the start-up characteristic “limited financial resources” was twofold, 
indicating that customer satisfaction could be reached in two ways. Either by setting 
the right ‘price’ or by increasing ‘visibility of benefits’. This lead to the third theme 
‘increase value’. Furthermore, interviews and co-creation sessions with NIRAS high-

Generating the concepts
The morphological chart was printed in a large format allowing the team to get an 
overview of all the ideas produced during the ideation phase. With the perspective of 
each concept theme, different sub-solution were chosen, generating the concepts. The 
process of generating each concepts can be seen in Appendix 30, showing coloured 
marks representing each concept.

→ Concept 1 - Decrease cost/time is marked with purple ●
→ Concept 2 - Increase value is marked with green ●
→ Concept 3 - Easy to implement is marked with blue ● 
→ Concept 4 - The intelligent solution is marked with yellow ●
The concepts are separated and individually summarized in Appendix 31. Each 
concept is further described in more detail in Appendix 32
9.3 Concept screening
The four initial concepts were evaluated based on the concept screening method 
- described by Ulrich and Eppinger (2012). The method was used to screen the 
concepts, while still being at a low detail level, with the purpose of excluding two of 
the concepts. The selection criteria was based on the KPIs identified in collaboration 
with NIRAS. The scoring of each concept was based on the team members assess-
ment of each KPI. Normally, concepts are evaluated compared to a reference product, 
but since there was no reference product in this thesis, the scoring of the concepts 
was adopted and instead of “better than”or “worse than” the reference product, the 
concepts were rated as having a negative (-), neutral (0) or positive (+) impact on the 
KPIs. The results of the screening can be seen in Table 17.
As shown in Table 17, concept 1 and 3 had the highest net score, with a score of 4 
and 3 respectively. 
When investigating the scores, it was clear that the concepts scored high on different 
KPIs. Therefore, it was decided to combine the two concepts and test separate elements 
from each concepts before  merging the concepts (Ulrich & Eppinger, 2012 p. 152).

Updating the initial impact model 
The initial impact model from Figure 36 was updated to visualise the intended impact 
of the concepts 1 and 3, and it can be found in Figure 48. The overall elements of the 
concepts were added to the model in place of the ‘Potential solutions to the gap’ to 
show how they might solve the gap.
 
+ Predefined services & Standardised processes and templates
The needs of start-ups are much less complex than the needs of NIRAS’ current 
customers. Simpler needs can be accommodated by simpler, more generic offer-
ings, and simpler, more generic offerings are more likely to be delivered through 
predefined services with standardised processes and templates. Standard-
ising processes and services such as the specification process, makes it possible 
to reduce the amount of communication back and forth, as well as the number 
of physical meetings needed to provide a service. Standardised templates for e.g. 
results will reduce the time spent on reporting. Consequently, the offerings will 
become less complex and less costly, hence increasing the suitability of NIRAS’ 
offerings for start-ups.
 
+ List of relevant funds + ”How to”-guide
NIRAS could supply a list of  relevant funds and guide to applying for 
funding, with the aim of increasing the sustainability-driven start-ups’ chances 
of receiving funding. If the start-ups receive funding, it is assumed that they 
will be more willing to spend money on sustainability consultancy, hence the 
profitability of them as customers.    
 
+ 1st session is free, Presence in start-up communities & Promotional material 
First free sessions are very common within offerings for start-ups. A first free 
session in a start-up community would remove the barrier for the start-ups to 
get engaged with NIRAS. NIRAS could use the first free session to convince 
the start-ups of the importance of sustainability consultancy, by giving holistic 
sustainability advice, hence increasing the visibility of benefits from the start-ups’ 
point of view. To increase the awareness of the NIRAS within start-up commu-
nities, the concepts 1 and 3 employ promotional material like posters and flyers.
 
 
+ Ask customers to evaluate post services
The continuously increase their services, NIRAS should ask their customers to 
evaluate after the completion of a service.

9.4 Initial concept detailing
Before testing and detailing the concepts, a joint service blueprint for concept 1 and 
3 had to be created, visualising the fundamental activities in the concepts. The new 
service blueprint was based on  an analysis of the current NIRAS service blueprint, 
introduced in Chapter 8. The purpose of the analysis was to locate areas of interest 
in the blueprint, that had improvement potential. It was clear that there were opera-
tional steps that could either be optimized, reduced or removed in the original service 
blueprint. The results of the analysis can be seen in Figure 50, where areas of interest 
are marked in red. The results can also be seen in a larger dimension in Appendix 33.
As seen in Figure 50, some of the areas of potential improvements are contract 
formulation, negotiation, data collection and quality assurance, as they are all manual 
and time consuming activities. This highlighted the need for automated operations 
that eventually could reduce the time spent by the consultants. Based on the service 
blueprint analysis, a draft was made for the combined concept (1 and 3), visualising the 
initial operational structure of the developed concept. The new service blueprint can 
be seen in Figure 51 and in detail in Appendix 34.
With the derivation of the new service blueprint an initial new customer journey 
was generated, as the service steps had changed from the original service blueprint. 
The new customer activities, can from the perspective of NIRAS, be described as 
“Sales activities” and “Project activities”. The following Figure 49 shows the initial 
new customer journey.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Conceptualisation
In short, the new customer activities are as follows:
1. Start-ups meet NIRAS in entrepreneurial communities, where they are intro-
duced to the new services and are offered a free consultant session.
2. If the start-ups are interested they can book a free session via a booking site.
3. As a prerequisite to the free session, the start-up fill out a questionnaire with 
basic information about their company, sustainability ambitions, etc.
4. The start-ups participate in a free consultancy session, receiving general advice 
and tips relevant to their business, while being introduced to the new services.
5. If the start-up decides to buy a service, they receive an a quote from NIRAS, 
including a contract to sign.
6. After accepting the quote, the customers are invited to an initial workshop, 
focusing on aligning the expectations and agreeing on the service results.
7. Following the initial workshop, the start-ups provide what data they have avail-
able, through a data template, enabling NIRAS to conduct the results.
8. After the assessment NIRAS delivers the results either via email or verbally, 
depending on the client needs and chosen service package.
As the goal of this thesis was to develop a consultancy service, it was decided that 
the customer activities had a substantial role in determine whether or not customers 
would perceived the services as successful or not. As such the customer journey laid 
the foundation of the first testing sessions, allowing the team to test and evaluate each 
activity, ultimately ensuring only relevant activities.
9.5 Concept testing and detailing
An essential part of the project was to test the concepts with users to create and 
improve the service elements and to deliver a feasible, viable and desirable (Tim 
Brown, IDEO) proposed solution to the client. The testing was planned with users, so 
that the first tests would be with case companies early in their business development 
phases (e.g. Hacktus and Amino Wool), and the later tests would be with more estab-
lished case companies (e.g. AA-water and DropBucket). This was done purposefully, 
because it was assumed that the more established case companies would have a more 
critical eye due to their enhanced business experience, hence saving that for the most 
detailed version of the concepts.  
The test sessions had already been planned in the beginning of the project, where it 
was still uncertain, what the format of the concepts to be tested, would be. Initially, the 
thought had been to specify the concepts to a rather detailed level before the tests, but 
- as it often does - the plan did not quite hold, and the concepts were not fully detailed 
in the week of the first test rounds. That of course affected what could be gained from 
the test sessions. The testing and detailing (prototyping) of the combined concepts (1 
and 3) were conducted in four rounds, enabling the team to test and evaluate different 
elements of the concepts, while ensuring a step by step development of the level of 
detail. Each round provided insights on the services, which was consolidated before 
initialising the next round. The testing and detailing process can be seen in Figure 52, 
as a zoom-in on the design phase: Prototype.
In the following sections, the purpose, planning and outcome of each testing round 
is described and discussed. For detailed readers, most the execution is documented in 
appendices, as the main focus in the following sections is to elaborate on the iterative 
development process, highlighting what was gained from each testing round.

9.5.1 Test round 1: Co-creating services and testing the customer 
journey
The first test round was conducted with a group of 4 peer students, and the following 
day with the case start-up Hacktus. The same material was used in both tests to increase 
the validity of the test results and the main focus of the this test round was to evaluate 
the elements of the combined concepts. 
A playbook was created through two iterations to guide the workshops. The first iter-
ation of the playbook can be seen in Appendix 35, very roughly outlining the steps 
of the test. The second iteration, hence the final playbook, can be seen in Appendix 
36. The final playbook was designed in a more user friendly manner, so the facilitator 
could turn a page when moving to the next step. A description and a purpose of each 
step can be seen in Appendix 37. In general, the steps were planned so that the least 
serious and more fun steps were placed in the beginning. By doing so, it allowed the 
participants to ‘loosen up’, and a safe atmosphere was established for sharing all sorts 
of ideas and opinions later on. All questions were formulated as open-ended questions 
to spark a discussion, rather than just ‘yes’ and ‘no’ answers (Patton, 2002)
The purpose
The purpose of the first test round was to elaborate on how the services should be 
detailed and the first part of the test round focused on the following:
 
+ Should the services be presented as predefined packages? (I.e. select a predefined 
service package given customer needs)
or
 
+ As customised services based on the inputs in the questionnaire? (I.e. individu-
alised services are suggested based on customer information and product data)
The initial questionnaire can be seen in Appendix 38.
The second part focussed on co-developing the services, by asking the participants 
to cluster the needs cards, which can be seen in Appendix 39 in groups representing 
three services. From the original pile of case start-up needs - hence a list of 22 needs 
out of the 16 in total - it was decided to compare the needs and evaluate, if they were 
understandable. As a result, the needs were combined/reduced from 22 needs to 18 
needs, due to similarities.
Furthermore, the participants were asked to discuss when a physical meeting/work-
shop with NIRAS would create most value.
 
+ The questionnaire should be available digitally
 
+ Customers should be able to preview the service package online
 
+ The open office session should be located where the start-ups are (hubs, 
communities)
 
+ The open office session is a great way for start-ups to investigate their needs
 
+ The services should be translated in Danish when marketed in Denmark
 
+ A workshop meeting with NIRAS would be most valuable early in the service 
activities
 
+ It might not make sense to group several start-ups, because of sensitive 
data/knowledge
 
+ Staying in touch with customers could be done by providing additional services, like 
newsletters, online tools etc.
 
+ Start-ups see sustainability as also providing marketing value, demanding 
the results to be communicable and visual, like certificates
 
+ Start-ups are attracted by the promise of learning something, that can be 
used in the future

9.5.2 Concept detailing round 1: Designing services and refining 
the customer journey
It was now necessary to define, what the predefined services should contain, and how 
they should differ from each other. The research team discussed the options:
 
+ A staircase-like/car-wash model, where package 1 contains X, package 2 contains 
X and Y, and package 3 contains X, Y and Z
or
 
+ Service packages of different content and prices, but not staircase-like/carwash. 
So package 1 contains X, package 2 contains Y, and package 3 contains Z. More 
like a program / journey
From the first test round, the participants were asked to cluster the needs cards in 
3 different boxes, at the time named package 1, package 2 and package 3, i.e. three 
individual services. Two sets of package suggestions from the peers (teams of two) 
and three sets package suggestions from Hacktus (one from each owner). When the 
participants were clustering the need cards, they were allowed to copy cards and place 
them in multiple packages. They were also allowed to remove or add new functions if 
desired. The service package suggestions can be seen in Appendix 46, showing all 5 
sets of package suggestions. The team analysed the service package suggestions, and 
if a service element (needs cards) appeared in the same package 3 times or more, it 
was marked and attached to the belonging package, i.e. package 1, 2 or 3. During the 
analysis the team experienced that some service elements appeared in all tree packages,

9.5.3 Test round 2: Customer touchpoints
After generating the 4 services, it was necessary to test the content as well as the 
framework for the ‘free open office’ session. The second test round was conducted 
with Amino Wool and AA-water, focusing on testing the first meeting between NIRAS 
and potential start-up clients, and how they would react to the services (including price 
and specific service elements). From the insights gathered in test round 1, the initial 
services was generated as functional prototypes, including specific service elements. 
These prototypes laid the foundation for the second test round. Furthermore, a refined 
questionnaire and a ‘next step’ journey had to be tested. The testing material can be 
seen in Appendix 47, Appendix 48 and Appendix 49, respectively. As in test round 1, 
a playbook was developed to support the facilitation of the workshop, the playbook 
can be seen in Appendix 50. A description and purpose of each step in the workshop 
can be seen in Appendix 51.
The purpose
The purpose of the second test round was to test the content of the services with 
focus on the following:
 
+ Could the questionnaire work as framework for the ‘free open office’ session?
 
+ What do the case start-ups associate with ‘free open office’ session? And how 
would they describe the benefits?
 
+ How would case start-ups react to the predefined services?
 
+ How would the case start-ups react to the price levels and expectations of each 
predefined service?
 
+ It was beneficial to fill out the questionnaire before the free open office 
session, but the questionnaire should be shortened down
 
+ The questionnaire should contain questions about the idea and the reason for 
seeking sustainability advice
 
+ The inclusion and benefits of a free open office session was verified
 
+ It was verified that the questionnaire should be digital and be presented as 
a prerequisite before the free open session
 
+ Availability of data should be discussed during the free open office 
session to align expectations 
 
+ Free open office session will not always lead to a purchase
 
+ The wording is extremely important in the services, and should be targeted at the 
clients
 
+ The description of the service elements should be result-oriented rather 
than requirement-oriented
 
+ Besides the value of the service elements, the presence of the consultants are 
important to:
 - Allow clients to ask any questions that comes to mind
 - Establish a personal relationship
 - To educate the start-ups
 
+ It could be beneficial if the users could decide where to meet NIRAS, given the 
circumstances
 - In start-up communities
 - At NIRAS headquarters
 - Via online meetings
