9.5.4 Concept detailing round 2: Finalising the customer journey 
and determining the service blueprint activities
The insights from the second test round led to a number of changes in the service 
material and highlighted that the combined service blueprint had to be further detailed 
to each service. The refined material can be seen in Appendix 57, which contains six 
separate parts. Those parts are presented and discussed below. The service blueprint 
generation is described afterwards.
Disclaimer: The services are referred to the names they had at the time, hence “Bære-
dygtighedspotentiale”, “Benchmarking”, “Vurdering & strategi” og “Grønne fonde 
- how to?”.
Customer activities
This part describes the overall customer activities, and it thus similar to the ‘customer’ 
track in a service blueprint. The customer journey is described in a brochure, with the 
purpose of providing the overall context of the services. The customer journey consist 
of nine steps following the customer through the service experiences, based on the 
three main services (1-3), namely “Bæredygtighedspotentiale”, “Benchmarking” and 
“Vurdering & Strategi”.
Mail example
After having booked a open office session either via the start-up community facilities 
or directly via NIRAS, the customer receives an email confirmation with a link to fill 
out the questionnaire. The purpose of exemplifying the email was to concretise which 
means of communications that will be used. At a point in the concept generation, it 
was considered to propose an online platform on a website separate from NIRAS’, 
but this was not something that the test participants had valued, making it a non-val-
ue-adding, yet expensive activity. 
Questionnaire
In the confirmation email there will be a link to fill out a questionnaire, before the free 
open office session. From the tests it had become clear that the questionnaire was a 
good starting point for an open office session, because it would increase the amount 
of time spent on activities that benefit the customers, and thereby sooner lead to a 
transaction
Checklist for NIRAS
The checklist was created to ensure that the NIRAS consultants are able to capture 
the essence of the start-ups needs. Information such as the customer’s industry type 
and the amount of data they have available about their product/processes are essential 
to gather during the open office session, to reduce the time spent on communicating 
back and forth after the session. The consultant should, based on the checklist results 
and the business development phase, be able to recommend the most suitable service.
This stems from the literature study on start-up characteristics and needs, where it 
was clear that the development phase, as business is in, often determines the level of 
knowledge. This was further supported during the case study, where it could be seen 
that the complexity of the needs, differentiated depending on which development 
phase the start-up was in.
==PAGE:137==
121
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Conceptualisation
Service packages
If NIRAS deems it relevant - based on the checklist described above - they can recom-
mend one of the services. The service flyers, AS-IS, can be seen in Appendix 57. 
This version of the service flyers have not yet been beautified, but should at the end 
be printed and made available in start-up communities, before presented at the open 
office session, to allow the customers to orient themselves in the available services 
whenever possible.
Data template
The data templates are the last aspect of the services, and can be seen in the bottom 
of Appendix 57. When a customer has agree to buy a service - and the formalities 
have been taken care of - they will receive a data template, depending on the selected 
service. The ‘larger’ the package, the more detailed date has to be provided in the 
template. It is likely that the templates for the services “Bæredygtighedspotentiale” and 
“Vurdering & Strategi” will raise questions from the start-ups, which is also why the 
customer journey for these services include a pre-results workshop, with the purpose 
of aligning data availability, results expectations and so forth. 
Service blueprint development activities
The refined material of the services, indicated that is was necessary to create separate 
service blueprints, in order to specify the operations required to fulfil the elements of 
each service. This led to three individual service blueprints,  that can be seen in detail 
in Appendix 58. Each service blueprint is briefly described.
Service 1 and 4
At the time, service 1 and 4, hence “Bæredygtighedspotentiale” and “Grønne fonde 
- how to?”, was merged to one service blueprint, since the service elements were very 
similar. Fundamentally, the customer steps in the service blueprint was very similar 
to the customer steps showed in Figure 51 on page 114, but the operational steps 
required by NIRAS, had become more detailed. 
Service 2
The steps in service 2, hence “benchmarking”, is very similar to service 1 and 4. 
However, in this service blueprint, there is planned one extra workshop, where 
NIRAS can deliver preliminary results and align expectations with the customer, 
before finalising the results.
Service 3
The steps of service 3, hence “Vurdering og strategi” is very similar to service 2, and 
has besides the preliminary results workshop, one extra meeting in the beginning with 
the purpose of preparing the customer on how to deliver correct and valid data for 
the LCA.

each service, it was necessary to test and evaluate the entire service experience. The 
third test round was conducted with DropBucket, and was less formal than the prior 
rounds, and more of an evaluation of the content of the services.
The purpose
The purpose of the third test round with DropBucket was to evaluate and get feed-
back on the following:
 
+ Content of the service packages
 
+ Price of the service packages
 
+ Prototype: questionnaire, checklist and data templates

9.5.6 Concept detailing round 3: Finalising service instructions for 
NIRAS
In order to clarify the front and backstage interactions of the service blueprints shown 
in Appendix 58, instructions needed to be formulated to further develop and detail the 
services. The front and backstage interactions are all activities performed by NIRAS, 
and as such the instructions should enable NIRAS to understand the activities in the 
service blueprint. The instruction manual can be seen in Appendix 59. 
For each service tasks, it was necessary to formulate in depth descriptions of the 
content as well as instructions on how NIRAS should perform the tasks. The first 
section in the instruction manual introduced a description of the service blueprint 
activities and the second section introduces the service elements. 
9.5.7 Test round 4: Feasibility test with NIRAS
On the basis of the previous iteration rounds the service material was finalised. The 
purpose of the fourth and last test round was to facilitate an in depth feasibility test 
with NIRAS, focussing on the application of the different service parts. Furthermore, 
an initial idea - a sustainability principles toolbox was tested. The toolbox idea origi-
nates from the literature review and the empirical study, where start-ups and insights 
from the literature, expressed a need for a boundary object, that could be used to 
transfer knowledge between specialist and start-ups. 
A presentation of the material was generated to support the facilitation of the 
test round instead of a playbook. The reason for not using a playbook, was that the 
feasibility test needed to be more visual and descriptive and less workshop alike. The 
presentation can be seen in Appendix 60.
The purpose
The purpose of the fourth test round was to test and evaluate the different service 
parts, which after the three previous testing and detailing rounds had been developed 
in high detail. The following points needed to be clarified during the fourth test round: 
 
+ Where should the open office sessions take place? And how long should they be?
 
+ Decide on a visual identity in order to start ‘beautifying’ the services.
 
+ Evaluation of customer touchpoint prototypes.
 - It could be beneficial to include these services in a larger program, like DTU 
Stardust
 - The questionnaire focuses a lot on extracting information about the 
business, and so much about the specific product. For larger businesses 
with several products the questionnaire would less useful
 - Package 1 is perceived to have high market potential by DropBucket, since the 
service would also be relevant for larger companies in new product development, 
as a stage-gate service, providing the potential of moving forward.
 - The service packages should be available online, allowing anybody to 
get assess and be inspired

The feasibility test was organised in following steps:

+ Package 1: 7,500 DKK
 
+ Package 2: 15,000 DKK
 
+ Package 3: 40,000 DKK
 
+ Package 4: 3,500 DKK (1,500 DKK if an add-on service to the existing package)

spreadsheet. The descriptions and instructions for each tool would not be generated 
until after the test. Finally, step E was about agreeing on the format of the final client 
deliverable, due May 30th. 
 
+ The free open office session was validated and seen as a sales activity for 
NIRAS
 
+ Getting in contact with start-ups communities and be present near the customers, 
was seen as a good initiative for NIRAS to increase awareness

the open office session day most effectively

environmentally minded
 
+ The duration of the free open office session should be 1 hour for NIRAS to give 
usable advice
 
+ The free open office sessions should be available 1 day every month, with 6 time 
slots
 
+ The free open office session was perceived as a trade-off between non-chargeable 
yet customer acquiring activities by NIRAS
 
+ The questionnaire was confirmed as a prerequisite to the free open office 
session, and should be available online for two reasons:
 
+ To collect and sort customer statistics, build initial case database
 
+ To fill out and use the supporting service elements, like data templates and session 
checklist during the free open office session, reducing time spent if customers 
choose to buy a service
 
+ NIRAS verified the benefits of making the service digital
 
+ The customer journey activities was validated
 
+ The service descriptions should be more precise and clearly indicate what the 
customers an expect from each service package
 - Package 1 should be qualitative
 - Package 2 should be semi-quantitative
 - Package 3 should be quantitative
 
+ The service names are not precise enough
 
+ Package 1-3 should be targeted at start-ups that have in-house capital to 
buy the services, and package 4 should be perceived as a potential opportunity 
for NIRAS to collaborate with customers that show tremendous potential.
 
+ It was seen as crucial to stress that the assessments and advice are based 
on expert experience and knowledge.
 
+ The toolbox idea was validated and seen as having potential gains as support 
for the service package. NIRAS saw opportunities in being able to transfer the 
results via understandable method cards, that would allow the customers to “bring 
home” something physical from the result meeting.

9.5.9 Client presentation
The client presentation with NIRAS was focused on validating the developed concept, 
its elements and to align the expectations of the reminder of the project. The aim of 
the presentation was to elaborate on the service parts and to provide a holistic view of 
the service, with a walk through of the entire customer journey, highlighting how the 
different service parts contribute to delivering the service.
The result
In overall NIRAS was satisfied with the service result and had very limited feedback of 
the content or the final design. Therefore, most of the meeting was used to discuss the 
application of the toolbox. The feedback from the presentation can be seen in Table 
23 The most important points are highlighted in bold.
9.6 Discussion
The gap factors and the service blueprint was used to determine the “how might we” 
functions for the ideation process. The morphological chart was used to structure the 
ideation process which led to approximately 100 sub-solutions. Concept themes were 
created as the framework for the concept generation, based on the empirical study and 
the initial meeting with NIRAS. The concept themes were not contradictions to each 
other, and should instead be seen as user/client true directions of potential concepts. 
More themes could have been generated to open up the solution space, but during the 
task clarification, it was clear that the services had to solve some fundamental issues, 
e.g. the KPIs, and it would therefore not make sense to open up the solution space by 
generating multiple themes. 
The gap factors were originally identified based on the literature review and the 
case studies, meaning that they are more or less directly connected to the needs of 
start-ups. Furthermore, the service blueprint activities originally stemmed from 
NIRAS, meaning that services should be implementable.
Concept testing and detailing
Since the testing and detailing involved the case start-ups, it had been necessary to 
contact them well before the actual testing rounds, to ensure their participation. By 
doing so, it meant that the time and date of the testing rounds was fixed from the 
beginning. The original purpose of the testing and detailing was to evaluate and finalise 
the final concept, but because the time schedule was postponed during the develop-
ment, it meant that the first testing round turned out to be a co-creation workshop 
instead. This proved to be of great value and allowed the case start-ups to participate 
in the actual development of the service content, hence ensuring that the content of 
the services corresponded to their needs. 
The concept development, testing and detailing has been based on a user-centred 
development approach ensuring that the needs of the case start-ups have been included 
in the final concept. Given this approach is has been essential, when interacting with 
the case start-ups, to anticipate their behaviour and thereby attempt to avoid potential 
bias findings, that would lead to “specific or personal” solutions. During the testing 
rounds is was also important to ensure that the data from the sessions, was obtained as 
directly as possible from the participants, requiring as little interpretation as possible, 
before processing the data.  
Validity of the concept
From the case study, a list of needs was derived and used to determined the content 
of the services, during the testing and detailing of the final concept. When the partic-
ipants were asked to cluster, what originally was their own needs, in service package, it 
==PAGE:142==
126
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Conceptualisation
ensured that the services can be directly linked to the needs of the start-ups. Thereby, 
it is presumed that the validity of the concept, NIRAS sustain, is high, and that the 
services should be applicable with the case start-ups. On the other hand this also 
indicates that the reliability of the concept might be low, since it cannot be guaranteed 
that the services - based on the case start-ups - are applicable with other start-ups 
on the market. It became clear, early in the project, that sustainability is perceived 
as a complex definition, and often is misunderstood by non-experts. It is therefore 
presumed that NIRAS sustain can be successfully implemented, since the needs from 
the case start-ups are assumed to be of low complexity and quite generic in terms of 
sustainability. The literature study also indicated that many start-ups and SMEs find 
it difficult to implement sustainability principles. Since the case start-ups included in 
this thesis, represent different product development phases, the needs also represent 
different development phases. Thus, it is argued that the services, namely “Bæredyg-
tighedspotentiale”, “Bæredygtighedsanalyse”, “Miljøvurdering & Miljøstrategi” and 
“Funding program” should be relevant for many start-ups.
9.7 Conclusion
In overall it is argued that NIRAS sustain has been successfully developed based 
on user insights, and that the testing rounds have helped the authors to shape and 
finalise the concept. The participants expressed, during the testing rounds that the 
sustainability services - besides delivering expert based results - should be educational, 
enabling the start-ups to continue working with the results, as well as increase their 
knowledge about environmental sustainability. This emphasised the need for an object 
(tool) that could be used to support further work, beyond the scope of the interactions 
with NIRAS.
In combination with learnings from the literature study, this lead to the creation of a 
toolbox - that via method cards should support the transferring of knowledge between 
specialist and start-ups. 
The validation and discussion of the toolbox idea, lead to further development and 
detailing. Considering research question 3,  the next step was to explore how existing 
tools, methods or approaches could be implementable and how they can be used to 
benefit the NIRAS services. The development of the toolbox is described in Chapter 
10.
==PAGE:143==
127
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Developing the toolbox
This chapter stems from the idea of developing a toolbox - a collection of ecodesign, 
PSS and LCA methods and tools to be applied in the consultancy service NIRAS 
sustain. The chapter is therefore closely related to research question 3: 
 
+ How can approaches within ecodesign, PSS and LCA contribute to bridging the 
gap between sustainability-driven start-ups & small business and sustainability 
consultants?
With this chapter, the thesis team sought to investigate, how ecodesign, PSS and LCA 
approaches can be utilised to better enable a transfer of knowledge, skills and atti-
tude from sustainability consultants to start-ups wanting to become more sustainable. 
Answering that question gave rise to secondary questions, for which the literature was 
consulted, this time in a less systematic approach, with emphasis on learning about the 
use and classification of ecodesign, PSS and LCA methods and tools. The secondary 
questions will be answered continuously throughout this chapter, hopefully allowing 
the reader to follow our train of thoughts on the development of the toolbox: 
Why ecodesign, PSS and LCA - are there not other ways to engage in 
sustainability practices?
Environmental sustainability practices in businesses can cover a range of wide 
different activities, such as sustainable material and energy sourcing, reducing energy 
and material consumption in production, transportation, use and disposal, as well as 
ensuring that the material and energy used, retain as much value as possible. It of 
course also covers activities such as minimising waste, emission and pollution. As 
soon to be design engineers, our emphasis has mostly been on the reduction and value 
retention of energy and material consumption in production, transportation, use and 
disposal.    
Ecodesign does not refer to a specific method or tool, but is a holistic perspective 
on product design with the purpose of reducing environmental impacts (Lindahl and 
Ekermann, 2013). In fact, any specific procedure in a product development process 
that deals with environmental issues can be categorised as ecodesign (Baumann et al., 
2002). Design for X is another term, which overlaps with ecodesign, when mentioning 
the instances Design for Sustainability (or D4S), Design for Environment, Design for 
Re-manufacture, Design for Reliability, Design for Disassembly etc. The targeted users 
of the services developed in this thesis are product-oriented start-ups or innovative 
micro businesses, and thus ecodesign is a very relevant approach to include in the 
services. Introducing the users to analytical ecodesign tools and methods can ease 
their decision-making with regards to concept or product alternatives, whereas guiding 
principles can serve as inspiration and reminders on how to incorporate sustainability 
into a product. 
Product/service-system (PSS) is a strategy focussing on the integration of products 
and services in order to decouple the success of a business from the number of 
products sold (Finken et al., 2013). In that decoupling of property from material, 
there is a potential to reduce the environmental impacts of the given product. PSS 
was included as a source of tools and methods, because it offers a more radically 
innovative approach to sustainability practices by rethinking the entire business model, 
Chapter 10: Developing the toolbox
 
+ Why ecodesign, PSS and LCA - are there not other ways to 
engage in sustainability practices?    
 
+ Why include ecodesign, PSS and LCA approaches in a 
consultancy service?    
 
+ What is the idea with the toolbox and method cards in the 
service designed for NIRAS?    
 
+ How to use the toolbox?    
 
+ What is the point of method cards?    
 
+ How should the method cards look?
==PAGE:144==
128
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Developing the toolbox
rather than just the product, potentially reaching higher environmental gains (Keskin 
et al., 2013). There is a potential for the target users, because they are - according to 
the hypothesis stated in Chapter 6 - in early product development phases, where the 
business model behind the product may not have been determined yet. 
Finally, methods and tools within life cycle assessment (LCA) were included, because 
it is an acknowledged and systematic approach to determining products’ (and services’) 
environmental impact in the entire life cycle. By being acknowledged and systematic, 
the approach offers a way to quantify and benchmark against alternatives, which can 
be of great competitive advantage to businesses.   
Why include ecodesign, PSS and LCA approaches in a consultancy 
service?
Vallet et al. (2012) argue that environmental assessment tools, like LCA, cannot be 
applied by non-experts due to risk of misinterpretations and little relevance of results, 
whereas guidelines or manuals, like ecodesign, are more approachable to a non-ex-
pert. Unfortunately, these are often too generic, and the businesses lack validity of 
the results, because they applied the tools themselves [Amino Wool]. Hence “…a 
successful integration of the environmental dimension relies on the presence of an 
expert or an environmental group in active collaboration with design teams and the 
company’s top management.“ (Vallet et al., 2012, p. 351).
Furthermore, there is an abundance of methods and tools within ecodesign, and Le 
Pochat et al. (2006) find that it is not enough to just characterise and define a database 
of ecodesign methods and tools, but that it is necessary to deal with the fact that 
practitioners lack expert knowledge and guidance, in order to successfully integrate 
the methods and tools in their organisations. There are some general barriers towards 
the use of tools and methods; a high degree of complexity of eco-oriented tools 
and methods; hard to learn how to use a tool or method without prior experience or 
guidance (Klewitz et al., 2012); and that it can be challenging to select the appropriate 
tool or method for a specific task (Lindahl, 2005). 
From the literature review in Chapter 5, and throughout the testing sessions [peers, 
Hacktus and Amino Wool] described in Chapter 9, it became clear that the target users 
prefer an educational service, when it comes to sustainability consultancy. In oppose 
to more concrete, absolute services for start-ups - e.g. a legal consultant delivering 
a deed of foundation  (“stiftelsesdokument”) - a low-cost sustainability consultancy 
service cannot deliver exact, absolute results. Consequently, the services should be 
developed with an emphasis on the transfer of a mindset towards sustainability, and 
tools and methods are a way to do that, because they codify the otherwise tacit knowl-
edge of experts. 
What is the idea with the toolbox and method cards in the service 
designed for NIRAS?
The ideas with (Hansen & Andreasen, 2002) the “toolbox” within the service designed 
for NIRAS are to provide suggestions on how to deliver the elements within each 
predefined service, enable NIRAS to communicate results in a transparent manner 
and to enable a transfer of knowledge, skills and attitudes through the tools and 
methods, so the start-ups can continue to grow their sustainability practices. The idea 
Boundary objects
Successful scientific work is heterogeneous and 
requires cooperation between multiple actors. A 
boundary object is the medium of which actors can 
transfer knowledge between each other during 
research, development and so forth.
==PAGE:145==
129
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Developing the toolbox
in the toolbox is collection of ecodesign, PSS and LCA methods and tools classified 
on parameters that are relevant to application in a start-up context. The methods and 
tools can be selected based on their classification with each parameter. 
The idea with method cards is to elaborate and visualise the methods and tools in 
the toolbox. The method cards will thus work as instructions for NIRAS on how to 
deliver each service element. Physical cards will work as a boundary object allowing 
NIRAS to discuss the service process with the start-up customers, and by handing the 
cards over to the customers at the completion of the services, the cards will capturing 
the essence of each service. The aim is to enable the start-ups to re-experience the 
sustainability mindset that they - hopefully - reached in the consultancy by NIRAS 
sustain. The idea in the method cards is to produce A5 cardboard cards for each tool 
and method to be applied in the NIRAS sustain services, visualising on one side and 
describing and instructing of the other side of the card.
10.1 Structuring the toolbox
As already established, many tools and methods within ecodesign already exist, why it 
is necessary to structure the selection of appropriate methods and tools in a start-up 
context, i.e. in a NIRAS sustain context. The approach to the development of the 
toolbox is summarised in Figure 53. Given the scope of this project, the methods and 
tools were derived from five specified sources [1] that we have collected through our 
university experience or from prior used academic material. The final number of tools 
and methods were 117 - the number of methods and tools from each source can be 
found in Appendix 62.
Toolbox development process
1
Search and identification of 117 method and tools to be included in the toolbox. 
All tool and methods from 5 specific sources were included [1].
2
Collect all methods and tools in a spreadsheet, containing short descriptions and 
references to the origin of each. 
3
Consult the literature to explore how others have organised and classified ecodesign, 
PSS and LCA methods and tools, and then classify the content of the toolbox.
4
Define sortings parameters relevant to the purpose of this toolbox based on step       , 
and classify all the tools and methods according to the parameters. 
5
Expert evaluation of toolbox parameters and the tools and methods with Daniela 
Pigosso and Niki Bey. 
6
Update the toolbox parameters according to expert evaluation, and devise reccom-
mendations for further work and other possible sources of tools and methods.

Parameters for structuring the toolbox
There have been several collections and classifications of ecodesign tools and methods 
(e.g. Bovea & Pérez-Belis, 2010; Buchert et al., 2017; Byggeth & Hochschorner, 2006; 
Lindahl & Ekermann, 2013; Knight & Jenkins, 2009; Ness et al., 2007; Pigosso, 2012), 
which have been consulted to inspire the classification of the 117 methods and tools 
obtained in step 1, c.f. Figure 53. An overview of the authors, who have collected and 
classified methods and tools and their different suggestions for how to classify ecode-
sign methods can be found in Table 25. The overview does not provide an exhaustive 
list of parameters that have been used to classify ecodesign methods and tools, but it 
gives an indication of the most commonly used parameters. 
The toolbox to be developed in this thesis is to be used by sustainability experts 
(consultancies), so that the methods and tools can be applied to and with start-ups. 
Hence, the actual toolbox users are the sustainability experts, but the toolbox should 
also take the application in a start-up context into account. However, entering the 
search string “TITLE-ABS-KEY((startup* OR start-up* OR “start up*” OR ecopre-
neur* OR entrepreneur*) AND ecodesign)” in the database Scopus yielded only 17 
results, of which none actually tackled the application of ecodesign in start-ups. Thus, 
with inspiration from the aforementioned researchers’ suggestions for parameters and 
our understanding of the toolbox’s application in mind, the following parameters were 
chosen for this toolbox - they are shown in Table 26 in the toolbox in the green rows, 
and the parameter options in the rows below.
The parameter Outcome is inspired by the PhD thesis of Daniela Pigosso (2012), in 
which she developed an Ecodesign Maturity Model. The options within the parameter 
are: Prescriptive, comparative and analytical. Prescriptive tools and methods are generic 
guidelines, that prescribe an approach to increase the environmental performance of a 
product. Comparative tools and methods compare the environmental performance of

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Developing the toolbox
alternative products or concepts (p. 85), and lastly analytical tools and methods assess 
the most relevant environmental aspects of a product, in order to identify improve-
ment opportunities. 
Life cycle perspective refers to, which product life cycle, the method or tool considers. 
The life cycles are defined on the basis of Hauschild et al. (2018) as: Resources, Manu-
facture, Distribution, Use and End-of-life. The methods and tools can also consider 
all the life cycle stages.
Product development phase refers to the product development phase, in which 
the tool or method is most applicable. The phases are based on Ulrich & Eppinger’s 
(2015) six product development phases. From the hypothesis established in Chapter 
6 - namely that product development phases correlate to the business development 
phases of a product-oriented start-up or innovative micro business - this parameter 
was assumed to be applicable to the target customers of the developed service NIRAS 
sustain. The parameters Does it lead to radical or incremental changes? and Which 
data is required? are not meant to be used as selection parameters, but were used in 
the classification of the tools and methods, which is described in the next paragraph. 
Nature of  the results determines, whether the output is qualitative or quantitative. 
Complexity considers the complexity of the method or tool, based on if expert 
knowledge is required and how accessible it is.
10.2 Classification of methods and tools
The classification of the methods and tools were based on, what could be established 
directly from the origin of the method or tool (i.e. the article that introduces/develops 
it); from the articles, from which the parameters had been obtained; or assessed by the 
thesis team. The classification of the tools and methods can be found in the Toolbox, 
in the Google Drive folder. Assessments by the thesis team was kept at a minimum to 
increase validity and reliability of the toolbox, seeing that we have limited experience 
with actually applying the tools and methods - our experience was documented in 
column N for each tool and method. Our assessment was mostly necessary for the 
parameter ‘Product development phase’ because it had not always been considered 
by the creator(s), and there was also the risk of the creator(s) not referring to Ulrich 
& Eppinger’s product development phases. When it was not considered, or if there 
was any doubt about the meaning, the two parameters Does it lead to radical or 
incremental changes? and Which data is required? were be used to determine 
which phase, it is most suitable for. The first parameter regarding the type of change 
is inspired by Keskin et al. (2013), and is related to the insight that is more possible to 
implement large changes early in the product development. The data-related parameter 
is based entirely on Buchert et al. (2017). To provide an example: If the description of 
a method or tool indicated that it leads to ‘radical’ changes, and that the data required 
is only e.g. ‘solution concepts’, it was assessed that it would be most suited for ‘Early 
phases’ of product development.  
10.3 Expert evaluation
To increase the validity of the toolbox, the supervising team suggested that the 
toolbox content, parameters and parameter rating of the content should be evaluated 
and adjusted with the help of ecodesign specialists Daniela Pigosso and Niki Bey. 
The experts were each invited for a meeting of 1 hour, and they received the toolbox 
beforehand, so they would have time to look at it. The meetings were audio recorded, 
and the transcriptions are in Appendix 63. The interview with D. Pigosso led to the 
implementation of the parameter ‘Outcome’, which has already been described. Their 
recommendations are summarised in Table 27.  The additional outcome of the expert

10.4 Application of toolbox and method card 
development
How to use the toolbox?
The toolbox has been made in a spreadsheet, which enables quick sorting through 
the parameters. Depending on product development phase; desired outcome of the 
method or tool; and/or the product life cycle in focus, one can select one or more 
options for one or more parameters, which will then filter the view of the toolbox. As 
a result, the tools and methods that fit with the selected parameter options will remain, 
and the user can quickly get an overview of methods and tools that could be relevant 
in the given situation.    
What is the point of method cards?
It is a common understanding that tangible interfaces can increase learning, especially 
for novices to an area:
“… the more general view within education [is] that hands-on activity or 
manipulation of  physical manipulatives can be of  particular educational 
benefit.” 
(Marshall, P., 2007)
In oppose to digital interfaces that are often controlled by one user at a time, tangible 
interfaces can allow concurrent interaction, where the learners and teacher can share 
the control of the interface. In this case, the topic of interest is the use of tools and 
methods to enhance environmental sustainability. This is not a traditional domain in 
which to employ tangible interfaces as learning enhancers (Marshall, 2007). Rather, the 
more traditional domains are for instance programming, rhetorics, molecular biology 
and dynamic systems, and they all seem to have in common that they are either repre-
sented in a spatial manner or are in fact, physical/spatial domains. As such, the fact 
Method card development process
1
For each service, the correponding toolbox sheets ”Service #” are opened, and for 
each service element, relevant parameter options are check marked.
2
For each service element, the corresponding sheet ”Service #X” can be opened to 
find a net result list of relevant tool and methods for that service element.  
3
Evaluate the net result list by disqualifying tool and methods that are assessed to be 
irrelevant to the service element, due to beeing too specific or off-topic.  
4
Assess the tools and methods that were qualified, in order to select one or more for 
each service element to be made into method cards. 
5
Produce the selected method cards, with inspiration from IDEO [2]. Depending on the 
classification within each parameter of the associated tool or methods, the cards will 
have different icons.
6
Create a guide and a method card template, so additional cards can be produced if 
needed.

that the cards are tangible may not affect the learning, when it comes to environmental

It is not within the scope of this thesis to develop method cards for all 117 methods

start-ups. For the first three services, there are several service elements, which NIRAS

the column to the left. For each service element, the relevant parameter options have 
been defined, c.f. step 1 in Figure 54. Looking at the Toolbox, Table 28 corresponds 
with the sheets called ‘Service 1’, ‘Service 2’ and ‘Service 3’ - please scroll down to 
see all service elements. For each service element, e.g. ‘S1B. Bæredygtig livscyklus’,

and methods on the corresponding sheet, e.g. ‘Service 1B’. For other purposes, not 
related to NIRAS, the sheet ‘Custom filter’ can be used to configure any combination 
of parameters options to generate a resulting list of tools and methods.

Appendix 64.  The resulting list of tools and methods are based on 2-6 options within

methods. Considering the total number of 117 methods and tools, we have succeeded 
in making the tool and method selection easier for the user. Had there been even more 
parameters and parameter options, there might only have been 1-2 results for each 
service element. But, rather than defining more parameters and classifying each the 
tool and method within those parameters - which could lead to invalid results due to 
the team’s limited experience with the application of the tools and methods - it was 
decided that an average of 13 tools and methods is acceptable. Looking at Appendix 
64, we assessed which of the resulting tools and methods that should be “disqualified” 
for each service element, c.f. step 3 in Figure 54. The disqualified tools and methods 
were marked with grey, with the justification in the column ‘Justification for (dis)qual-
ifying’. Similar tools and methods were highlighted in the column to the far right, and 
the methods and tools selected to be made into methods cards were highlighted with 
green.

produced as method cards. For S3A ‘Streamlined livscyklusvurdering’ it was decided 
that NIRAS would be the better judges of which tool or method to apply and produce 
as a method card.
How should the method cards look?
The Californian design company IDEO [2] has developed a set of method cards to 
serve as inspiration for human-centred design approaches: “Each of the 51 cards 
describes one method and includes a brief story about how and when to use it.” They 
were used as inspiration in the development of the method cards. A method card 
template, which can be used by others, when producing additional method, is shown in 
Figure 55. The bottom row of icons refer to the parameters in the toolbox, that each 
tool and method has been classified according to.
Finally, the method cards for the selected tools and methods were developed, and they 
are presented in Chapter 11. It should be noted that this has been an exemplification 
of the toolbox, and it is recommended that NIRAS or other users of the toolbox use 
the sheet ‘Custom filter’ and the related sheet ‘Custom results’ to assess themselves 
which tools and methods to use and produce as method cards. The reason for this is 
that steps 3-5 in the Figure 54, were performed on a rather fragile foundation of our 
assessments of the tools and methods.

Recommendations for sustainability consultants
 
+ The toolbox and method cards can be used to hand-over knowledge and ways-
of-doing from one consultant to another - both for consultancies with high 
turnover of employees, but also if the services grow and more consultants are 
needed.
 
+ As a configurator in the customer journey?
As part of the customer journey, when NIRAS has determined what service to 
offer the potential customer, they could present the customer with a range of 
method cards that all fit within the proposed service, and have the customer 
select the methods, they want to work with. This could give the customers a 
feeling of customisation, but for the services proposed in this thesis, complexity 
reduction and standardisation have been key themes in order to reduce the cost 
of NIRAS’ offerings. Therefore we will not, for now, recommend that NIRAS 
opens up for customizability  
 
+ Add a parameter to the toolbox to keep track of experiences with applying the 
tool or method
 
+ Do not weigh the ‘Complexity’ parameter too heavily, and possibly simplify it by 
dividing it into several parameters
 
+ Consider the application sector, specific needs and type of product, that the 
customer is working with, when selecting the appropriate tools in one of the 
predefined services
Toolbox update instructions
To add tools to the toolbox, one simply scrolls 
down to the last row in the sheet ‘Methods and 
tools’ and fills out each column. The columns 
related to the parameters for sorting and 
selecting can be filled out by choosing an option 
from the drop-down menu. To add new parame-
ters, got the sheet ‘Parameter options’, and enter 
the parameters and its parameter options. Next, 
go to the sheet ‘Methods and tools’ and insert a 
new column with the parameter name. Mark all 
the cells in that column, below the parameter 
name, select the function ‘Data validation’ and 
choose the new parameter options as the data 
interval. Finally, classify all the tools and methods 
within the new parameter.

10.5 Further work
Expanding the toolbox
There are many more interesting tools that have been developed or are being devel-
oped, which would be useful to add to the toolbox, in order to keep it updated, and 
hence - as a sustainability consultant - keep one’s service up-to-date and relevant. It 
could also be considered to add SDGs as a parameter - this might increase the ease of 
use of the toolbox for non-experts. 
Furthermore, circular economy and servitisation are “buzzwords” in the industry 
right now. A point raised by Daniela Pigosso is that the content of the toolbox is 
already a bit outdated, and within a few months, even more new methods and tools will 
have appeared. For future work with the toolbox, it will be very interesting to update 
it with methods and tools that are more related to circular economy and servitisation. 
The idea in the toolbox that it should contain tools relevant for start-ups, and circular 
economy and servitisation strategies are extremely relevant for start-ups, seeing that 
they may not yet have settled on a business model, and are more risk-willing than 
established businesses. Table 30 shows an overview of relevant sources to consult, 
when looking for new methods and tools to update the toolbox with.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Developing the toolbox
10.6 Discussion
The intended use of the toolbox is that it could be applied by consultants or sustaina-
bility specialists, so they can select and apply relevant methods, to deliver their sustain-
ability consultancy services or guidance to start-ups. The intention of the method 
cards is to provide boundary objects between two heterogeneous actors, namely the 
knowledgeable consultants and the less knowledgeable start-ups. However, there are 
some limitations and shortcomings of the toolbox and method cards: 
 
+ Validity 
The tools and methods have not been developed with startups in mind - we 
have assumed that the toolbox is useful and suitable in a start-up context based 
on the hypothesis from Chapter 6: that product-oriented start-up development 
phases correlate with the conventional product development phases (Ulrich & 
Eppinger, 2015), to which many of the tool and method creators refer, when 
discussing the tools or methods application. Thus, the validity of the Toolbox in 
its intended use context may be doubtful. It should be tested, e.g. by launching 
the NIRAS sustain developed in this thesis project, and applying the methods 
and tools by either filtering the toolbox or using the results from the ‘Exemplifi-
cation of toolbox and method cards: NIRAS sustain’.  
The reason for the above is among others that the academic literature on ecode-
sign for start-ups and entrepreneurs is very limited, which made it hard to obtain 
any academically acknowledged methods and tools targeted at start-ups and 
entrepreneurs. Hopefully, the developed Toolbox and this thesis could contribute 
to the otherwise scarce amount of available literature in the area. 
 
+ Reliability
The reliability of the developed methods cards may also be a little low, consid-
ering that the steps 3-5 in the method card development process were not that 
well argued - it could have been improved by having experts or NIRAS them-
selves evaluate the resulting list of tools and methods for each service element 
and select the ones to be made into method cards. On a different note, the reli-
ability of the Toolbox is assessed to be high, because the origin of the tools and 
methods, as well as the parameters and the classification, are very transparent, so 
the work can easily be continued or challenged by others. 
10.7 Conclusion
This chapter argued for and described the development of a Toolbox - a collection 
of ecodesign, PSS and LCA approaches. We justified the relevance of a toolbox in a 
consultancy use context by consulting literature on the topic, and collected and classi-
fied 117 methods and tools, also grounded in the existing literature. The toolbox was 
evaluated by two ecodesign experts, and their recommendations were either imple-
mented or conveyed to potential users of the Toolbox. The use of the Toolbox and 
the development of method cards was exemplified, resulting in a number of method 
cards for the NIRAS sustain services, developed in this thesis. This chapter provided 
the final piece in order answer the third and main research question:
 
+ How can approaches within ecodesign, PSS and LCA contribute to bridging the 
gap between sustainability-driven start-ups & small business and sustainability 
consultants?
The (preliminary) answer is that ecodesign, PSS and LCA approaches can contribute 
to bridging the gap between sustainability-driven start-ups & small business and 
sustainability consultants by being central to the consultancy services. This is further 
elaborated in Chapter 14, where the impact of the developed services and Toolbox is 
discussed, and we attempt to conclusively answer the research questions.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
This chapter presents the final concept, NIRAS sustain and provides a detailed 
description of the elements in the final concept. To explain the reasoning behind 
the concept elements, the conceptual theory “the idea with” and “the idea in” by 
Hansen & Andreasen (2002) is used. The objective of the theory is to briefly explain 
the purpose as well as the function. NIRAS sustain consists of the following service 
elements:
11.1 Client guidebook - client oriented instructions
11.2 NIRAS sustain customer journey
11.3 NIRAS sustain predefined services
11.4 Toolbox
11.5 Method cards
11.6 Standardised result templates
11.7 Promotional material - brochures & poster 
11.8 Digital platform prototype - website functionalities
11.9 NIRAS sustain business case
11.10 Implementation roadmap - pilot testing
11.11 Exemplification of the services - case start-ups
Reader’s guide for concept presentation
Before introducing and describing the service elements, NIRAS sustain and the target 
customer is briefly explained. It is recommended to look in the client material while 
reading this chapter, to ensure a comprehensible understanding of the content. This 
chapter focuses on describing the functionality and purpose of each service element 
- without displaying the final design of each service element. Thus, to establish a 
convincing experience - functional and physical - the reader should combine the 
insights from this chapter with the client material.
The final client material consists of  digital as well as paper-based prototypes: 
The digital prototypes can be accessed via the provided Google Drive folder and 
consist of the following: Homepage, Predefined services, Case examples, Question-
naire, Checklist, Data templates, and Evaluation form as well as a website develop-
ment guide. The documents are interactive, allowing the reader to “use” the website 
functionalities. The Homepage.pdf includes the following elements: Case examples, 
Predefined services, Questionnaire and Checklist. The data templates are individual as 
for the evaluation form. The Toolbox prototype can also be accessed via the Google 
Drive folder, allowing the reader to utilise the instructions - provided in this chapter - 
to search and select new tools.
The paper-based prototypes are: Client guidebook, Predefined services (brochures), 
Method cards and Promotional poster. The client guidebook was developed to define 
the context of the application of NIRAS sustain - specifically aimed towards NIRAS. 
There are four predefined service brochures, namely ‘Bæredygtighedspotentiale’, 
‘Bæredygtighedsanalyse’, ‘Miljøvurdering & Miljøstrategi’ and ‘Funding program’.
What is NIRAS sustain?
Niras sustain is sustainability consultancy made affordable for start-ups and innovative 
businesses, driven by sustainability and a hope for a brighter future. The customer-cen-
tred services enable start-ups to tackle sustainability in three steps according to their 
development phase and sustainability ambitions. The process and results are supported 
by method cards that enable start-ups to continue progressing within sustainability, 
even after the consultation, when insights are often forgotten. NIRAS sustain operates 
in entrepreneurial communities to make sure that personal relations are made with 
potential clients, i.e. small Danish businesses likely and willing to make changes in 
the future. NIRAS sustain is about preparing and priming Danish companies to start 
thinking about the environmental impacts of products and services, while educating 
the companies to make smarter and more environmentally sound decisions.
Chapter 11: Concept presentation
==PAGE:159==
143
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
Target customer
The targeted customer with NIRAS sustain is start-ups aiming at environmental sustain-
ability, by working with a product or service that potentially performs environmentally 
better than its alternatives. This specific goal can also be shared by more mature busi-
nesses, which in this case is described as innovative and sustainability-driven micro 
businesses - less than 10 employees. In order to generate a better understanding of 
these customers, we have investigated the characteristics of start-ups and micro busi-
nesses, and their drivers for and barriers to sustainability practices. In a way, start-ups 
can be described as private customers, in the sense that they often are run by a few key 
personas, and the strategic direction is often based on personal interests. This means 
that start-ups to some extent can be treated the same way marketers treat private 
consumers. 
Today’s customer looks to be inspired by, to sympathise with and to be recognized 
by their service providers through channels that are easily available to them (Subsero, 
2018). At the same time, the services must be adapted to their individual needs to 
provide a feeling of customisation in a time, where almost any service is individualised 
based on Big Data analyses. Based on the characteristics of start-ups, it is clear that 
they differ from NIRAS’ current clients, i.e. medium to large companies and munici-
palities. 
The targeted customer can be described as ‘small businesses, driven by a few people 
with limited resources, operated on a day to day basis, with limited long term perspec-
tive, willing to take risks and ready to make changes’.
==PAGE:160==
144
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
11.1 Client guidebook - client oriented instructions
The idea with the client guidebook  is to briefly outline the service NIRAS sustain, while 
describing the supporting elements of the service. Since NIRAS’ current clients differ 
a lot from start-ups, it has been necessary, to outline the characteristics, barrier to 
sustainability practices and drivers for sustainability practices - ensuring that NIRAS is 
able to better understand and process start-ups.
The idea in the client guidebook is to prepare NIRAS to implement the service, by intro-
ducing - in a comprehensible manner - what is required by NIRAS, to proceed from 
pilot testing to realisation.
The guidebook include descriptions and instructions on how NIRAS can get started 
on the following actions:
 
+ Understand the customer - who are the target customer and how do they differentiate?
 
+ Define the customer journey - what are the customer touchpoints in the service?
 
+ Implement the predefined services - what are the service elements?
 
+ Prepare for open office sessions - which services fits the different customers?
 
+ Support services with the toolbox - how to use and define method cards?
 
+ Deliver results - how to utilise the result templates?
 
+ Collaborate with start-up hubs - where to meet the customers?
 
+ Promote NIRAS sustain - how to increase awareness?
 
+ Initialise NIRAS.dk/sustain - utilise the website functionalities how to interact with 
customers?
Following the instructions to each service element, the guidebook presents recom-
mendations,  an implementation roadmap and an estimated business case verification. 
The roadmap visualised the most important activities - which should be done - in 
order for NIRAS to successfully implement NIRAS sustain. The recommendations 
are based on the teams assessment of how NIRS can avoid certain pitfalls and main-
tain the service once implemented. The business case is based on assumptions - made 
by the team - and might therefore not represent a true scenario, but should be seen as 
a rough estimation of the business potential.

11.2 NIRAS sustain customer journey
The idea with the customer journey is to visualise the main touchpoints in the NIRAS 
sustain service, while emphasising the customer activities and how NIRAS can ensure 
a successful customer experience. The customer journey is shown in Figure 57 and can 
be seen in a large format in the client guidebook page 10-11.
The idea in the customer journey is to provide concrete information about the customer 
activities to ensure that NIRAS can accommodate the needs of the start-up - when 
they occur during the service journey. Each customer activity is grounded by acquired 
customer needs, and is shortly presented including the motive behind the activity:
 
+ Become relevant for start-ups: The first but important step is to ensure that start-ups 
are familiar with NIRAS sustain. It is presumed that there are two ways NIRAS 
can ensure that. Firstly, NIRAS needs to be present in start-up communities, 
allowing customers to approach NIRAS, where their daily routines are located. 
Secondly, NIRAS needs to promote the services via social media, to prime and 
persuade potential clients to consider the service, when sustainability issues arise. 
It has been acknowledged by the case start-ups, that a free open office session 
would intrigue the start-ups to learn more.
 
+ Allow start-up to explore NIRAS sustain: The case start-ups indicated that it should 
be possible to access and explore the services online before committing to a 
open office session. In order to support this demand, NIRAS should establish a 
convincing website showcasing the benefits of NIRAS sustain.
 
+ Reduce time by standardising processes: As a prerequisite to the open office session - to 
obtain information about the customer - the questionnaire is used to standardise 
the open office session, while enabling NIRAS to prepare for the best possible 
way to deliver consultancy to the client.
 
+ Convince the clients: The free open office session has two main purposes. One to 
verify the market need for sustainability services and two to enable NIRAS to 
market - and potentially sell - the new service. The open office session ensures 
that NIRAS has obtained the appropriate knowledge about the client, before 
suggesting one of the service predefined services, i.e. to appear as professional as 
possible. The open office session is perceived, by the case start-ups, to be a risk 
free opportunity to investigate the potential benefits of implementing environ-
mental sustainability principles.
 
+ Delivering the results: If the start-ups chooses to buy one of the services - and 
the formalities have been taken care of -  a standardised data template is used 
to collect the appropriate data from the client. NIRAS applies the appropriate 
method cards - to deliver the service element - and use the result template to 
graphically show the results.
 
+ Handing over the results: When NIRAS have conducted the service - based on data 
provided by the client and expert knowledge - the results are presented to the 
client in a face-to-face meetings, enabling NIRAS to present and describe the 
method cards - which have been used to support the conduction of the results. 
The methods cards allow the start-ups to re-experience the NIRAS sustain 
consultancy meeting and to re-apply the methods at a later phase - thus enabling 
the start-ups to make better decisions in the future.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
11.3 NIRAS sustain predefined services
Consultancy services are often complex and customised to each client. However, as 
a start-up, the budget is limited and the company structure is most likely simple and 
focussed on a single product development process at a time, rather than a range of 
products and services. This means that start-ups cannot afford expensive services and 
have relatively simple needs. To accommodate the constraint of very limited economic 
resources and meet the needs of start-ups, we performed qualitative interviews and 
testing sessions with six case start-ups ranging from the early to the late business 
development phases, and who were all interested or engaged in environmentally 
sustainable practices.
The idea with the predefined services is enable NIRAS to be able to deliver relevant - both 
in terms of content and price - services to start-ups, based on their expert knowledge 
and without incurring deficits, because the services are standardised and reuse material 
from client to client.
The idea in the predefined services is collect NIRAS competencies and then simplify and 
organise them into predefined services moulded directly based on user involvement 
of six case start-ups. Since the start-ups have limited resources - and NIRAS have high 
hourly wages - many of the case start-ups perceived the current offerings of NIRAS 
to be out of scope. Is was thus important to design services that could successfully 
balance the two contrary demands. 
The four predefined services are briefly described, and it is highlighted which customers 
they are relevant to. For detailed descriptions of the supporting service elements, the 
service blueprint and the specific elements, please see the client guidebook page 12-37. 
Service 1: Bæredygtighedspotentiale
The first service package: “Bæredygtighedspotentiale”  is targeted at technological 
start-ups and micro businesses in the early stages of product/business development, 
where product-specific changes are not too costly. As a result, there is no need for a 
high degree of detail, and this service is therefore based on qualitative data, expert 
knowledge and experience from prior cases. The targeted customers seek overall guid-
ance and pointers on how to proceed towards an environmentally sustainable product 
and business model. 
Service 2: Bæredygtighedsanalyse
The second service package: “Bæredygtighedsanalyse” or in english: “ Analysis of 
sustainability” is a service targeted at start-ups and micro businesses in the in-between 
development phases, where the product is more developed. The product is about to 
be tested and prepared for market launch and most of the fundamental data about 
design, material and production should be defined. On that note the main goal of 
the service is to provide more in depth data on the life cycle phases, as well as to 
incorporate circular economy as a mean to achieve a more environmentally sustain-
able product. Furthermore the aim of this service is to establish the foundation for 
sustainability comparison with competing products, by introducing functional units 
and system boundaries.
Service 3: Miljøvurdering & miljøstrategi
The third service package: “Miljøvurdering & miljøstrategi”, or in english: “Environ-
mental assessment and environmental strategy” is a service targeted at start-ups and 
micro businesses that have a fully develop product that has been introduced to the 
market as a sustainability driven product. The customers have a relatively in depth 
understanding of the products life cycle phases and have fundamental data informa-
tion about materials, production and disposal. As the product has been established on 
the market, the seek expert knowledge and advice to evaluate the products environ-
==PAGE:163==
147
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
mental performance, potentially compared to an alternative product on the market. 
The aim of the service is thus to provide a streamlined life cycle assessment, indi-
cating the most critical areas of improvement. In continuation NIRAS sustain should 
provide environmental strategic advice on potential sustainability initiatives that can be 
applied on the product to enhance the environmental performance in chosen impact 
categories. The results should be communicable and presented in a visual manner, 
enabling the customers to maintain or initiate “green marketing” and reduce the risks 
of “greenwashing”.
Service 4: Funding program
The fourth service package: “Funding program” is a funding program and not directly 
a sustainability service. The funding program is developed as a hidden service, not 
visible to the customers. The idea behind the program is that NIRAS sustain can bring 
forth this service if customers have a substantial environmental sustainability potential 
that needs in depth analysis and testing. It is assumed that this service becomes relevant 
when NIRAS sustain experience products out of the ordinary in “Bæredygtighedspo-
tentiale”, and sees a potential to collaborate on a funding  application, ultimately with 
the purpose of receiving funds to analyse the product. As a whole customers would 
through the application receive the full extent of the services that NIRAS currently 
have today, and most likely over a longer period of time. It should be made clear that 
the there is no guarantee of receiving funds when applying, which means that both the 
work of NIRAS sustain and the customers should be seen as an investment.
The price of  the services
It must be noted that the ‘price’ of each service package is based on customer insights, 
gained during the testing and detailing phase, and might not reflect the actual market 
price, one’s the service is marketed. It is assumed that NIRAS will adjust the price 
according to “what is possible”, but it is recommended to maintain a transparent price 
structure - even if a fixed price setting is very far from NIRAS’ current price structure 
- to ensure that start-ups are not intimidated by the size of NIRAS’ organisation and 
existing brand value.
==PAGE:164==
148
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
Introduction to toolbox, method cards and result templates
The four services are thoroughly described with regards to their content, and how to 
present them to the customers. The service blueprints give a step-by-step description 
of the activities, as well as time estimations. The toolbox and method cards serve as 
inspiration for exactly how to deliver each service element. Finally, the results templates 
provide a standardised way to present and discuss results with the customers, based 
on the method cards.
11.4 11.4 Toolbox
Studies clearly indicate that the application of methods and tools when working 
towards more environmentally sustainable practices is an advantage that increases 
the rate of successful implementation in product development (Knight & Jenkins, 
2009). At the same time, it is clear that many small businesses encounter challenges 
when applying advanced environmental tools - like assessment tools or environmental 
management systems - because they are expensive, complex and difficult to under-
stand. On the other hand, with the less advanced tools and guidelines, the businesses 
lack validity of the results, because they apply the tools themselves, thus failing to reap 
the benefit of applying them. 
The idea with the toolbox is to enable NIRAS to search an select for methods and tools 
with acknowledged sustainability principles - with the purpose of supporting the 
elements and results of the services. The toolbox can be used to find inspiration on 
how to perform and deliver the tasks within each service as well as to hand-over tools 
and skills, so that the start-ups can adopt the necessary knowledge, skills and attitudes 
and continue improving their sustainability performance.
The idea in the toolbox is a spreadsheet based collection of tools and methods from 
acknowledged sources. It consists of 117 methods and tools that can be sorted and 
selected based on five parameters: ‘Outcome’, Life cycle perspective’, Product devel-
opment phase’, ‘Nature of the results’ and ‘Complexity’.
Applying the toolbox: The toolbox can be accessed via the Google link. To search and 
select methods and tools, go to sheet ‘Custom filter’ and check mark relevant param-
eter options to sort the tools. The results can be found in the sheet ‘Custom results’. 
For the NIRAS sustain services, the parameter options are already check marked in the 
sheets ‘Service 1’, ‘Service 2’ and ‘Service 3’, and the resulting tools and methods can 
be seen in sheets ‘Service 1A-3B’, respectively.
Updating the toolbox: The toolbox, AS-IS, can be seen as the foundation of the method 
cards, and provides the basis for many method cards - but if the toolbox should be 
able to continue feeding relevant method cards, it would require NIRAS to update 
the toolbox, when tools within ecodesign, LCA, PSS or e.g. circular economy and 
servitisation appear.
==PAGE:165==

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
11.5 Methods cards
The idea with the method cards is to enable a knowledge transfer between NIRAS and 
start-ups, transforming the results of the services into visual representations, allowing 
the start-ups to continue progressing with the tools after the consultation. Thus, the 
methods cards can be seen as tangible boundary objects enabling the start-ups to 
understand and utilise the results and knowledge. 
The idea in the method cards is to visualise how NIRAS - in a step by step approach - 
have delivered the service elements, hence allowing the start-ups to re-experience the 
NIRAS sustain consultancy meeting, and re-apply the methods at a later phase - thus 
enabling the start-ups to make better decisions in the future.
Simplified examples of  method cards: Based on the toolbox selection parameters, tools 
were selected for the services “Bæredygtighedspotentiale” and “Bæredygtighed- sana-
lyse” and made into method cards, supporting the services. The result is 11 simpli-
fied method cards that illustrate how the methods and tool from the toolbox, can 
be translated into user-friendly boundary objects - which can be used to deliver the 
service elements. Miniature examples of the method cards as well as the method card 
indicators can be seen in Figure 58, but please see the paper-based prototype: Method 
cards, for further details.
As a an additional method card, the element in the toolbox called “PSS types” was 
added, even though it may not actually be classified as a method or tool, rather a 
model. The purpose of producing that particular element as a method card was to 
enable NIRAS to suggest their product-oriented start-ups to move towards a more 
service-oriented business model, if there is a potential for reducing the environmental 
impact.

Ten Golden Rules
1. 
Do not use toxic substances and utilize closed loops for necessary but 
toxic ones.
2. 
Minimize energy and resource consumption in the production phase 
and transport through improved housekeeping.
3. 
Use structural features and high quality materials to minimize weight 
- in products - if such choices do not interfere with necessary fl exibility, 
impact strength or other functional priorities.
4. 
Minimize energy and resource consumption in the usage phase, 
especially for products with the most signifi cant aspects in the usage 
phase.
5. 
Promote repair and upgrading, especially for system-dependent 
products. (e.g. cell phones, computers and CD players).
6. 
Promote long life, especially for products with signifi cant 
environmental aspects outside of the usage phase.
7. 
Invest in better materials, surface treatments or structural 
arrangements to protect products from dirt, corrosion and wear, 
thereby ensuring reduced maintenance and longer product life.
8. 
Prearrange upgrading, repair and recycling through access ability, 
labelling, modules, breaking points and manuals.
9. 
Promote upgrading, repair and recycling by using few, simple, recycled, 
not blended materials and no alloys.
10. Use as few joining elements as possible and use screws, adhesives, 
welding, snap fi ts, geometric locking, etc. according to the life cycle 
scenario.

Sustainability criteria suggestions
What
Why
The Life Cycle Perspective is a fundamental model, that 
can be used to visualise and describe the diff erent life 
cycle phases of a product, from raw material extraction 
to disposal.
The model enables designers to include all the life cycle 
perspectives during the development of a product. 
The purpose of the model is to establish an overview 
of potential pitfalls in manufacturing, distribution, use 
and disposal, while ensuring that designers include the 
rights thoughts from the beginning.

Functional Unit
What
Why
The Functional Unit describes the basic function of a 
product by quantifying the obligatory properties, i.e. 
the must have properties. The functional unit is often 
used by LCA expert to defi ne the basis of a products 
functional performance.
The Functional Unit is used by designers and engineers 
to defi ne the functional purpose of a product. When 
the functional properties are mapped, designers can 
compare the products environmental performance to 
another products with the same functional properties.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
11.6 Standardised result templates
The method cards are meant as a support for NIRAS sustain in delivering the four 
predefined services. They will be especially valuable in interactions with the customer, 
because they can ‘codify’ some of the knowledge, which is otherwise hard to obtain. 
For each service, a number of method cards have been produced. These method cards 
should be translated into comprehensible result templates, enabling NIRAS consult-
ants to standardise the process of delivering the results of a service to the start-up. 
The idea with the standardised result templates is to enhance the knowledge transfer between 
specialist and start-ups as well as to simplify and streamline the reporting process. The 
result templates should be generically designed to reduce time spent per customer, 
while still allowing the consultants to specify the templates and provide a feeling of 
customisation. 
The idea in the standardised result templates is to present the results based on the choice 
of methods and nature of the results. Thus it is argued that the design of the result 
templates should, to a certain degree, be based on how the consultant wishes to hand-
over the results - while still be generically generated based on the methods and tools, 
to reduce time spent by the consultants.
Figure 59 shows a rough example of how a result template could be designed, based 
on a method card. In continuation of this, it is recommended, that NIRAS focus on 
developing a result template for each predefined service, as they get comfortable with 
delivering the services to start-ups.

11.7 Promotional material - brochures & poster 
It is important to reach the customers, where they are and through their preferred 
channels. The start-ups targeted with the NIRAS sustain services will - if contact is 
established - be located in and around Vaeksthus Copenhagen. Therefore, NIRAS 
should prioritise and distribute promotional material to increase awareness. It is recom-
mended that NIRAS reach these customers through three promotional elements: 
Printed posters, social media stories & post and printed service brochures. 
The idea with the brochures is to provide easy to understand 1-pagers, displaying each 
element of the services. They should be distributed during the open office sessions, be 
available online, and perhaps also be handed out in common areas of start-up commu-
nities - beneficially near the location of the promotional poster to create context.
The idea in the brochures is to show the benefits - the “why” of the predefined services. 
Each service is marked with a “taxonomic identifier”, i.e. ‘initiate’, ‘analyze’, ‘strate-
gize’ and ‘accelerate’, indicating the difference between the services as well for which 
business development phase the services are useful in. In recognition of the customer 
needs, transparency is a key theme. This is emphasised by the transparency of the 
price, and the “helt konkret” section that shows exactly, what the customer can expect 
from each service. Miniature versions of the service brochures can be seen in figure xx, 
but please see the paper-based prototype: Predefined service (brochures) for more details.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
11.8 Digital platform prototype - website functionalities
The idea with the website functionalities is to collect and present the standardised service 
elements included in NIRAS sustain. The purpose - besides digitalising the interac-
tions between NIRAS and the start-ups, hence reducing time spent per customer - is 
to allow NIRAS to “store” user data and communicate with clients.
The idea in the website functionalities is to create a coherent user experience of NIRAS 
sustain and the underlying service elements - without undermining the importance 
of face-to-face expert advice. From the case study it became clear that the start-ups 
wanted to have a digital experience - to reduce time and hassle - while still being able to 
talk directly to an expert - ensuring the human touch between specialist and start-ups 
- providing credibility and trustworthiness of the results.
The website functionalities is briefly introduced and described in the following 
sections, but please see the digital prototype: Homepage, Predefined services (online), Case 
examples, Questionnaire, Checklist, Data templates, and Evaluation form, for further detail 
when reading through the sections.
11.8.1 Homepage
The purpose of the homepage is welcome the customer when entering the website. 
Centrally on the page is a previous customer quote, followed by a “call- to-action” 
button, prompting the customer to book the free session. With reference to the 
customer journey previously described, it is expected that the customer will have heard 
of the NIRAS sustain services previously in one of the start-up hubs. Thus, they will 
likely already have heard about the free session. This means that it is important to 
make it hassle free and easy for the start-ups to book the free session online.
The menu-bar is placed at the top of the homepage. When entering one of the 
sub-sites, the users “position”, is highlighted by a thicker and dark blue font. Above 
the navigation bar, in the top right corner, there is a login and search bar. By pressing 
the login button the user is redirected to the login page, where they can sign in or 
create an account. NIRAS can also login to their admin account and manage live and 
prior cases, as well as communicate with clients. A screenshot of the homepage can be 
seen in Figure 62, but please see the digital prototype: Homepage (front page) for further 
details.

11.8.2 Predefined services (online)
The purpose of the sub-site “Services” is to present the users with an overview of 
the services that NIRAS sustain offer. When entering the site the users are presented 
with four boxes representing the service packages and a call to action button. The 
users are then able to click the packages and read more about the specific benefits 
and requirements for each services. When clicking on a service the other services will 
be greyed out and a in depth description of the service will appear. At the bottom of 
the description another call to action button is places: “Book et uforpligtende møde” 
- prompting the users to proceed. A screenshot of the predefined services pages is 
presented in Figure 63, but please see the digital prototype: Homepage (services) for 
further details.
11.8.3 Case examples
The purpose of the sub-site “Cases” is to present the users with successful case stories 
from previous customers. Similarly to the boxes displayed on the “Services” page 
the users are presented with a short intro to the case story and key results from the 
collaboration. To inspire new customers, the applied service for each case is attached 
to the case examples. When clicking on a case example, a more in depth description 
appears on how the collaboration went and what others can learn from going through 
the same service experience. The case examples should be presented with a picture 
from the workshop, ensuring a personal touch to the site. This requires that NIRAS 
document every case. A screenshot of the case example page is presented in Figure 64, 
but please see the digital prototype: Homepage (cases) for further details.

Grundlæggende vejledning på baggrund af vores eks-

Bæredygtig livscyklus
Vi viser jer, hvordan livscyklustankegangen kan bruges 
til at identifi cere brændpunkter, og I får konkrete råd 
til, hvordan I kan indtænke bæredygtige principper. At 
tænke i produktets livscyklus er afgørende for at sikre, 
at kritiske miljøpåvirkninger tages højde for allerede i 
udviklingen af produktet. Som startup er der stort po-
tentiale ved at implementere den rette tankegang tid-
ligt, da det kan spare ændringer - og dermed omkost-
ninger senere i produktudviklingen.  
Vurdering af bæredygtighedspotentiale
Er det bæredygtigt? Med udgangspunkt i den situation, 
som jeres produkt skal forbedre, foretager vi et kvalita-
tivt estimat af produktets miljømæssige bæredygtig-
hedspotentiale. Resultatet præsenteres på en bære-
dygtighedsskala.
