Analyse af livscyklusfaser
Hele livscyklussen sættes i fokus, og der zoomes ind på 
de faser med størst potentiale for bæredygtig udvikling. 
Ved at sætte generelle tal på faserne, kan vi hjælpe jer 
med at omstille og minimere produktets miljøpåvirkning. 
Tænk cirkulært
Materialerne, der indgår i dit produkt, kan være nyttige 
efter endt livscyklus. Ved at tænke dette ind allerede i 
produktudviklingen, kan du sikre højest mulig nyttevirk-
ning af produktets materialer.
Grundlag for sammenligning med alternativer
Én af de største udfordringer ved at sammenligne et 
produkt med konkurrerende produkter er, at de er for-
skellige og derfor ikke direkte sammenlignelige. For at 
overkomme den udfordring, skal systemet omkring pro-
duktet defi neres og afgrænses, hvilket vi kan hjælpe jer 
med. Kombineret med analysen af jeres produkts livs-
cyklusfaser, danner dette grundlag for at sammenligne 
jeres produkt med konkurrerende produkter. 
Services
D
enne service er relevant for startups og virksom-
heder, som er i gang med at detaljere, teste eller 
opskalere et produkt og har brug for at sætte tal på 
livscyklusfaserne som grundlag for ændringer, eff ek-
tivisering eller sammenligninger med konkurrenter, 
mens prisen for forandringer stadig er relativt lav.

denne service

Services
Streamlined livscyklusvurdering
En livscyklusvurdering (LCA) er en kortlægning og en 
vurdering af et produkts påvirkning af miljøet fra pro-
duktets skabelse, indtil det bortskaff es eller genanven-
des. LCA-metoden er baseret på ISO standard 14040, og 
er en meget kompleks proces. ‘Streamlined’ henviser til 
den type LCA, hvor man for at simplifi cere processen fx 
kun fokuserer på udvalgte livscyklusfaser eller anvender 
kvalitativ information til dele af vurderingen. Dette er en 
fordel for især små virksomheder, da kravet til mængden 
af data er mindre omfattende, og der ikke udføres unød-
vendigt komplekse analyser.
Miljøstrategisk rådgivning
På baggrund af NIRAS’ ekspertviden inden for miljømæs-
sig bæredygtighed, hjælper NIRAS med at skabe overblik 
over potentialer for værdiskabende miljøtiltag, nye stra-
tegier og cirkulære forretningsmodeller. Samtidig er det 
også afgørende at opbygge viden om, hvordan virksom-
heden holder sig omstillingsparat til kommende lovgiv-
ningsændringer og reguleringer. 
Services
Denne service er relevant for startups og virksomhe-
der, som er ved at etablere et produkt på markedet 
og har brug for en målbar miljøvurdering, samt søger

11.8.4 User page
The “User page” has been designed to include a portal where users can interact with 
the services and with the NIRAS consultants. If the users chooses to book a free 
session, they receive a link to the online questionnaire. Before filling out the ques-
tionnaire, it is required to create an account on the website portal. The portal enables 
NIRAS and the start-ups to share and store data and information relevant for the 
services. The portal includes elements like:
 
+ Online booking-system 
 
+ Pre-session questionnaire 
 
+ Data templates
 
+ Service result templates 
 
+ And much more.
For NIRAS this portal also enables the possibility to collect and store information 
about their customers centrally, and generate statistics. A screenshot of the user page

11.8.5 Questionnaire
After signing up for a free session, the start-up automatically receives a link to an 
online pre-session questionnaire. If the start-up hasn’t already signed up and created a 
profile, they will have to do so first.
The questionnaire is created as a tool to help NIRAS get a quick overview of the 
start-up before the free open office session. At the same time, the questionnaire is 
designed to prepare the start-ups for the open office session, forcing them to consider 
and define their sustainability attitude and perspective.
The questionnaire consists of seven questions which is designed to provide NIRAS 
with the necessary information - needed to be prepared for the open office session. 
The 7 questions are introduced, but please see the digital prototype: Homepage (login 
- book free session - answer ‘questionnaire’) for further details. 
1. The product idea - what are the intention of the product?
2. The environmental sustainability ambitions - what is the sustainability goal of 
the product?
3. The competencies of the start-up - what skills and competencies does the 
start-up possess?
4. The product/business development phase - how far in development is the 
start-up?
5. Indication of sustainability mindset - what are the key drivers/barriers to/for 
sustainability?
6. Indication of green purpose statement - what is in reality important to the 
start-up?
7. Indication of UN sustainability goals - what goals is you product affecting?
 
A screenshot of the questionnaire is presented in Figure 66.

11.8.6 Checklist
The checklist was created to provide a framework for the open office sessions, with 
the overall purpose of gaining an understanding of the customers, to assess their 
needs with regards to services and initial sustainability recommendations. Information 
such as the customer’s industry type and the amount of data they have available about 
their product and processes are essential information to gather during the open office 
session, to reduce the communication after the meeting - and thereby keep the price 
to a minimum.
After filling out the checklist during the free open office session, the last part of the 
checklist presents the NIRAS consultant with a guide “How can NIRAS help” on 
how to assess which service to recommend the start-up, based on the business devel-
opment phases. Characteristics and needs obtained about the case start-ups indicated 
that, the business development phase of a company, determines to a certain degree, 
the level of detail required with regards to sustainability consultancy.
A screenshot of the checklist pages is presented in Figure 67 but please see the digital 
prototype: Homepage (admin site - ‘checklist’) for further details.

11.8.7 Data templates
When start-ups have decided to buy a service - and the formalities have been settled - 
the customer has to provide data to NIRAS, enabling the consultants to generate the 
results. The data templates can be assessed online, via the website portal. The ‘larger’ 
the package, the more detailed date has to be provided in the template. It is likely 
that the templates for the services “Bæredygtighed- sanalyse” and “Miljøvurdering & 
miljøstrategi” will raise questions from the start-ups, which is also why the customer 
journey for these services include a pre-results workshop, with the purpose of aligning 
data availability, results expectations and so forth. 
The data template consist of the following pages, but please see the digital prototype: 
Data templates (1-3) for further details.
 
+ Information about the materials used in the product
 
+ Information about the production
 
+ Information about the resources used in production
 
+ Information about the start-ups business model
 
+ Information about the disposal of the product
 
+ Defining the functional unit (Only for the Miljøvurdering & miljøstrategi service 
package)

digital prototype: Data templates (1-3) for further details.

11.8.8 Evaluation form
To follow up on the services delivered to start-ups, it is important to collect insights, 
experiences and feedback from the customers. In order to do so, the website portal 
includes an evaluation form, which is automatically sent to all clients that has received 
results after a service. The evaluation form should extract information from the 
customer, that NIRAS can use to further develop and update the NIRAS sustain 
services. Furthermore, the evaluation form shows the start-ups that NIRAS cares 
about delivering the best possible services for their customers.
As a result, the measure: Net Promoter Score, was implemented in the evaluation 
form, since the measure is acknowledge other service providers - enabling NIRAS to 
set customer goals for the final service and measure the customer satisfaction, e.g. to 
reach a NPS score of minimum 8 or similar.
A screenshot of the evaluation form  is presented in figure x, but please see the digital 
prototype: Evaluation form for further details.

11.9 NIRAS sustain business case
The business case of NIRAS sustain is based on assumptions about the cost of imple-
menting the final concept and its elements. The assumptions are based on a simple 
forecast of the performance of the service during the first ten years. The cash flow 
assumption can be seen in Table 31. The ‘price’ of each service package is based on 
customers insights, gained during the testing and detailing phase, and might not reflect 
the actual market price, one’s the service is marketed. Furthermore, the cost of deliv-
ering the open office session is based on the price of one assistant for 7,5 hours per 
day, at an hourly wage of 1.000 kr. It is assumed that the profit margin is 40 % when 
NIRAS starts to deliver the services. This assumption might not reflect the true profit 
margin in NIRAS, and thus it is expected that NIRAS can/will adjust the assumption. 
It is furthermore assumed that the distribution of services is 50 %, 30 % and 20 % for 
service 1, service 2 and service 3, respectively. This assumption is based on insights 
from the testing and detailing phase and is therefore not representative of the entire 
start-up market, and it is expected that the distribution of sales must be tested in a 
pilot project. It is assumed that after one year, the conversion rate from the open office 
session is 33 %. This is relatively low, e.g. compared to the legal services in Skylab, 
which is 50 %. This reason is that legal and intellectual property services are perceived 
as critical and irreplaceable service when establishing a tech business. The team experi-
enced during the empirical study that the same does not apply for sustainability, which 
is currently seen more as an add-on. As a result the assumed conversion rate for these 
services were estimated to be lower than legal and intellectual property services.
Since the final concept, NIRAS sustain, is based on a digital platform, Table 31, 
shows the assumption about implementation and maintenance cost of such a plat-
form. The development cost assumption was validated by Jens Christian Pedersen, 
front-end it developer in Danske Bank. One the it platform has been developed it is 
assumed that there will be a yearly maintenance cost of 50.000 kr., including renewal 
and manufacturing of materials for marketing and sales activities.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
Cash flow
Based on the assumptions presented above, the estimated cash flow is generated. 
Figure 70, shows the cash flow in a ten year period. The cash flow visualised the flow 
of cash being spent versus earned.
Based on the assumptions about a steady conversion rate, the figure illustrates that 
the cash flow is steadily and positively growing during the ten years, which might 
not be the case in a real life scenario, and is perceived as a major uncertainty in this 
business case forecast. Furthermore, it is assumed that the yearly maintenance cost of 
the service is the same during 10 years. This might not be true either and it is expected, 
given the pace of technological development anno 2018, that the digital platform has 
to be updated ones or twice during the ten years.
Accumulated cash flow
The accumulated cash flow is a statement of the outgoing the incoming cash in a 
company, and are used to indicate when a company reaches an overall break-even. The 
accumulated cash flow can be seen in Figure 71, indicating that NIRAS sustain should 
generate profit at the end of the third year. As described above, this is only viable if 
the assumptions about the cash flow is accurate and that the conversion rate of the 
open office session is correctly estimated.
In conclusion, the accumulated cash flow indicates that the services are profitable, 
but that it will be small scale income compared to NIRAS’ current offerings. This 
can be explained by the necessity of low prices of the service, in order for NIRAS to 
become relevant for start-ups. With that being said, it is assumed that, as sustainability 
become more important, the need of sustainability services will rise, allowing NIRAS 
to benefit from the early market position. Furthermore, this would increase the 
conversion rate of the open office sessions, while also attracting customers in general.
Figure 70: Cash flow in a 10 year period
Figure 71: Cash flow in a 10 year period
==PAGE:178==

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept presentation
11.10 Implementation roadmap - pilot testing
It is recommended that NIRAS initiates a pilot project, to evaluate the services of 
NIRAS sustain, and to verify the market demand. We recommend that the pilot testing 
runs for 6-12 months in collaboration with a start-up community.
The purpose of the pilot test should be to answer the following:
 
+ Is there a market interest? From start-ups and/or the government?
 
+ Does the open office sessions work?
 
+ Does the service reflect the needs of the start-ups?
 
+ Are the prices of the services right? Are start-ups willing to pay for sustainability 
consultancy?
 
+ Can NIRAS sustain work along other free consultancy services in start-up hubs?
An important first step - to pilot test NIRAS sustain - is to initiate contact with one 
or more start-up hubs. To get this process started, Vaeksthus Copenhagen (Væksthus 
Hovedstadsregionen) - from now on referred to as VC -  was contacted, with the 
purpose of investigating the possibility of developing a collaboration between NIRAS 
and VC.
Facts about Vaeksthus Copenhagen (VC): VC offer guidance to start-ups and businesses 
with ambitions towards growth and reaching new heights of success. The target group 
for VC is start-ups and businesses, who wish to enter new markets, develop new prod-
ucts and increase profit. VC services are free of charge, and they employs more than 
30 consultants and give counsel to around 1000 start-ups and small businesses every 
year. 
When reaching out to VC, the team established contact with Robert Schmidt - start-ups 
consultant and head of “Grøn vækst via grønne forretningsmodeller” (Green growth 
through green business models). The meetings with Robert verified that NIRAS 
sustain would be able to benefit many start-ups and small businesses in VC and was 
very open towards establishing a collaboration with NIRAS.
Other potential collaboration partners: Besides VC, ‘Vaeksthus’ have multiple departments 
in Denmark, that would be interesting to get in contact with. Furthermore, DTU 
Skylab and  KU Science Innovation Hub are interesting start-up communities for 
NIRAS to investigate.
Pilot testing roadmap
Based on initial verification of potential collaboration with VC, an implementation 
roadmap provides NIRAS with concrete recommendation on how to pilot test and 
implement the developed solution: NIRAS sustain. The roadmap can be seen in 
Figure 72. 
The roadmap shows some of the necessary steps to pilot test and implement the 
solution. The steps is briefly described:

11.11 Exemplification of the services - case start-ups
It was identified through user involvement and literature studies that start-ups in 
different business development phases need different sustainability consultancy 
services. As a result, Figure 73 provides an overview of how we see the application 
of the services, and how they are connected to the different business development 
phases.
The case study further indicated that product-oriented start-ups can be categorised 
based on, which product development phase they are in, because start-ups often focus 
on one product at a time, rather than having a range of products. We argue that there is 
a comparable connection between the product development phases and the business 
development phases. 
It is further emphasised that the specific needs of a customer, should be assessed by 
a NIRAS expert in the “open office session”, to offer them the most suitable of the 
four services. A rule of thumb is that technology-driven start-ups are more complex 
than product-oriented start-ups, and might thus need a service designed for a business 
development phase, later than the one they are in. 
In continuation of this, the case start-ups involved in the empirical study -  and then 
later in testing of the services at a conceptual level - are linked to Figure 73, based 
on the business development phase they are in. The result - an exemplification of the 
services - can be seen in Table 32.
Start-ups with less complex products might need a less advanced NIRAS sustain 
service - than the one suggested in Table 32  and the other way around for complex 
products or technologies. This underlines that the NIRAS sustain team take advantage 
of the ‘open office session’ to assess the start-up’s needs and openness to change, in 
order to offer the most expedient NIRAS sustain service.

Servitisation as a sustainable strategy 
In the delimitation of the scope of this project, it had been decided that the included 
case companies and the start-ups, for which the services would be relevant, should 
be product-oriented to some degree, and not only provide a ‘pure service’ (Tukker, 
2004). This was due to NIRAS’ experience within the assessments of physical systems 
and products. However, circular economy and servitisation are “buzzwords” in the 
industry right now, thus it would be relevant for NIRAS to consider including tools 
and methods with emphasis on circular economy and servitisation - see also Table 30 
in Chapter 10.
In continuation of this, the “PSS types” (Tukker, 2004) and “PSS strategies” (Kjær et. 
al, 2017) were selected as method cards, to enable NIRAS to introduce their customers 
to servitisation strategies and the potential environmental benefits. The case start-ups 
that have been involved throughout the thesis were used to provide an example of 
how those two method cards might be used, see Table 33.
The method card “PSS types” appeared in the toolbox search for tools and methods 
in Service 1: “Bæredygtighedspotentiale”, hence would only appear for start-ups who 
fit that service, according to Table 32, namely: Amino Wool, AA Water and CRR 
Denmark. However, the “PSS types” method could also have been interesting in the 
cases of FiveMoreMinutes and DropBucket, as seen in Table 33. This is an example 
that less complex products, like bedding or cardboard waste bins, might need a less 
advanced NIRAS sustain service than the one according to the development phase, 
they are actually in, and the other way around for complex products or technologies. 
Again underlining that the NIRAS sustain team should use the ‘open office session’ to 
assess the start-up’s needs and openness to change, in order to offer the most suitable 
NIRAS sustain service.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept evaluation
This chapter presents an evaluation of the final concept: NIRAS sustain, based on 
the requirements and client specific KPIs. The evaluation of the concept can thus be 
seen as NIRAS and case study specific. The requirements can be seen as “quantified” 
user insights - which were translated directly from the needs of the case start-ups. 
The KPIs were generated in collaboration with NIRAS - representing the successful 
solution, regarding implementation in the NIRAS organisation. 
The requirement evaluation is used to assess to what extent, the solution answers 
the needs of the case start-ups and the KPI evaluator is used to compare NIRAS 
sustain with the current NIRAS offerings. Finally the uniqueness of NIRAS sustain is 
presented and discussed via a value strategy canvas.
12.1 Requirements evaluation
The requirements used to evaluate the final concept were generated, based on the 
adoption of axiomatic design as described in chapter eight (Thompson, 2013). The 
requirements can be seen as function-based and quantified needs, translated directly 
from user needs - establishing the foundation of the product specification. The theory 
states that successful design, thus should meet all the requirements, fostered during 
the specification process. By meeting the requirements - the theory states that the 
final solution should include design parameters corresponding to the requirements, 
i.e. the final design should have a design-functions, that solves the requirement - hence 
indirectly the needs of the users. As such, the goal of this section is to evaluate the 
final concept with comparison to the requirements - e.i. determine whether the final 
solution has corresponding design parameters.
The requirement evaluation can be seen as a binary measure of the final solution 
- since binary refers to whether the solution fulfils the requirements or not. The eval-
uation of the solution, based on the requirements, can be seen in Figure 74 indicating 
met requirements in green, partially met requirements in black, not met requirements 
in red and discarded during development requirements in blue.
Besides indicating in colours whether the requirements are met, partially met, not 
eet or discarded, the design parameter - if existing - is stated. If a requirement is 
met - by the concept as a whole or by a specific design parameter - is is stated how 
the requirement is met, i.e. if a specific service element solves a requirement, it is 
stated. A service legend is showed in the following Table 34, indicating how each 
part of the service is defined as a design parameter. For example service 1, namely 
‘bæredygtighedspotentiale’ is defined as 1A, 1B and 1C, representing ‘grøn rådgivning’, 
‘bæredygtig livscycklus’ and øvurdering af bæredygtighedspotenetiale’, respectively.
In order to quantify the evaluation, a percentage calculation of the scoring - based 
on the amount of met, partially met, not met and discarded requirements - was made. 
The result of the percentage distribution of the total 46 requirements can be seen in 
the following Table 35
The percentile distribution show that around 72 % of the requirements were fulfilled 
by NIRAS sustain. 
Furthermore, around 17 % of the requirements were not met, around 4 % were 
partially met and around 6 % of the requirements were discarded during the devel-
opment of the final solution. In total this meant that 13 requirements were either 
partially met, not met or discarded during development. A short description of these 
requirements are presented in the following section, including the reason behind the 
verdict.

briefly presented.
 
+ R8.1: Provide “customised” offerings from NIRAS
This requirement was only partially solved, since the final solution does not offer 
individualised and customisable offerings. However, the solution - as a whole 
- was developed based on the individual needs of start-ups in 6 different devel-
opment phases, and should thus to some extent be customised.
 
+ R15.1: Assess and compare production options
This requirements has not been specifically met by the solution. However, it is 
argued that production - as a principle - can be defined as a part of the life cycle 
perspective, hence partly as a part of the services.

solution. They are briefly presented.
 
+ R13.1: Provide evaluation of rebound effects
During a testing round with NIRAS, the consultants indicated that an evaluation 
of ‘rebound effects’ was too complex for a start-up service, and thus did not see 
any purpose of offering.
 
+
R14.1: Use generic data to estimate sustainability
The use of generic data was deemed difficult by the consultants in NIRAS. The 
reason being that generic data can be difficult to collect in all circumstances, and 
thereby not a reliable data source in a standardised start-ups service.

+ R25.2: Ensure that the development cost of the solution correspond with the

There were 8 requirements that were not met by the final solution. They are briefly 
presented.
 
+ R6.1: Provide sustainability verification to apply for funding
It was not possible to include this offering in the services, since there would arise 
a conflict of interest - between the start-ups and NIRAS - if the start-ups were 
to pay NIRAS to assess their sustainability to apply for funding. 
 
+ R7.1: Provide sustainability calculations for benchmarking of alternatives
During testing and evaluation of the concept, this offering was characterised 
to be out of scope, since benchmarking require a very thorough assessment of 
the product and alternative product. Thus us was deemed unfit for a start-ups 
service, where clients in general have low data availability.
R10.2: Ensure that the start-ups can customise their offerings from predefined 
packages
This offering could not be included in the final solution, since it became clear 
during the development, that customised offering would increase the price - 
which was seen as one of the essential issues, by the case start-ups. Furthermore, 
predefined services - with no option of customising - allowed the team to stand-
ardise the offerings - hence lowering the price.
 
+ R17.1 Provide assessment of economic sustainability
This requirement was not met by the final solution as economic sustainability is 
not a core competency in the ‘eco footprint’ department.
 
+ R20.1: Provide inspirational “channel” about sustainability
Was deemed not necessary to include in the service, since an inspirational channel 
would require a lot of maintenance to run - thus increasing the non-profit sales 
activities, eventually increasing the price of the services.
 
+ R21.1: Provide the bigger picture: Motivation behind sustainability
Was deemed not necessary to include in the service, since an inspirational channel 
would require a lot of maintenance to run - thus increasing the non-profit sales 
activities, eventually increasing the price of the services.
 
+ R21.2: Provide educational “channel”
Was deemed not necessary to include in the service, since an inspirational channel 
would require a lot of maintenance to run - thus increasing the non-profit sales 
activities, eventually increasing the price of the services.
 
+ R24.1: Propose solutions for how to easily connect with the right internal 
competencies
As the project evolved, it was realised that NIRAS sustain most likely would 
be run by few people in the organisation - and the need for internal knowledge 
sharing decreased.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept evaluation
12.2 KPI evaluator
In chapter 8, eight KPIs were determined in collaboration with NIRAS, with the 
purpose of evaluating and screening concepts during the concept generation process. 
Furthermore the KPIs were used - as a guideline - to relatively assess the final solution 
compared to NIRAS’ current offerings. Thus, the KPIs were used to evaluate whether 
or not the final solution met the performance criteria, provided by the client. 
Generally, KPIs are seen as quantifiable measures used to assess a product or compa-
ny’s performance over time - and should thus be metric. Since this project has been 
about developing a service for NIRAS, without actually implementing the service, 
it has been difficult to assign metric values to the KPIs. From the perspective of 
NIRAS, the KPIs were delivered as guidelines emphasising what NIRAS characterise 
as a successful, start-up-oriented, solution - and thus the KPIs were used to evaluate 
the final concept NIRAS sustain, objectively, to assess whether the concepts could 
be deemed successful by NIRAS. As a results, the KPIs were used to evaluate the 
solution, based solely on the team’s ability to assess - to what extent - the KPIs have 
been met. Thus the resulting KPI evaluator can only be seen as an estimation of the 
concept quality - reflecting how well the final solution has achieved the stated goals 
and objectives, provided by the client in the design brief.
The KPI evaluator for NIRAS sustain and NIRAS’ current offerings can be seen in 
Figure 75, indicating NIRAS sustain in green and NIRAS’ current offerings in red. 
Each KPI is assessed on a scale from 0-5 - where 0 indicating that the KPI was not 
met at all, and 5 indicating that the KPI was fully met by the concept.
Figure 75 - KPI evaluator of NIRAS sustain & NIRAS should be seen as a visual 
representation of the relative comparison of NIRAS sustain and NIRAS’ current 
offerings.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept evaluation
The evaluator clearly show that the final concepts performs specifically better in the 
KPIs: ‘consider different start-up needs’, ‘reduce time spent per customer’, ‘increase 
awareness in start-up communities’, and ‘provide results in a visual manner’. This is 
perceived as successful and at the same time expected, since these were some of the 
core objectives from the design brief. It is presumed that start-ups - seeking consul-
tancy within environmental sustainability - will find that the service NIRAS sustain fits 
their needs to a higher degree then the current NIRAS offerings. 
Furthermore, it can be seen that the final concept performs worse - than NIRAS’ 
current offerings, in KPIs: ‘little time required to implement’, ‘low economic invest-
ment’ and ‘utilise existing KSAs’. This can be explained by three reasons as follows:
 
+ Insights from the empirical study and the literature review, highlighted the 
potential benefits of digitising the service - to ensure a coherent and transparent 
customer journey, allowing NIRAS to collect user data and establish a closer 
relationship with customers. Such an online platform is estimated to be quite the 
investment, compared to a “paper-based” consultancy service and is expected to 
have some maintenance costs as well. 
 
+ During several meetings with the client, NIRAS, is became clear that NIRAS has 
to invest time and effort in getting to know the new customers and most likely 
will have to acquire new KSA’s 
 
+ Furthermore the team realised that the final solution was intended to be applied, 
by a few employees only - hence including the entire organisation’s KSAs, would 
have overcomplicated the services.

 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept evaluation
12.2.1 What makes NIRAS sustain unique?
Compared to existing consultancy services - e.g. NIRAS’ current offerings - NIRAS 
sustain add a new value to the users: A high acquisition of new knowledge, skills and 
attitude. This is achieved via the method cards and the result templates - all presented 
in a visual manner. A value strategy canvas has been used to visualise the current 
sustainability consultancy landscape - with the purpose of mapping the values of 
NIRAS sustain compared to the competing offerings. As a result, the value strategy 
canvas is used to show the current state of the known market space, allowing users to 
see the main factors - that the industry competes on - highlighting how NIRAS sustain 
differentiate within these factors. The value strategy canvas can be seen in Figure 76.
It can be seen in the value strategy canvas that NIRAS sustain’s value curve diverges 
from its competing offering. This is according to Kim & Mauborgne (2005), important 
to avoid getting stuck in “the red ocean” - hence indicating that NIRAS sustain is 
perceived as value-adding and at the same time “innovative” - compared to competing 
offerings.
12.2.2 Conclusion
From the requirement concept evaluation it can be concluded that a majority of the 
requirements for the solution was successfully met with the developed service NIRAS 
sustain. There has been a successful transfer of customer needs to concept function-
alities. Likewise, the KPI evaluation showed that NIRAS sustain performs well on the 
start-up oriented KPIs. This supports that the service elements have been transpar-
ently derived directly from the user needs. Thus it is concluded that start-ups and small 
businesses, seeking expert assessments and advice within environmental sustainability, 
will recognise the new services as relevant and fitted to their needs. 
As the KPIs was used as non-metric measures - compared to how they are normally 
applied - they cannot be used to fully conclude if the solution is successful, but 
instead be used to assess if objectives and goals of the design brief has been met. In 
continuation of this, the KPIs has helped to ensure client involvement to guide design 
decisions and increase the chance of successful implementation. 
In overall, NIRAS sustain, has been successfully evaluated indicating that the concept 
- to a high degree - can be perceived as successful. This, though require that NIRAS 
invest time and resources during the pilot testing and implementation - ensuring for 
example that user feedback is gained and implemented in the service - before full scale 
launch of NIRAS sustain.
==PAGE:190==
174
 Work Package 3: Concept Development | Concept evaluation
The purpose of Work Package 3 was to develop a solution to the gap, identified in 
WP2, and based on a service blueprint of NIRAS’ current activities. Thus the aim of 
this work package is to provide the basis for answering the third research question:
RQ3: “How can approaches within ecodesign, PSS and LCA contribute to bridging 
the gap between sustainability-driven start-ups & small businesses and sustaina-
bility consultants?”
In Chapter 8, the problem and requirements were further specified, to ensure a solid 
foundation for the concept development. This included setting the requirements 
employing axiomatic design methods, clarifying the impact potential and co-devel-
oping KPIs with the client. The conclusion was that the concept to be developed 
should focus on reducing the complexity of and increasing the suitability of NIRAS’ 
offerings for start-ups in terms of price and visible benefits.   
The concept NIRAS sustain was developed in Chapter 9, following classic ideation 
methods like brainstorming and the morphological chart. The concept was tested and 
detailed through four iterative rounds with case start-ups, before being finalised and 
presented to NIRAS. During the testing rounds, the need for a toolbox to support the 
concept execution arose. 
In Chapter 10, a literature study was conducted to justify the purpose of the toolbox 
to supporting the developed services. This led to a collection of 117 method, tools 
and models, classified on five parameters to enable quick sorting and selection of 
methods, tools and models. As a result, the consultants can find inspiration in the 
toolbox on how to deliver the new, user-centred services, and they can make the 
process more transparent to the customers by discussing the results using method 
cards as a boundary object. The purpose of the method cards is to devise standardised 
result templates, to reduce the amount of time spent on reporting.
The final concept, NIRAS sustain, is presented in Chapter 11, as well all the service 
elements, customer touch points and a client guidebook.  
Finally, NIRAS sustain, was evaluated in Chapter 12, where is was concluded that 
around 72 % of the requirements -  that is quantified needs from the case start-ups 
- was met by the proposed solution. Further, the client specific KPIs was used to eval-
uate the solution, showing that NIRAS sustain performs relatively better than NIRAS’ 
current offerings - when targeted at start-ups. In overall it could be concluded by the 
team that, the needs of the start-ups had been successfully transferred into concept 
functionalities. Thus it is concluded that start-ups and small businesses, seeking expert 
assessments and advice within environmental sustainability, would recognise the new 
services as relevant and fitted to their needs.

 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Concept evaluation
In this final work package, we reflect on the developed concept, the use of methods 
and the research and design process. There are three key limitations to the outcome of 
thesis, which are related to the reliability of the conducted case studies and the applica-
bility of the developed Toolbox in the intended use context. This leads to suggestions 
for further work - both for the client and other design researchers - and ultimately to 
the final conclusion of the thesis.

 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Reflection
In this chapter, we reflect on the developed services, our choice of methods and the 
overall process. 
Throughout the chapter, we will reflect on the learning objectives presented in the 
beginning of the thesis. The impact model will be used to visualise the intended impact 
of the developed services, and the research success criteria from Chapter 4 to assess 
the actual impact. The reflection on our choice of methods will be steered by the five 
steps in d.school’s Designing Thinking, whereas the overall process is reflected upon 
from the perspective of the Design Research Methodology by Blessing & Chakrabarti 
(2009). 
13.1 Reflection on concept
The learning objectives, reflected on in this paragraph, are: 
6. Design training material to facilitate a transfer between specialists and 
non-specialists, focusing on the acquisition of  the right knowledge, skills and 
attitude towards sustainable product/service design
7. Consider the balance between the environmental performance and the 
economic viability of  a business model
The target users of the designed NIRAS sustain services are defined as “sustainabili-
ty-driven, product-oriented start-ups and innovative micro businesses”. These target 
users came from the delimitation of the scope due to the client’s, NIRAS, capabili-
ties and in general to narrow the scope of the project. It was decided from very in 
the project to utilise ecodesign, PSS and LCA approaches to facilitate a transfer 
between specialists and non-specialists, focusing on the acquisition of  the 
right knowledge, skills and attitude. To ease the application of these approaches,

==PAGE:195==
179
 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Reflection
that have often been created with product developers in mind, in a start-up context, it 
was attempted to pair the business development phases of a product-oriented start-up 
with the classic product development phases (Ulrich & Eppinger, 2015).  
This led to the hypothesis: “Product development phases and the business devel-
opment phases of a start-up that is product-oriented cohere with one another”. 
However, the hypothesis stands on a fragile foundation, and it should be verified if 
this comparison can indeed be made for product-oriented start-ups, to improve the 
applicability of the toolbox. The verification could be part of NIRAS launching the 
NIRAS sustain services, by testing whether the content of the Toolbox actually is 
suitable and useful when delivering the services.
In Chapter 4, we presented five factors for a successful project, of which three had 
measurable criteria. They are presented in Table 36. The “actual” impact model in 
Figure 77 is used to visualise that the non-measurable criteria have been met. 
 
+ To develop a feasible concept by considering NIRAS’ processes and compe-
tencies: This criteria is met, because the concept generation was clearly based on 
the identified gap factors and the analysis of NIRAS’ current processes. It was 
crucial for the developed concept that it is implementable within NIRAS, but at 
the same time it should also challenge their ways of doing. Furthermore, as seen 
in Figure 77: The guidebook; the wireframes for the digital platform including 
homepage, questionnaire, data templates, checklists, evaluation form; and the 
guidelines for IT developers, which all constitute the hand-over to the client, 
ensure that the designed services are easy to develop.
 
+ To develop a viable concept by considering considering the business case poten-
tial: The criteria was met by considering cash flow estimations
 
+ To develop a desirable concept by proposing a solution which is clearly linked 
to the identified needs of start-ups: This criteria is met by the traceable line from 
the obtained user needs to the content of the services, which help to ensure that 
the developed services are relevant and educational for the target group.
 
+ To reduce the complexity of NIRAS’ services for start-ups, compared to now: 
This criteria is met by the simplifying the processes with standardised services 
and templates. That the services are standardised also increases the desirability 
to start-ups, because it helps overcome the barriers of engaging with a consul-
tancy due to unforeseeable prices.
Regarding the measurable success criteria, the balance between the environmental 
performance and the economic viability of  a business model was considered 
throughout the project. This was achieved both by in general considering the scarce 
economic resources of start-ups as a decisive factors for the success of the designed 
service, and by considering the business case for NIRAS. The business case was 
considered by estimating the cash flow over a 10 year period, which showed that a 
break-even after 3.1 years. However, it was not possible to verify our estimations about 
internal NIRAS margins, and hence there is some uncertainty about the results. The 
measurable criteria ‘Number of new target customers’ is taken directly from the busi-
ness case assumptions; i.e. to reach the break-even of 3.1 years, there is a (minimum) 
required number of new target customers per year. 
The measurable criteria for success were not met, seeing that they cannot be meas-
ured until after the service has been launched. According to Blessing & Chakrabarti 
(2009), the measurable criteria should have been formulated so that they could be 
measured within the limits of the thesis. However, seeing that the designed services 
reach beyond the duration of the thesis, it was deemed more relevant and interesting 
to formulate long-term measures, that the client can use to measure the performance 
of the designed services.

13.2 Reflection on choice of methods
Empathize & Define
The learning objectives, reflected on in this paragraph, are: 
5. Systematically facilitate, document and evaluate fieldwork and co-creation 
and with stakeholders to obtain insights about their needs and translate those 
into design requirements
9. Reflect on the most important drivers and barriers for implementation of  
sustainability within start-ups via workshop sessions, in order to set up selec-
tion criteria to be used by the consultancy
The many interviews with NIRAS consultants; external specialists; and start-ups and 
the involvement of the client; the users; and ecodesign experts in the development of 
the services, ensured that we, throughout the project, have observed and involved 
stakeholders in co-creation sessions and semi-structured interviews. NIRAS 
contributed with determining the system, that the services should work in (as did 
external specialists); KPIs to guide design decisions; and continuous concept feedback. 
The users inspired the content and format of the services, and took great part in the 
prototyping and detailing of the service. Ecodesign experts helped in the evaluation 
of how to actually deliver the designed services. The useful outcome of especially 
the early interviews with NIRAS consultants was not clear-cut, but it helped us to get 
an understanding of the consultancy world, and was in general very interesting and 
educational for us - giving us new perspectives on the sustainability issues and oppor-
tunities for businesses.  The processes were systematised through documentation 
and coding, which could be turned into design requirements using axiomatic 
design.
One of the main focus areas of this thesis has been to develop consultancy services. 
Therefore, we could have focussed more on analysing NIRAS’ structure and activities 
deeply, to manage their complexity in a more well-argued and feasible manner. A less 
thorough approach was followed, by employing the service blueprint based on an 
underlying analysis of quotes from earlier projects and having it verified approved by 
NIRAS Environmental Footprint. In hindsight, we find that the followed approach 
was suitable, because, being a service provider, their set-up is less rigid than e.g. a 
manufacturing company, and it is more individualised based on the consultants, so an 
in-depth company analysis might have been redundant.
A systematic literature search was performed to determine the important drivers 
and barriers for implementation of  sustainability within start-ups, in combina-

 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Reflection
tion with the case study of six start-ups. The case sampling is questionable: Are the 
selected cases really a representative sample of the potential users: Product-oriented, 
sustainability-driven start-ups or innovative micro businesses? Maybe not really? There 
is a risk that the low number of case start-ups resulted in a low reliability of the 
obtained insights and needs, but due to the continuous involvement of the same case 
start-up, it is assesses the validity obtained insights and needs and the designed is high 
within the six case start-ups.
Another question to consider is, if a systematic literature search really was necessary? 
It was quite time consuming, and we obtained few novel and no surprising insights 
compared to the case study. However, the literature review did provide an under-
standing of the underlying causes of the obtained needs and insights, which enabled 
us to model the system in the reference model. Furthermore, most of the studies in 
the literature review were about SMEs, possibly due to bad search string formulation, 
and the quality of the obtained studies was not thoroughly evaluated and discussed, 
since the thesis team did not feel qualified nor able to - within the scope of the project 
- to assess the quality of the obtained literature.
A way to mitigate the possibly low reliability of the case study results could have 
been through a more quantitative study. It was chosen not to follow that approach, 
because of the main RQ3 and because conducting a comprehensive, quantitative 
study to obtain an exhaustive list of user needs would be overly thorough, seeing 
that customer trends and needs can change quickly, and the content of the services 
is relatively easily to update and change accordingly. The mitigating actions for the 
lack of reliability of the case study results were instead that the literature search was 
systematic; that axiomatic design methods were employed to ensure measurability; 
and that the line between case study-obtained needs and services content was clear 
and traceable.   
Ideate, Prototype & Test
The learning objective, reflected on in this paragraph, is:
 
6. Design training material to facilitate a transfer between specialists and 
non-specialists, focusing on the acquisition of  the right knowledge, skills and 
attitude towards sustainable product/service design
An interesting aspect to reflect on is our application of product development methods 
to a service development process. In the ideating steps, we found that the methods 
were equally applicable to service design, e.g. morphological chart and concept 
screening. However, in the prototyping and testing steps, we encountered challenges,

because the value of the services is ‘knowledge transfer’ and thus not physical. We 
found it hard to test that value - both within the time-frame of the project - but also 
because we are not the service providers and cannot provide the same value as the 
NIRAS consultants. As a result, we often ended up testing the physical evidence of 
the service rather than the value-adding elements, like the workshop of 1.5 hours or 
the application of the method cards. We tried to make up for it by designing a frame-
work enabling NIRAS to target start-up as customers in a profitable manner, and by 
providing thorough instructions for how to initiate pilot tests and launch the services 
full scale. The perfect scenario would have been to test the setup with NIRAS and a 
start-up customer. Hopefully, this is possible within a few weeks after the completion 
of this thesis. In hindsight, we could also have tested the value-adding elements in 
‘mock-up’-scenarios, playing out the roles simply to test the flow of activities: E.g. is 
it logical and feasible? 
    Throughout the prototyping and testing steps of the design process, we involved the 
potential users of the developed concept multiple times. We involved users even in the 
early prototyping phases to help us test the concepts and steer us in the right direction, 
rather than waiting until we had a finished “product”. This was possible, because we 
were designing a service. A disadvantage of the early user involvement is the potential 
lack of reliability of the case start-ups, so we cannot be sure that the developed service 
will fit a larger group of customers, until it has been tested by NIRAS. However, due 
to the nature of a service and the configurability of the toolbox, we strongly recom-
mend that NIRAS do not feel limited to the proposed services and their content.
The learning objectives, reflected on in this paragraph, are: 
3. Study and apply approaches, methods and tools within sustainability, ecode-
sign and product/service-systems
4. Demonstrate a fundamental understanding of  Life Cycle Assessment (LCA) 
in order to select elements of  the method to be utilized in a simplified manner 
for the analysis and management of  start-ups
8. Reflect on the relation between PSS theory, specifically servitization, and 
sustainability by determining the PSS competence of  the case start-ups
A general observation within these learning objectives is that rather than applying 
the ecodesign, PSS and LCA methods and approaches, we collected, studied and 
classified them in the Toolbox. Had we tested the value-adding elements of the 
service in ‘mock-up’-scenarios, we might have been able to apply the approaches.
Some reflections on the Toolbox:  The validity of the Toolbox in its intended use 
context may be questionable, because the tools and methods in it were created with 
product developers in mind. This was due to little academic literature on ecodesign 
approaches and start-ups/entrepreneurs, and should definitely be tested. The reliability 
of the Toolbox is assessed to be high, because the origin of the tools and methods, 
as well as the parameters and the classification, are very transparent, so the work can 
easily be continued or challenged by others. The reliability of the developed methods 
is relatively low, considering that the selection of them was solely based on our assess-
ments - it could have been improved by having experts or NIRAS themselves evaluate 
the resulting list of tools and methods for each service element and select the ones to 
be made into method cards. Also, in the production of the method card, we needed to 
make trade-off between communicating the method or tool in a simplified manner, 
without harming the integrity of it. 
In the delimitation of the scope of this project, it had been decided that the included 
case companies and the start-ups, for which the services would be relevant, should 
be product-oriented to some degree, and not only provide a ‘pure service’ (Tukker, 
2004). This was due to NIRAS’ experience within the assessments of physical systems 
and products. However, circular economy and servitisation are “buzzwords” in the 
==PAGE:201==
185
 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Reflection
industry right now, thus it would be relevant for NIRAS to consider including tools 
and methods with emphasis on circular economy and servitisation - we used the case 
start-ups to provide an example of how those two method cards might be used to 
connect servitization and sustainability.
The learnings objectives, reflected on in this paragraph, are: 
10. Evaluate our results in cooperation with consultancy and customer, and on 
this basis propose a plan for improvement and implementation 
To evaluate the developed services, we employed three types of evaluation: Require-
ments derived directly from user involvement via axiomatic design; KPIs established 
in collaboration with the client; and the research criteria above. The first two types 
of evaluation were in cooperation with consultancy and customer, and they also 
contributed to points of  improvements that NIRAS should consider.

academic and societal perspective and, on this basis, propose a variety of  
possible actions and systematically select the most appropriate one(s)
2. As a group, and individually, plan and execute a large project in which we 
reflect, develop and demonstrate our results effectively, continuously involving

Figure 80: A threefold evaluation process
==PAGE:202==

 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Reflection
The design research methodology by Blessing & Chakrabarti (2009) guided the 
planning and execution of this design research project, as well as introduced us to 
new tools: ARC diagram, Reference and Impact model, which helped us to delimit 
a complex, open issue, see it in a broader academic and societal perspective, 
namely sustainability consultancy of start-ups. 
DRM provided us with good means for visualising the system surrounding the 
services through the reference and impact models, which are similar to system 
dynamics modelling. The main difference from system dynamics modelling is there 
are not necessarily loops in reference and impact model to connect the success factors 
back to the key factors. 
In hindsight, the models may have limited the project’s scope, because the bounda-
ries of the system were determined by the identified factors - an inside-out modelling 
and not an outside-in modelling. However, looking the development arena in WP1, 
Figure 15, where no gap factors had yet been identified to limit the system bound-
aries, it can be seen that the only direct flow between start-ups and other actors or 
objects in the development arena are: “Funding”, “NIRAS” and “Existing solutions 
within sustainability counsel for start-ups” (the latter referring to free sustainability 
tools and approaches, developed with the purpose of guiding companies to become 
more sustainable). Even though a more outside-in approach would have been valuable 
and might have provided new insights about the relations between the factors in the 
system, it is assessed - from the development arena - that we did not limit the system 
boundaries of the developed service in a way that reduced the societal impact of the 
solution. This last point is emphasised by the fact that that the developed service 
actually includes collaborating with several other actors in the development arena: 
Funding (through service 4), Existing solutions (the toolbox and method cards), and 
start-up incubators /communities (presence in start-up communities). 
13.4 Conclusion
In conclusion, we found that there are three main limitations to the outcome of the 
project and the designed services:
1. The reliability of the case start-ups: With only six case start-ups, we cannot be 
sure that the designed services will be suitable for a larger, more heterogeneous 
target group.
2. The applicability of the toolbox in the context of start-ups is based on a hypoth-
esis stating that the business development phases of a product-oriented start-up 
is similar to the classical product development phases. This hypothesis has not 
been tested. 
3. A lack of distinction between product and service design resulted in testing 
and detailing focussing mostly on the physical evidence, rather than the actual 
value-creating elements of the developed services. Consequently, the services 
and the application of the toolbox have not been tested. By testing that in a 
systematic manner can - in case of a successful outcome - lead to that the two 
above limitations can be disregarded. If the outcome is less successful, it is likely 
that one of the two points above is the cause.  
==PAGE:203==
187
 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Conclusion and further work
This thesis was founded on the basis of NIRAS experiencing that their offerings did 
not match the needs of the start-ups seeking environmental sustainability consultancy. 
The project’s focus was defined:
Enhancing the environmental sustainability of start-ups and small businesses: 
User-centred development of consultancy services
The aim of the thesis was thus to explore the identified problem and develop as solu-
tion capable to solve it. The research methodology, DRM, was chosen as the research 
framework, outlining 4 main Work Packages, used to plan the project activities.
The first research question was focussed on determining the characteristics, needs, 
barriers to and drivers for sustainability of start-ups and small businesses - in Figure 
81 referred to as the ‘what’. 
The second research question was focussed on investigating the factors contributing to 
a ‘gap’ between sustainability-oriented start-ups and specialists - referred to in Figure 
81 the figure as the ‘how’.
The third research questions was focussed on - based on the insights from RQ1 and 
RQ2 - to explore how ecodesign, PSS and LCA approaches could be used to bridge 
the gap between startups and sustainability specialists. The following sections seeks 
to clarify and answer the research questions one by one, while determining the corre-
lation between the questions and how they have contributed to developing the final 
solution - hence bridging the gap.
Chapter 14: Conclusion and further work

 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Conclusion and further work
14.1 Bridging the gap: User-centred development of 
consultancy services, employing ecodesign, PSS 
and LCA to enhance environmental sustainability 
of start-ups and small businesses
The systematic review of relevant literature provided a substantial foundation, 
enabling a better and more in-depth understanding of the characteristics of, barriers to 
and drivers of start-ups and small businesses engaging in environmental sustainability 
practices. 
Characteristics of start-ups
 
+ Limited resources: Economic, people and time-wise
 
+ Short-term focus: Focus particularly on the day to day activities - lacking strategic 
planning.
 
+ Have a low degree of complexity
 
+ Risk-willing and adaptable
 
+ Owner has high influence on company activities: Want educational offerings
 
+ Wide range of responsibilities
Barriers to sustainability practices
 
+ Little awareness and knowledge of environmental problems, impacts and risks
 
+ Lack of holistic perspective on sustainability practices with short term planning
 
+ High level of uncertainty - lack of data and need of concept validation
 
+ Find it hard to prove sustainability, hence market a business on it
 
+ Large diversity of small businesses - the guidelines for sustainability practices are 
too generic
 
+ Insufficient access to adequate environmental information, tools and training
Drivers of sustainability practices
 
+ New market opportunities
 
+ Effective cost savings
 
+ Sustainability passion of owner
 
+ Green competitive advantages
 
+ Compliance with environmental legislations and regulations
 
+ More motivated employees
Based on the above characteristics about start-ups and factors contributing to the 
gap between sustainability consultants and start-ups, solutions to the gap were 
proposed, continuously involving users and stakeholders. The final concept, NIRAS 
sustain, consists of four predefined services shaped directly from the identified user 
need, aimed at standardising and automating processes, so that NIRAS can offer the 
start-ups services that are desirable to them, yet still feasible and viable for NIRAS. 
To support the implementation, the services and the new customer touch points have 
been thoroughly tested and detailed - the result is a deliverable, that NIRAS can take 
over and launch straight away.    
To make the services even more desirable to the users, an educational element was 
added in the shape of methods cards. The most common ecodesign, PSS and LCA 
approaches have been collected in a Toolbox, enabling an easier selection of relevant 
tools and methods. As a result, the consultants can find inspiration on how to deliver 
the new, user-centred services, and they can make the process more transparent to 
the customers by discussing the results using method cards as a boundary object. 
The method cards should be used to devise standardised result templates, to reduce 
the amount of time spent on reporting. Finally, the consultants can hand over the 
method cards to the start-ups, which will allow them to recall the learnings from the 
consultancy, as well as reapply the methods and tools themselves. This will create long 
term and educational value, which was often asked for and suggested in co-creation 
==PAGE:205==
189
 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Conclusion and further work
sessions with potential users. In combination with standardising the reporting process, 
the Toolbox and Method cards will reduce the complexity of and increase the visibility 
of NIRAS’ offerings to start-ups, ultimately leading to ‘start-ups as customers’ being a 
more viable business case for NIRAS. 
14.2 Recommendations for sustainability consultants
Initiate a pilot test 
… of the predefined services to verify or adjust the content of them, as well as the 
supporting framework and customer touch points. 
Set up KPIs for the pilot testing
… some suggestions are:
 
+ Profitability of start-ups as customers: To break-even after maximum 3 years
 
+ Number of new target customers: 7 new target customers in year 1 and 20-25 
new target customers/year after year 1
 
+ Satisfaction of new target customers: 8 or above on net promoter score (using 
the evaluation form)
Apply and customise the Toolbox
 
+ Explore the content of the Toolbox according to each service element, or make 
custom filters, to assess, which tools and methods, you find the most relevant and 
useful as method cards
 
+ Use the toolbox and method cards to hand-over knowledge and ways-of-doing 
from one consultant to another
 
+ Consider the application sector, specific needs and type of product, when 
selecting the appropriate tools in one of the predefined services
 
+ Add more tools methods - for instance related to servitisation and circular 
economy - and remove the ones, you find less useful 
Employ the “open office session” 
… to assess your customer’s needs. It has been emphasised that the predefined pack-
ages and the types of start-ups for whom, they have been suggested should always be 
assessed by the NIRAS sustain consultant
Ensure price transparency 
… to avoid intimidating the price sensitive start-ups 
Refine the services
… by considering customisation: The toolbox and methods cards could be used as 
configurators in the customer journey. 
Take advantage of the positive image, NIRAS sustain, can create
==PAGE:206==
190
 Work Package 4: Evaluation | Conclusion and further work
14.3 Implications and suggestions for further work
Sustainability practices and start-ups
The academic literature on ecodesign for start-ups and entrepreneurs is very limited, 
which made it hard to obtain any academically acknowledged methods and tools 
targeted at start-ups and entrepreneurs. Hopefully, the developed Toolbox and this 
thesis could contribute to the otherwise scarce amount of available literature in the 
area. The specific contribution is the combinations of the parameters “Radical or 
incremental change” and “Data required” to decide which product development 
phase, a tool or method is most suited for.
Characterising product-oriented start-ups
The model developed in Figure 33, Chapter 6, contributes with a new characterisation 
of product-oriented start-ups, which validity would be interesting to test. 
Educational setting
The developed services - especially the Toolbox and Method Cards can be utilised to 
transfer sustainability knowledge, skills and attitude from specialists to non-specialists. 
An example of this is could be in educational settings. 
Test and expand the Toolbox
 
+ It has been assumed that the toolbox is useful and suitable in a start-up context 
based on the hypothesis from Chapter 6: That product-oriented start-up devel-
opment phases correlate with the conventional product development phases 
(Ulrich & Eppinger, 2015), to which many of the tool and method creators 
refer, when discussing the tools or methods application. Thus, the validity of the 
Toolbox in its intended use context may be doubtful. It should be tested, e.g. by 
launching the NIRAS sustain concept developed in this project, and applying the 
methods and tools by either filtering the toolbox or using the results from the 
‘Exemplification of toolbox and method cards: NIRAS sustain’.
 
+ Add more tools and methods, e.g. related to servitisation and circular economy
 
+ Test the content of the developed services through a quantitative study or by 
including more heterogeneous case start-ups
